Page ii                                                                                               Rewards are Eternal
      Books by the Author
    1. The Race of Grace—introductory book in which performance-related themes (such as perseverance) are
        considered essential for rewards in heaven. Entrance into heaven, in contrast, is regarded as free. A strong
        defense of unconditional security derived from believers being given to Jesus is a particular strength of this book.
    2. Is Heaven a Gift or a Reward?—while some assert that heaven is a reward in some sense, this book maintains
        that regarding heaven itself as a gift is best.
    3. Breaking the Rocking Horse—introductory book regarding the distinction between having eternal life as a gift
        versus obtaining it as a reward. An overview of the outer darkness and an exegetical treatment of the parable of
        the soils are this book’s primary strengths.
    4. Mere Christianity and Moral Christianity—a more comprehensive discussion regarding perseverance (and other
        performance-related themes) as being needed for rewards in heaven rather than for entrance into heaven. This
        book provides both introductory material and advanced treatments.
    5. Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep—a rejection of the notion that profession of salvation is proven genuine by
        the way a person lives. This book focuses on the parable of the sheep and the goats and shows the inadequacy
        of conditional security in interpreting this parable.
    6. Sheep and Goats—an advanced discussion of the parable of the sheep and goats that evaluates four
        interpretations that are compatible with unconditional security.
    7. Sealed and Secure—a thorough defense of a popular argument for unconditional security that also defends the
        premise of unconditional promises.
    8. Fallen from Grace but Not from Perfection—an advanced defense of unconditional security which affirms that
        believers can fall from grace but not fall from perfection.
    9. Salvation—a study of how the verb save (sozo) and the noun salvation (soteria) are used in the NT. A
        comprehensive chart of NT usage is provided.
    10. Believe—an aspectual defense of unconditional security that examines how the verb believe is used in the Gospel
        of John. A comprehensive chart of metaphors for believe in the Gospel of John is provided also.
    11. Carnal Corinth—an affirmation of the categorical existence of carnal Christians within the Corinthian epistles.
    12. John in Living Color—a color-coded translation and reference manual of the Gospel of John that focuses on the
        grammatical and aspectual indications of unconditional security.
    13. Dad, can I be sure I am saved?—an intermediate discussion of assurance.
    14. Trichotomy—an advanced holistic dichotomous discussion of trichotomy with an integration of nikology.
    15. Ordo Salutis—a Free Grace order of salvation.
    16. Soteric and Mistholic Faith—an advanced discussion of the nature of faith.
    17. Karpology—a discussion of the soteriological implications of singular versus plural fruit.
    18. Predestination and the Open View—a discussion of predestination that focuses on free will and the book of life
        from an open view.
    19. Logic Tables and Diagrams—illustrations of logic with scriptural implications pertaining primarily to the
        soteriological and misthological arenas.
    20. Drawing and Appointment—an examination of Calvinistic claims regarding drawing and appointment as found
        principally in Jn 6:44 and Acts 13:48.
    21. The Outer Darkness—a magnum opus that ties together the author’s arguments for unconditional security and
        conditional rewards.


                See UnconditionalSecurity.org for the release status of these and other books by the author.

       Several people deserve thanks for having a hand in the production of this book. Jerry Jamerson and his church,
First Born Baptist Church of Weaverville, NC, hosted a Bible conference put on by Scott Crawford and myself at which
I presented much of this material in Power Point presentations that now have been uploaded to YouTube. They and those
who provided financial contributions made the conference and video presentations possible and are certainly deserving
of special thanks, as this was the occasion for my producing not only this book but two others as well. My friend and co-
lecturer Scott Crawford must be thanked, of course, for giving me the opportunity to co-host this Truth Topic Bible
conference with him, for his proofreading, and for all the assistance he rendered behind the scenes.
       My primary motivator to address this particular topic, the eternality of rewards, was Martha Lever. During the
past year she has persisted in asking me interesting questions on this topic as she read my book, The Outer Darkness.
Her persistency paid off, leading to my creating the supplemental discussion comprising the present book. Her delightful
children’s book, Morphie the Caterpillar, also served as the inspiration for a number of my illustrations at the conference,
and she and her publisher, Lewis Schoettle, graciously granted me permission to use one of her illustrations in my
material. She and her husband, Chauncey, made a long trip to attend the conference, for which I am appreciative.
       Pastor Jim Hollandsworth attended the conference and afterward requested that I provide some feedback regarding
Craig’s Dualism of Eternal Life. Although I mentioned Craig in Darkness, I did not provide sustained interaction with
his book therein. The follow-up, post-conference presentation, Truth Topic 10 Misthological Death, now on YouTube,
and two of the excursus subsequently added to the present book are directly attributable to Jim’s inquiry. A friend, Keith
Call, who made a long trip to attend the conference, had just recently suggested that I read Stearman’s book, Time
Travelers of the Bible. The Lord arranged the timing of Keith’s suggestion so that, in conjunction with the timing of Jim’s
inquiry, it inspired me to break up my model of misthological death into quadrants and also to provide an excursus on
time travel, making the discussion much more interesting and precise. To my mother, Gladys J. Cauley, I would like to
express thanks for proofreading this book. Her attendance at the conference, along with other family members, was
certainly a boost to morale.

                                                     Key Terms
       Marrowism is the present author’s coined term for describing his mediating non-Calvinistic Free Grace theology,
which is positioned between Arminianism (PEARS) and Calvinism (TULIP) and summarized herein by the author’s
acronym GRAPE. Historically, this movement was expressed in seminal form by the Marrow Controversy. In a number
of key areas, the present author may be considered a modern-day Marrowist. The present book is an explanation of
Marrowism as the present author has come to refine and define it. As used by the author, Marrowism refers to a defense
of unconditional security consisting of soteriological and misthological consistency. Marrowism is summarized by the
acronym GRAPE:
• Gift is Free—means that the gift of eternal life is offered as a free gift and must be passively received as such.
• Rewards are Earned—means that the distinction between unmerited gifts and merited rewards must not be blurred.
• Absolute Assurance—means that personal assurance of one’s salvation from eternal damnation is the essence of
    saving faith.
• Passive Persuasion—means that faith is a passive persuasion, not an active commitment.
• Eternal Security—means that the person who trusts in Christ alone for eternal life immediately and eternally
    thereafter has eternal life and cannot experience eternal damnation. This doctrine of eternal security is known
    popularly as OSAS (once saved always saved) and is consistently and emphatically affirmed by GRAPE, in contrast
    to the conditional denunciation this doctrine receives in TULIP and PEARS. The affirmation of eternal security (E)
    is placed strategically at the end of the GRAPE acronym in order to contrast the most distinctive aspect of
    Marrowistic theology from the other two acronyms. Unlike Arminianism and Calvinism, Marrowism emphatically
    does not condition salvation from hell on the believer’s perseverance. GRAPE affirms true eternal security, not some
    Arminian or Calvinistic imitation of security. Marrowistic eternal security is unconditional security.

       Misthologist: refers to an advocate of unconditional soteriological security who has a sufficient degree of
misthological competency so as to recognize that the biblical teaching regarding the outer darkness pertains to
misthological themes so that salvation from the outer darkness depicts conditional misthological security.
       Misthology is a coined term derived from the Greek words misthos (reward) and logos (teaching). It and its
derivatives will be used to refer to matters pertaining to the doctrine of rewards.
       Mistholic is an abbreviation for misthological (that has been shortened syllabically to match its counterpart soteric,
which, in turn, is the abbreviation for soteriological).
       Misthopology is a coined term derived from the Greek words misthos (reward) and city (polis) and refers to the
study of the reward city. It is principally concerned with the capital city, that is, the bridal city. This city is properly called
Heavenly Jerusalem during the millennium (when it hovers as a satellite city above the earth during the millennium) and
New Jerusalem (when it comes down to rest upon the New Earth during the eternal state).

       Soteriology is based on two biblical Greek words, soteria (salvation) and logos (teaching), and correspondingly
refers to biblical teaching about salvation. Although the biblical concept of salvation includes many dimensions other
than deliverance from hell, it is this aspect of salvation to which writers refer frequently when they use the word
soteriology. Thus, salvation from hell (or more specifically, from eternal damnation in the Lake of Fire) will be referred
to as soteriological salvation.

       Unconditional security is the conviction that once a person believes in Christ for eternal life, that person is
unconditionally secure in Christ and unconditionally assured of reaching heaven unconditionally. Technically, this
articulation is superior to saying that believers are unconditionally assured of reaching heaven. In Calvinism, God
unconditionally elects certain individuals to reach heaven through means of their perseverance. The elect not only will
persevere, they must persevere in order to reach heaven. Within Calvinism, reaching heaven is conditioned on the
perseverance of the elect. In contradistinction, unconditional security does not condition reaching heaven upon the
believer’s perseverance. Accordingly, unconditional election is not to be confused with unconditional security.
                                       Chapter 1. Plural Aion
                                                         Introduction
        Kingdom teachers are divided on the nature and duration of biblical rewards but united in insisting on the
importance of such rewards and that these rewards be clearly distinguished from the free gifts of salvation and eternal
life offered in Scripture. Our unity on these fundamental matters is far more important than our diversity regarding the
nature of rewards. Nevertheless, seeking to rightly divide the Scripture regarding the nature of rewards is a worthy goal
for any kingdom teacher. Defining one’s terms is important within any study, so from the onset it is important to clarify
just what is meant by a kingdom teacher.1
        As a token of our unity, the term Marrowist will be extended as an umbrella term for such teachers within the
present study.2 Broadly speaking, modern-day Marrowists will be presumed to be misthologists (i.e., experts in the
doctrine of rewards). Misthology is a coined term derived from the Greek words misthos (reward) and logos (teaching).
It and its derivatives will be used to refer to matters pertaining to the doctrine of rewards. Those holding to a strong view
of rewards in general and to a misthological view of the outer darkness in particular, while affirming unconditional
security, qualify to be called misthologists. In short, then, Marrowists are misthologists. They believe in unconditional
security (i.e., eternal security) and that the outer darkness, in contrast, pertains to conditional rewards. “But although our
security is eternal, are our rewards also eternal?” is the kingdom question to be examined in the present study.
                                 Illustration 1. Three Options Regarding Eternal Rewards

            (1) None               (2) Some                  (3) All
       No rewards are eternal Some rewards are eternal All rewards are eternal
        The basic three Marrowistic answers to that question are: (1) no rewards are eternal; (2) some rewards are eternal;
(3) all rewards are eternal. Some misthologists take the mediating position and pose that some negative rewards, such as
the outer darkness, must be temporary, possibly not even lasting throughout the millennial kingdom. This position is
highly inconsistent in that such writers tend to acknowledge that rewards in general, and even some negative rewards in
particular, are eternal. But if rewards as a whole are eternal, then why assume that some negative rewards are not eternal?
Other misthologists, taking the first option, allow that all negative rewards last throughout the millennial kingdom but
then, with a greater measure of seeming consistency, insist that all rewards cease at the conclusion of the millennial
kingdom. Other misthologists, such as myself, believe that all biblical rewards are fundamentally eternal. The endeavor
of this present study is to demonstrate, at the rudimentary level, why the third option is the most biblically consistent
model and thus worthy of being adopted in all kingdom circles.
                                        Primary Greek Words for Eternal Rewards
        As one might expect in a discussion over the nature of eternal rewards, stating one’s understanding of the standard
Greek words translated as eternal is obligatory. The NT Greek dictionaries define the adjective aionios as meaning
eternal. Its noun aion can be used to refer to this present age, the future millennial age, or eternity. Still, the latter meaning
is most common, and my contention is that it is the only part of its semantic range transmitted to its adjectival form—
aionios—within the NT. My fellow misthologists holding the opposing points of view will not agree with me on that
latter point. I will not press that matter in the present study. Nor do I need to in order to win my case.
        In Rev 22:5, aion cannot refer to the present age or the millennial age, for the time period being referred to is the
eternal state after the millennium. The verse thus confirms that in the eternal state: “They will reign forever and ever.”
Those Marrowists who deny that rewards are eternal will quickly point out a couple of things, however: (1) Aion is being
used in the plural in Rev 22:5. Naturally, if aion is used in the plural, it clearly refers to eternity. But if used in the
singular, whether it means eternity must be determined from its context. (2) They will acknowledge that rulership is
eternal in this context but then insist that at the end of the millennial kingdom, such rulership undergoes such a

fundamental change that it ceases to be a reward. Instead, it becomes a gift given to all kingdom inhabitants at that point.
      I will challenge their second rejoinder momentarily, in that the context of Rev 22:5 indicates that the rulership is
a reward restricted to the faithful. First, though, I will be content with their admission that the plural form of aion
necessarily refers to eternity. Although much more will be said in favor of my case that rewards are eternal, that singular
admission is all I need to win my case.
1Chron 28:9
        David warns, “As for you, my son Solomon, know the God of your father, and serve Him with a whole heart and
a willing mind; for the Lord searches all hearts, and understands every intent of the thoughts. If you seek Him, He will
let you find Him; but if you forsake Him, He will reject you forever [Heb. ad]” (1Chron 28:9). King Solomon will be
rejected permanently from being king in the millennial and eternal kingdom because he forsook the Lord. The Hebrew
word ad is used also in 1Chron 28:9, where David warns Solomon that if he forsakes the Lord, which he did, that the
Lord would reject him forever. (The Greek expression is eis telos). Solomon was not rejected as king during his lifetime.
Rather, he will be rejected as king in the eschatological future. But note that this rejection cannot be limited to the
millennial kingdom if it is to be forever. He will be in the eternal kingdom, but he will be excluded from the eternal
rulership entailed in Rev 22:5. Thus Rev 22:5 cannot be affirming that everyone in the eternal kingdom will be kingdom
rulers. Solomon will be one of those raised to experience the eternal misthological shame of being rejected as king.
Dan 12:1-3
      Daniel uses this same Hebrew word to describe the eternal misthological shame of unfaithful OT believers, which
would include Solomon:
         1
           Now at that time Michael, the great prince who stands guard over the sons of your people, will arise. And
         there will be a time of distress such as never occurred since there was a nation until that time; and at that
         time your people, everyone who is found written in the book, will be rescued. 2 And many of those who
         sleep in the dust of the ground will awake, some to eternal [Heb. olam; Gr. aionios] life, but others to
         disgrace and eternal [Heb. olam; Gr. aionios] shame. 3 Those who have insight will shine brightly like the
         brightness of the expanse of heaven. And those who instruct the many in righteousness will shine like the
         stars forever and ever [Heb. olam and ad; Gr. pl. aion]. (Dan 12:1-3; TM)

    After contrasting the misthological fate of unfaithful believers in Dan 12:2, in terms of everlasting life versus
everlasting shame, in the very next verse (Dan 12:3), Daniel immediately adds that the wise will shine brightly forever
and ever, illustrating the eternality of the misthological distinction. In Greek, this is a plural form of aion. In Hebrew,
forever and ever is a combination of forever (olam) and ever (ad). Even if one wishes to translate olam as age, one would
have a misthological distinction that lasts for an age plus eternity—thus forever. Wilkin correctly perceives this text as
describing a misthological judgment dealing exclusively with believers before the millennium:

               Note that “many” will awake. Why doesn’t it say “all”? The reason is that Daniel is thinking of the
         resurrection of the righteous before the Millennium. Unbelievers will be resurrected after the Millennium
         (Rev 20:13).
               Thus Daniel speaks of two types of resurrection that OT believers will experience. Some will be
         resurrected to “everlasting life,” which is a reference to abundant eternal life. We find the same concept in
         Matt 19:29, Gal 6:8, and Heb 11:3[5]. Whenever eternal life is spoken of as a possible future experience,
         the reward of abundant eternal life is in view. Even in the OT.
               But some believers will experience shame when resurrected. Of course this is a New Testament
         teaching as well: “Little children, abide in Him, that when He appears, we may have confidence and not be
         ashamed before Him at His coming” (1 John 2:28). See also Mark 8:38.
               Now admittedly Daniel does speak of “everlasting contempt” which is literally “everlasting
         abhorrence.” But this is parallel to shame. It simply means that forever this person will live with the
         consequences of their unwise life. “Those who are wise shall shine like the brightness of the
         firmament…forever and ever” (Dan 12:3). The implication is that those who are unwise shall not “shine”
         forever in their service for Christ.3

       In other words, Daniel says that many rather than all “of those who sleep” will be raised because he is not talking
about a general resurrection of all people (many ≠ all). Some of the dead (i.e., the unsaved dead) will not be raised until

3
    Robert N. Wilkin, “Regeneration in the OT?” GIF (March-April, 2004).
Chapter 1. Plural Aion                                                                                                 Page 3
the end of the millennium a thousand years later. Peterson acknowledges that many rather than all is the most common
meaning of this word and yet seeks to maintain the traditional interpretation by asserting that God will only raise some—
friends and foes—basically martyrs and those who martyr them.4 While Peterson’s affirmation that this is only a
resurrection of part rather than the whole is appreciated, it is unlikely that God will only raise part of the unbelievers at
this time. The resurrection of the lost is better understood as occurring at the end of the millennium. God will not raise
any of the lost before the millennium. There is no need to raise the lost before the millennium since they will not have
any entrance into the millennial kingdom. It is much more natural to suppose that the part (i.e., the many) God will raise
at this time described by Daniel will be those who are believers. All believers will need to be raised before the millennium
for their participation in the millennial kingdom. There is no need to raise unbelievers at this time. As already
demonstrated in the Johannine aside in Rev 20:4-5, the martyrs are raised at the beginning of the millennium, but “the
rest of the dead were not resurrected until after the millennium” (TM).
         Secondly, those raised in Dan 12:2 appear to be those who have their names written in the Book of Life in verse
1. Those Jews living at the end of the tribulation who have their name in the Book of Life will be rescued, while those
Jews who are dead at the end of the tribulation who have their name in the Book of Life will be raised. Thus, those Jews
who have their names written in the Book of Life are either rescued or raised at this time. This is a resurrection of only
those who, at the end of the tribulation, have their names in the Book of Life.
         Thirdly, those being raised are described as those who sleep. This imagery is certainly capable of describing dead
believers (cp. Is 26:19). And although the NT uses a different word for sleep in reference to NT believers of the church
age being asleep in Christ (in order to limit that reference to believers, cf. 1Thess 4:16), there seems to be no
insurmountable reason for not limiting those asleep in the ground to believers—in this particular context. The context,
rather than the phrase itself, supplies the limitation. In other words, the context would limit those who sleep in the ground
(with their names in the Book of Life who are resurrected at this time) to be understood as believers at the conclusion of
the tribulational period.
         Fourthly, the LXX word for shame (aischune) is the same word that Jesus uses concerning believers experiencing
shame at His judgment (Rev 3:18). And, as Wilkin points out, the verbal form (aischuno) is used of believers being
ashamed when the Lord returns in 1Jn 2:28, and a compound of it is used of their being put to shame in Mk 8:38. As for
disgrace (oneidismos), Paul says that believers may fall into disgrace (1Tim 3:7).
         Overall, no compelling reason exists to believe that the second part of Dan 12:2 is not describing believers. Instead,
significant eschatological and misthological grounds indicate that those resurrected to everlasting shame are believers.
Contextual considerations lean in favor of this conclusion as well. Therefore, concluding that the rewards being described
in this verse are for faithful and unfaithful believers is best. The negative rewards are just as permanent as the positive
rewards. The shame for the unfaithful believer will be just as eternal as the shine for the faithful believer. The shameful
believers will have a darkened misthological experience throughout eternity future.

        The premillennial judgment being described in Dan 12:1-3, in which only some are raised, is part of a complex
series of judgments taking place at the time of the Sheep and Goats Judgment described in Mt 25:31-46. The Danielic
aspect is limited to what happens to resurrected OT believers at this judgment, many of whom will be OT Jewish
believers. This judgment also will include the Jewish believers who are alive when the Lord returns at the end of the
tribulation. Hence, this Danielic judgment is predominantly a Jewish Bema, and it is appropriately termed as such in
contradistinction to the Gentile Bema (i.e., the Sheep and Goats Judgment) that occurs shortly thereafter. Daniel is
describing a premillennial resurrection of OT (predominantly Jewish) believers so that they can be judged to determine
what their status will be in the millennial kingdom. Whereas Matthew is dealing with all the [living] nations (Gentiles)
at this judgment (Mt 25:32), Daniel is focusing on the believers at this judgment, specifically Jewish believers. A Jewish
Bema is in view.
        Emphatically, Daniel does not limit the outcome of this Jewish Bema to the millennial age. Although this judgment
occurs before the millennial kingdom, the results of this judgment are not limited to the millennial kingdom. Daniel is
very clear, here as he is elsewhere, that the results are eternal. Even if one insists upon translating the Hebrew word olam
in Dan 12:3 as referring to the millennial age, then the addition of the second Hebrew word ad moves beyond this
millennial age to eternity, eternalizing the misthological benefit. According to this rendering of the Hebrew text, the
reward of shining brightly as a star lasts for the millennial age plus eternity—encapsulated by one Greek word, aion,
used in its plural form meaning forever.
Dan 7:18
      Pertaining to this discussion of aion as found in Rev 22:5, one would be remiss not to point out the Danielic
background for this passage as found in Dan 7:18.5

       Because the rulership (possession of the kingdom) referenced in this Danielic verse is exercised during the
millennial kingdom, it must be a reward limited to faithful believers. Yet those misthologists who deny that rewards are
eternal nevertheless admit that, when the Greek text uses aion in the plural, it necessarily means eternal, even regardless

reward. How could Daniel have made it any clearer than a triple forever? Any attempt to limit the reward to one aion is

comparison of the other texts using this forever-and-forever phrase will reveal that rewards are eternal in that the
forever-and-ever mercy given to the faithful in Ps 103:17 is just as from everlasting to everlasting as the forever-and-
ever human praise given to God in 1Chron 16:36; 29:10; Neh 9:5; Ps 106:48. The misthological mercy bestowed upon
faithful humanity is just as eternal as God’s praise coming from redeemed humanity.

of contextual considerations. And as can be seen above, both Dan 7:18 and Rev 22:5 use the plural form of aion.6
Therefore, on the basis of their own lexical admission, Dan 7:18 must be expressing an eternal dominion. Yet this
rulership is a reward that commences in the millennial kingdom! Because this rulership is eternal (according to the plural
aion), it cannot be limited to the millennial kingdom. Because it is a reward (according to the context), it cannot be a gift.
Thus this rulership is an eternal reward.
                                            Illustration 4. Dan 7:18 Syllogism
                          1. The rulership exercised in Dan 7:18 is eternal.
                          2. The rulership exercised in Dan 7:18 is a reward.
                          3. Therefore, the rulership exercised in Dan 7:18 is an eternal reward.

        The whole argument that rewards are not eternal is falsified by Dan 7:18. Many related conclusions by those who
deny the eternal nature of rewards fail as a result. For instance, some such age-limiting misthologists argue that the
parable of the sheep and goats cannot be dealing with eternal verities because that passage is dealing with inheriting the
kingdom, and inheriting the kingdom, supposedly, cannot be eternal because it is dealing with rewards, and rewards are
not eternal within their assessment. But if rewards are eternal, as Dan 7:18 proves, then their whole argument begins to
unravel. Accordingly, their argument that aionios in Mt 25:41 cannot mean eternal falters for the same reason.
        Furthermore, because they disallow the possibility of aion and aionios referring jointly to both the gift and reward
aspects at the same time, they cannot claim that (1) the rulership in Dan 7:18 is eternal but (2) the reward aspect of the
rulership is only age lasting. Rather, their dichotomy requires that the rulership modified by aion be one or the other: a
gift or a reward. Consequently, since the verse unquestionably is talking about possessing the kingdom/kingship as a
reward for plural ages, it must be describing the possibility of possessing the kingdom/kingship as a reward, not only
during the preliminary millennial age but also during the subsequent eternal state. Consequently, Rev 22:5 forms a
suitable conclusion for Dan 7:18.
                                     Illustration 5. Rev 22:5 is just Part of Dan 7:18




        Whereas Dan 7:18 focuses on the beginning of this eternal misthological reign, showing the start of this rulership
during the millennial kingdom, Rev 22:5 focuses on the continuation of this eternal misthological reign during the eternal
state. Some misthologists miss the point completely when they conclude erroneously that unfaithful believers not holding
positions of power and authority during the millennial kingdom will have a part in this rulership during eternity (Rev 21:4;
22:2,5). Not so! Far to the contrary, those Christians who were denied positions of authority in the millennial rulership
will not have a part in the eternal rulership! Rev 22:5 is the logical extension of Dan 7:18. The OT background found in

Daniel already has established that the misthological rulership entailed is an eternal reward that begins in the millennial
kingdom. As pictured in the illustration, the eternal rulership confirmed in Rev 22:5 is just a subset (⊂) of the
millennial-eternal rulership affirmed in Dan 7:18. The plural aion of Dan 7:18 governs both the nature and duration
of the reign. The nature of the reign is a reward, and its duration is forever. It is an eternal reward. This misthological
rulership cannot be confined to the millennial kingdom.
Ps 103:17
       Misthological forgiveness is an eternal prize to be won in Ps 103:17. Some believers, not all, will win that prize.
The picture of sins being forgiven as far as the east is from the west is not talking about a free forgiveness but an earned
forgiveness! The only way to acquire benefits such as these, either now, in the future millennial kingdom, and even into
eternity future, as David will show us, is to obey God fearfully. Contextually, Ps 103:12 is extolling the forgiveness given
to obedient believers because of their obedience. Their sins are forgiven as far as the east is from the west, to be sure,
but the passage is talking about how God deals with those who fear Him, which contextually is equated with keeping His
commandments. The misthological aspects are highlighted in gold accordingly:
         1
          Bless the Lord, O my soul, and all that is within me, bless His holy name. 2 Bless the Lord, O my soul,
         and forget none of His rewards [Heb. gemul; Gr. antapodosis]; 3 Who pardons all your iniquities; Who
         heals all your diseases; 4 Who redeems your life from the pit; Who rewards you with mercy and
         compassion; 5 Who satisfies your years with good things, so that your youth is renewed like the eagle. 6 The
         Lord performs righteous deeds and judgments for all who are oppressed. 7 He made known His ways to
         Moses, His acts to the sons of Israel. 8The Lord is compassionate and gracious, slow to anger and abounding
         in mercy. 9 He will not always judge us; nor will He keep His anger forever. 10 He has not dealt with us
         according to our sins, nor rewarded us according to our iniquities. 11 For as high as the heavens are
         above the earth, so great is His mercy toward those who fear Him. 12 As far as the east is from the
         west, so far has He removed our transgressions from us. 13 Just as a father has compassion on his
         children, so the Lord has compassion on those who fear Him. 14 For He Himself knows our frame; He
         is mindful that we are but dust. 15 As for man, his days are like grass; as a flower of the field, so he flourishes.
         16
            When the wind has passed over it, it is no more; and its place acknowledges it no longer. 17 But the mercy
         of the Lord is from everlasting to everlasting on those who fear Him, and His righteousness to children’s
         children, 18 to those who keep His covenant, and who remember His precepts to do them. (Ps 103:1-
         18; TM)

Note carefully that the benefits in this passage are for the children of God who fearfully obey Him:

•   So great is His mercy toward those who fear Him (Ps 103:11; TM).
•   So the Lord has compassion on those who fear Him (Ps 103:13).
•   The mercy of the Lord is from everlasting to everlasting on those who fear Him (Ps 103:17; TM).
•   To those who keep His covenant and remember His precepts to do them (Ps 103:18).

        His children who fear Him are those who obey Him and the ones to whom the benefits expressly and especially
apply. The child-Father relation is presumed, yet so is the obedience of the children: “Just as a father has compassion on
his children, so the Lord has compassion on those who fear Him” (Ps 103:13). The context is praising God for how He
deals with His obedient children. The obedience of all His children is not assumed. How God deals with His disobedient
children who do not reverence Him by keeping His commands is not the subject matter of this Psalm. Thus to assume
that all God’s children fearfully obey Him goes beyond the Psalm and against other texts that clearly assert otherwise.
        Consider the first two benefits listed: “Who pardons all your iniquities, who heals all your diseases” (Ps 103:3).
This passage does not deny that God sometimes heals the diseases of those who are not His children. God does not
necessarily withhold healing from the lost on all occasions. Nevertheless, His obedient children rightly look to Him more
confidently for healing. Suppose that when David had an affair with Bathsheba, God afflicted him with diseases, and
subsequently when David repented, God removed the diseases. David might well praise God as healing all diseases for
those who fearfully obey Him. Even then, however, one cannot regard this praise as an assurance that God always will
remove every disease when His children repent. That He does so on some occasions does not guarantee that He will do
so on all occasions. Similarly, the fact that God may heal all diseases without distinction in the present life does not mean
that He necessarily heals all diseases without exception. Ultimately, God will heal all diseases without exception for His
children. Even then, though, experiencing a super abundance of health may be a reward reserved for His obedient
children.
Chapter 1. Plural Aion                                                                                                Page 7
        While it might be said that all God’s children have all their sins forgiven permanently as far as the east is from
the west in terms of their relationship with God, only obedient children have their sins forgiven subsequently as far as
the east is from the west in terms of their fellowship with God. And the forgiveness in this context carries over to the
misthological strata. Only those believers who are obedient to the end of their lives will find their sins removed as far as
the east is from the west misthologically.
      In the context, the reason God “has not dealt with us according to our sins, nor rewarded us according to our
iniquities” (Ps 103:10) is because we fear Him, which in this context would be His children who obey Him. Not being
rewarded negatively with what one deserves because of one’s positive performance is itself a reward. Therefore, these
verses are describing misthological mercy (chesed, vv. 4,8,11,17). “For as high as the heavens are above the earth, so
great is His mercy toward those who fear Him” (Ps 103:11; TM). This Davidic concept of merited mercy has its roots
in earlier OT passages. According to Moses, God promises, “I will have mercy through a thousand generations on those
who have love for me and keep my laws” (both Ex 20:6 and Dt 5:10 in BBE; cp. Dt 7:9; Dan 9:4). Those believers who
lovingly keep God’s commandments will be shown the fullest expression of God’s mercy as a result. This great mercy
is described as being as high as the heavens are above the earth in Ps 103:11. In Ps 103:4, the soul (who contextually
fears the Lord) is described as being crowned (stephanoo) with mercy. Mercy is available as a crowning reward to God’s
children who fearfully obey Him. Indeed, stephanoo is translatable as reward. Thus, Ps 103:4 could be translated
appropriately as: “Who rewards you with mercy” (TM). Mercy is a reward in this context. Surely, God gives undeserved
mercy to the lost on occasion and even to His disobedient children. Nonetheless, the great, crowning, heaven-reaching
dimension of grace being described in this context is a superlative experience of God’s mercy bestowed on God’s
obedient children.
        English translations of this Psalm do their readers a great disservice by translating Ps 103:2 benignly as benefits:
“Bless the Lord, O my soul, and forget none of His benefits [gemul].” Not all of God’s benefits are unmerited. Almost
every time the word gemul is used in the OT, it refers to merited benefits, and this passage is certainly no exception.
Rationale for translating stephanoo in Ps 103:4 as reward has been provided already. A stephanos is a victor’s crown.
Hence if one wants to retain the typical translation, the appropriate gloss would be: “Who redeems your life from the pit;
Who rewards you with lovingkindness and compassion as your victor’s crown.” In Ps 103:10, not being rewarded
according to our sins is a reward in this context! It is conditioned on fearfully obeying Him and keeping His covenant.
Forgiveness according to works is being affirmed by David.
     If there is no fearful obedience, then there is no forgiveness, at least not of the type David is talking about, because
he is dealing with misthological forgiveness, as is proven by verse 17: “The mercy of the Lord is from everlasting to
everlasting on those who fear Him.” Just as Daniel affirms the eternal nature of this reward in Dan 12:1-3, so David’s
expression from everlasting to everlasting affirms the eternal misthological nature of this forgiveness, with all the benefits
it entails in this context. Like all the other rewards in this passage, misthological mercy is eternal. This point must be
stressed because the biblical writers are stressing it: Rewards are eternal. Indeed, Daniel uses this same double affirmation
of the eternal nature of the benefit and then even adds a third one: “The saints of the Most High shall receive the
kingdom, and possess the kingdom forever, even forever and ever” (Dan 7:18). How many times must David and
Daniel say that rewards are everlasting and forever before kingdom teachers universally will acknowledge that rewards
are eternal?
Rev 22:5
        The OT background demands that we regard the rulership in Rev 22:5 to be a reward. How about its own context?
Concerning rulership in Rev 22:5, John repeatedly makes it clear from within the context of Revelation itself that the
rulers are overcomers, not subcomers (e.g., Rev 2:10,26-27; 3:21; 20:4,6). Lang likewise states concerning Rev 22:5,
“Let each who aspires to reign with the Son of man in His kingdom walk now in His steps.”7 Lang correctly surmises
that both Rev 21:7 and 22:3-5 describe the blessings given to overcomers in the eternal state. That the expression, “His
servants,” in Rev 22:3-5 does not necessarily mean “all His servants” should be obvious.8
        In Rev 2:20, His servants are lead astray. Clearly, this does not mean that all His servants are lead astray. Not all
believers are subcomers. Likewise, when we read in Rev 11:18, that the time has come for God to reward His servants,
we should not automatically conclude that this means all His servants. Surely those servants who are lead astray are not
to be rewarded in a positive manner. “The saints” does not mean “all the saints” in Rev 11:18. Since the rewarded servants
in Rev 11:18 do not refer to all the servants, why jump to the conclusion that the rewarded servants in Rev 22:3-5 refer
to all the servants? Those who inherit, rule, and are blessed in the eternal state (Rev 21:7; 22:3-5,14) are those who
overcome and wash their robes.

 G. H. Lang, The Revelation of Jesus Christ (London: Oliphants, 1945; reprint, Schoettle Publishing Co., 2006), 379.

 See discussions of Rev 22:3 (and similar texts) in relation to the “assumed all” in Darkness. “His servants” should not

     As to the actual wording in the text for eternal, since the plural form of aion is used in Rev 22:5, this verse must be
talking about an eternal rulership: “They shall reign forever and ever.” So if this passage is referring to a reward limited
to faithful believers, then the whole argument against eternal rewards crumbles. Consequently, those rejecting the
misthological nature of the rulership contend that the rulership exerted in the eternal state is exercised by all the redeemed.
So in terms of rewards, after a 1000 years it will not matter any longer, in their opinion, if you lived for Christ during
your life on Earth.
     They assert that all the redeemed will rule eternally by insisting that anastasis does not refer to a bodily resurrection
in Rev 20:5-6 but to a standing up of the faithful believers out of the company of unfaithful believers in order to rule
and reign. They then assert that after the 1000 years, in Rev 22:5, the unfaithful believers get to stand up and rule.9 Thus
their argument looks at the passage something like this:
         4
          And I saw thrones, and they sat upon them, and judgment was given to them. And I saw the souls of those
         who had been beheaded because of the testimony of Jesus and because of the word of God, and those who
         had not worshiped the beast or his image, and had not received the mark upon their forehead and upon their
         hand; and they stood up to rule [never mind that the Greek word is zao not anastasis!!!] and ruled with
         Christ for a thousand years. 5The rest of the dead did not stand up to rule [never mind that the Greek word
         is zao not anastasis!!!] until the thousand years were completed. This is the first standing up to rule
         [anastasis]. 6Blessed and holy is the one who has a part in the first standing up to rule [anastasis]; the
         second death has no power over these, but they will be priests of God and of Christ and will rule with Him
         for a thousand years. (Rev 20:4-6; TM)

     Two entirely different Greek words are being used! The verb ezesan (from the root zao) is being used for live or
come to life in vv. 4-5! But if ezesan refers to a physical resurrection rather than a misthological resurrection, then their
whole argument collapses like a house of cards. Ezesan refers to a physical resurrection. For that matter, anastasis refers
to a physical resurrection within this passage, being synonyms with a physical resurrection. What John is saying is that
these faithful tribulational martyrs were resurrected and ruled with Christ for a thousand years and that the rest of the
dead were not resurrected until the thousand years were completed. For some misthologists to assert that the unfaithful
believers get to rule after the 1,000 years is to add words to the text. They have put words into John’s mouth that John
purposefully left out. John only says that the rest of the dead are resurrected after the 1,000 years. In contradistinction to
the faithful believers who are resurrected and ruled with Christ , the rest are only resurrected a thousand years later! To
express this important point with an inequality:

                                      resurrected and ruled with Christ ≠ resurrected

     To be resurrected and rule with Christ cannot be equated with simply being resurrected! John omits what they assert:
that the rest who are simply resurrected a 1,000 years later either rule or do so with Christ! By his omission John has
made it conspicuous that the rest do not rule with Christ. John anticipated that there would be those who would add to
his words, and he gave this warning: “If anyone adds to them, God will add to him the plagues which are written in this
book” (Rev 22:18). Some examples are dangerous to follow. Let us not follow the examples of those misthologists in
inserting or asserting that the rest rule with Christ when John has indicated that they only will be resurrected. Contrary
to some Marrowists, the rest, who are merely resurrected, do not rule with Christ!
     Thus, as a result of such a colossal misrepresentation of the rulership in Rev 20:4-6 of ch. 20, it is not surprising
when some misthologists reach ch. 22 and assert that the fruit of the tree of life (Rev 22:2) and the eternal rulership
(Rev 22:5) is for all the redeemed. After 1000 years, that is, after the Messianic Era, the tree of life and its attendant
rulership will no longer be a reward for overcomers, according to such misthologists, but given freely to all redeemed
humanity: overcomer and subcomer alike. Oh, yes, according to such misthologists overcomers will still rule, though the
nature of that rule will be changed, of course, so that the rulership will no longer be a reward. Rewards are no more. No
more tears, no more crying, no more death, no more rewards. After the millennial kingdom is over, rewards are over.
Rulership goes on, to be sure, but not as a reward for being faithful, only as a privilege for being a believer.
     Such misthologists are adding to the text with such sentiments. They say that all the redeemed will be able to eat of
the fruit of the tree of life in the eternal state, but the text only says that the leaves of the tree are for the healing of the
nations. I will only mention here that subcomers will have glorified bodies that do not need healing. Therefore, they are
not part of the nations. Subcomers will not have access to the leaves, much less to the fruit of the tree!

9
  Arlen L. Chitwood, The Time of the End: A Study About the Book of Revelation (Norman, OK: The Lamp Broadcast,
Inc., 2011), 400-403.
Chapter 1. Plural Aion                                                                                                    Page 9
     Such misthologists claim that all Christians will rule in eternity: carnal Christians, lukewarm Christians, apostate
Christians, and the like. The reign is eternal; the reward is not. They claim that distinctions between faithful and unfaithful
believers will be done away with in the eternal state. This is a misthological lie. First, the wiping away of tears is a
litotetical reward promised to the faithful. Second, the fact that some aspects of the overcomer’s rulership change does
not mean that the misthological nature of that rulership is done away with. Jesus continues to deserve His rulership, and
overcomers continue to deserve their co-rulership with Him. Third, to tell overcomers that their rulership changes at the
conclusion of the millennial kingdom so that whatever form it carries over into the eternal state is no longer a reward but
a free gift shared with non-overcoming Christians is to discourage the faithful and provide a lullaby for the unfaithful.
Sleep on, unfaithful Christian, you will get to rule with Christ for all eternity; those serving Him now will do no better
than you in the eternal state—unless, of course, such misthologists are wrong. And I have no reason to think that they are
right. Fourth, they say that Scripture is silent on the matter. Nonsense! Scripture is silent on the matter only for those who
have muzzled it and closed their ears to it. If rewards are eternal (and they are!), then Scripture is most emphatically not
silent on the matter. Immediately, after describing the tree of life in Rev 22:2 and the eternal rulership in Rev 22:5 during
the eternal state, John goes on to conclude:
       11“
          Let the one who does wrong, still do wrong; and let the one who is filthy, still be filthy; and let the one
       who is righteous, still practice righteousness; and let the one who is holy, still keep himself holy.” 12Behold,
       I am coming quickly, and My reward is with Me, to render to every man according to what he has
       done. 13I am the Alpha and the Omega, the first and the last, the beginning and the end.” 14Blessed are
       those who wash their robes, that they may have the right to the tree of life, and may enter by the
       gates into the city. 15Outside are the dogs and the sorcerers and the immoral persons and the murderers
       and the idolaters, and everyone who loves and practices lying. 16 “I, Jesus, have sent My angel to testify to
       you these things for the churches. I am the root and the offspring of David, the bright morning star.” 17And
       the Spirit and the bride say, “Come.” And let the one who hears say, “Come.” And let the one who is thirsty
       come; let the one who wishes take the water of life without cost. 18I testify to everyone who hears the words
       of the prophecy of this book: if anyone adds to them, God shall add to him the plagues which are
       written in this book; 19and if anyone takes away from the words of the book of this prophecy, God
       shall take away his part from the tree of life and from the holy city, which are written in this book.
       (Rev 22:11-19)

       Having done away with eternal rewards, within their own minds at least, some misthologists rather conveniently
stop their discussion on Revelation before they get to this closing passage. John, however, concludes Revelation, and
thus his discussion of the eternal state, with these promises and warnings regarding rewards. Both (1) the reward
according to your works and (2) the gift without cost are millennial and eternal in scope. The difference is that you can
drink the water of life freely even now. But the eating of the tree is not free and awaits a future realization or future
forfeiture, depending upon the believer’s faithfulness. Regardless, the results of both the realization and forfeiture are
equally eternal in scope. Context is king. Context has spoken. Context has placed this warning at the conclusion of the
book and of the eternal state to make the warning applicable to both the millennium and eternal state. For some
misthologists to take this concluding warning as skipping over the eternal state and only applying to the millennial
kingdom is bad enough. But to ignore it altogether is nothing less than taking away these words of Revelation from
consideration as to the eternal ramifications rewarding these promises and warnings as they are contextually placed as
apparently applying to the eternal state. This context is only silent to those who have muzzled it.

10
  Soteriology is based on two biblical Greek words, soteria (salvation) and logos (teaching), and correspondingly refers
to biblical teaching about salvation. Although the biblical concept of salvation includes many dimensions other than
deliverance from hell, it is this aspect of salvation to which writers refer frequently when they use the word soteriology.
Thus, salvation from hell (or more specifically, from eternal damnation in the Lake of Fire) will be referred to as
soteriological salvation.
Page 12                                                                                              Rewards are Eternal

        The first thing that we must note is that Rev 21:4 has a strong parallel in Rev 7:17. Both verses say that God will
wipe every tear away from their eyes. The context of both passages has been color coded and provided in the chart for
easy comparison. Those misthologists who deny that rewards are eternal will admit that the passage on the left pertains
to rewards but then insist that the same phrase in the passage on the right describes a gift. The alleged justification for
this claim actually backfires, however. Because these passages are covered in much detail in Darkness, only illustrating
the highlights will be attempted here.
Temple
        Intimate, permanent, misthological association with the temple, city, and Lord are promised and limited to
overcomers: “He who overcomes, I will make him a pillar in the temple of My God, and he will not go out from it
anymore; and I will write upon him the name of My God, and the name of the city of My God, the new Jerusalem, which
comes down out of heaven from My God, and My new name” (Rev 3:12). Obviously, overcomers are not made literal
pillars of the temple.11 Likewise, the temple itself is used metaphorically in this passage to symbolize the nature of this
eternal reward.12 No conflict needs to be imagined, therefore, with the fact that during the eternal state no literal temple
will be in the city because the Lord is the temple: “And I saw no temple in it, for the Lord God, the Almighty, and the
Lamb, are its temple” (Rev 21:22). The eternal regal association with the Lord granted exclusively to overcomers from
the beginning of the millennial kingdom continues through the eternal state.
Dillow
        The strong double negative ou me used in Rev 3:12 is a litotes,13 as Dillow correctly noted in Reign (p. 558), but
therein he incorrectly used it to support unconditional security. In Destiny (p. 959), he corrected this error by
emphasizing the eternal nature of this reward. They will never be disassociated misthologically with this temple and city
(ESV). They are granted permanence and prominence. Any attempt to grant this type of association with the temple or
city to all humanity must be rejected. For overcomers would have to be stripped of their permanent prominence granted
in Rev 3:12 in order for everyone to be made a pillar allegedly in Rev 22:5. Basically, if everyone is a pillar, then there
are no pillars. If some are not more prominent than others, then there is no such thing as prominence, and the promise of
Rev 3:12 is revoked. Consequently, this type of priestly ministry must be eternally conditional, just as one would expect
from other Scriptures addressing this matter:

•    “If indeed you will obey My voice and keep My covenant, then you will be a special treasure to Me above all
     people; for all the earth is Mine. And you will be a royal priesthood and a holy nation to Me” (Ex 19:5-6; TM).
•    “But you are (to be) a chosen race, a royal priesthood, a holy nation, God’s special treasure, so that you may proclaim
11
   The overcomer is given permanent misthological association with the Heavenly Temple and Heavenly City (Rev 3:12),
though on the basis of Rev 21:22 some would question whether there is a literal Heavenly Temple and thus argue that
perhaps Rev 7:15 refers to Ezekiel’s temple, the millennial temple, and that the references in Rev 11:19 and related
passages are symbolic. Serving day and night in the temple need not refer to millennial service in Ezekiel’s earthly
temple, however. Rather, they will not need rest as they minister continuously in the Heavenly City while the Earth
experiences day and night. There is an antithesis between type and antitype during that tribulation when the earthly temple
(the outer court) is desecrated by antichrist (Rev 11:1-2). The earthly temple may be considered the outer court of the
heavenly temple. In some passages the temple (naos) seems to refer simply to the Holy of Holies (e.g., 1Cor 6:19), which
would be the case in a number of passages in Revelation (3:12; 7:15; 21:22), though at times it might possibly include
the Holy Place also (Rev 11:19; 14:15,17; 15:5-8).
12
   This is a promise that the overcomers’ reward will be forevermore. It will be eternal and thus not be obliterated at the
end of the millennium. This conclusion is strongly supported by the next part of the verse, which says that the name
written on the overcomer will not only be the name of God but also “the name of the city of My God, the New Jerusalem,
which comes down out of heaven from My God, and My new name.” This promise to the overcomers concerning the
names written on them are associated with the New Jerusalem and the eternal age, not the Heavenly Jerusalem and the
millennium age. This eternal reward of having the name of God during the eternal state is promised to overcomers, not
subcomers.
13
   Litotes are a normal part of everyday speech. Webster defines a litotes as an “understatement for effect, in which
something is expressed by a negation of the contrary (Ex.: not a few regrets).” Thus, it is an understatement formed by
the negation of the contrary. The litotes not a few regrets is a negation formed by the addition of the word not to create
an understatement which means the exact opposite—many regrets. Some everyday examples of litotes would be:
“Michael Jordan was not a bad basketball player.” “That coat is not cheap.” “This is no small problem.” “That test was
no breeze.” “He is not a Scrooge.” Johannine literature contains at least 63 litotes.

        Dillow is correct to conclude from Rev 21:7 (and related passages) that this priestly ministry and God-son status
is an eternal reward limited to misthological overcomers.14 To inherit means to own. Only overcomers will have
“meritorious ownership over the New Jerusalem.” The distinction between inheriting and not inheriting that Dillow poses
is that all believers will inhabit (dwell in) the city but only overcomers will inherit (own) the city.15 In contradistinction,
I contend that unfaithful believers will neither inherit nor inhabit the Reward City, but that point of differentiation between
Dillow’s position and mine is not really germane to the present study and is pursued in more depth elsewhere in my
writings. As to the matter at hand, which is the eternal nature of rewards, Dillow and I agree that rewards are eternal.
Moreover, Dillow correctly understands that the passage in the left-hand column (in Illustration 6. Rev 7:19-17 & Rev
21:2-8) deals with rewards (Destiny, 668), as does the one in the right-hand column in that illustration. Yet he
inconsistently suggests that the wiping away of tears, even in the left hand column, might lend some support to what he
acknowledges to be his personal opinion, namely that the tears of all believers are removed “just prior to the second
coming” (p. 903)—even though he acknowledges that the wiping away of tears in the right-hand column does not occur
until the end of the millennial kingdom (p. 114) and that the wiping away of tears in both columns is within a context
dealing with rewards! A more consistent approach, therefore, would be to affirm that the wiping away of tears is a litotes
promising supreme joy only to overcomers as their reward.
Govett

                                                       Rev 3:12
         In his comments on Rev 3:12, Govett rightly concludes: “His reward once begun, shall receive no after-check or

conclusion.”16 And he rightly notes that the passage in the left column in Illustration 6. Rev 7:19-17 & Rev 21:2-8 is
dealing with rewards (p. 270). But he assumes that although the passage on the left pertains to rewarded believers, the
first part of the passage on the right pertains to “men in general, [rather] than of the inhabitants of the city” even though
the wiping away of tears in that passage “is mentioned especially at the close, in connection with the eternal city” (p.
281; emphasis his). But this rather odd assessment depends on how one interprets men in Rev 21:3 and overcomes in

the eternal state (for the most part). But he denies that it is dealing with rewards. According to Govett, the conditions
expressed by such passages bulleted above no longer apply (p. 335)! The risen saints will live in the city as kings while
those in physical bodies will live outside the city and be governed by them (pp. 338, 348). In one aspect this is superior
to Dillow, in that Dillow does not allow that there will be anyone in flesh and blood bodies in the eternal state, due to his
overly stringent understanding of no death in Rev 21:4 and his applying it universally. On the other hand, Dillow correctly
perceives that even if all glorified saints live in the city, only those saints who achieve co-glorification status will rule, in
harmony with his correctly taking Rev 21:7 as affirming a misthological inheritance during the eternal state.

                                                           Rev 21:4
        A large part of why Govett dispenses with rewards in the eternal state is that he appeals to the OT background,
such as Is 25:8, to universalize Rev 21:4.18 But what he fails to understand is that this OT background only universalizes
this promise litotetically to those who are qualified misthologically to dwell in Mount Zion, the Reward City. A brief
survey of that OT context from Isaiah follows.
        Those who dwell in Zion who will weep no more: “O people in Zion, inhabitant in Jerusalem, you will weep no
longer. He will surely be gracious to you at the sound of your cry; when He hears it, He will answer you” (Is 30:19). But
the promise is for city dwellers, not general kingdom inhabitants. Of all the kingdoms on Earth, some of which will
experience drought during the millennial kingdom, you must qualify to live in this particular part of the kingdom. Thus
these promises are contingent. Isaiah promises eternal joy, no sorrows, for those in Zion: “And the ransomed of the Lord
will return, and come with joyful shouting to Zion, with everlasting joy upon their heads. They will find gladness and
joy, and sorrow and sighing will flee away” (Is 35:10). “So the ransomed of the Lord will return, and come with joyful
shouting to Zion; and everlasting joy will be on their heads. They will obtain gladness and joy, and sorrow and sighing
will flee away” (Is 51:11). Contextually, Is 57:18 promises comfort for those who respond to His chastisement, not for
those who continue to walk in wickedness: “‘And a Redeemer will come to Zion, and to those who turn from transgression
in Jacob,’ declares the Lord” (59:20; cf. Is 57:20-21). This comfort is a reward. The promise is for those who take refuge
in the Lord and who thus will inherit the land and possess His holy mountain, that is, Mount Zion: “He who takes refuge
in Me shall inherit the land, and shall possess My holy mountain” (Is 57:13). Those who inherit the land will be the ones
who possess the city. Yet in the broad context, there is no difference between inhabiting Zion and inheriting Zion. The
city dwellers will be its rulers. Dillow poses a false dichotomy between being a city inhabitant and inheritor. Only those
who inherit the city will inhabit it.19 They will own it as their misthological inheritance.
        The Lord promises to revive those who are contrite and poor in spirit (i.e., those who have a contrite and lowly
spirit). Compare the misthological nature of this promise in Mt 5:4: “Blessed are those who mourn, for they shall be
comforted.” Such blessings are rewards: “Rejoice, and be glad, for your reward in heaven is great” (Mt 5:12). “The days
of your mourning will be finished. Then all your people will be righteous; they will possess the land forever” (Is 60:20-
21). There will be no mourning in the glorified Zion. Its inhabitants not only dwell in the land, they possess it. No more
mourning is a promise for those who possess the land. Inhabiting, inheriting, and possessing the land are misthological
concepts that correspondingly are limited to overcomers.
        Is 61:2 says that the Lord will “comfort all who mourn.” Does this mean that He will comfort everyone, even those
in hell? Hardly! The context rules out those who are confined to hell. It also rules out comfort for those who are confined
to the outer darkness, since the very next verse defines the comfort as “the oil of gladness” and limits the comfort to
“those who mourn in Zion” (Is 61:3; cp. Lk 6:25). Only those who inhabit Zion inherit these promises. Their comfort
consists of wearing a garland on their head as a “crown” (Is 61:3 NIV; cf. 51:10), being praised (Is 61:3), being called
priests (Is 61:6), having a double portion (Is 61:7). These things are eternal rewards limited to the faithful believers, not
gifts for all believers, as 61:8 goes on to explain: “For I, the Lord, love justice; I hate robbery and iniquity. In my

16
    Robert Govett, Govett on Revelation, 2 vols. (London: Bemrose and Sons, 1891; reprint, Schoettle Publishing
Company, 2010), 1:198.
17
   Govett, Revelation, 2:333.
18
   Ibid., 348-347.
19
   For fuller discussion, see Darkness, 554-561, 931-932.
Chapter 2. No Death in the Eternal State                                                                                 Page 15
faithfulness I will reward them” (NIV). This comforting removal of mourning is a reward, just as it is in Mt 5:4. Jeremiah
also says that the cessation of weeping is due to rewarded work: “Thus says the Lord, ‘Restrain your voice from weeping,
and your eyes from tears; for your work shall be rewarded’” (Jer 31:16). It is a reward experienced by those in Zion
(Jer 31:12-13).
        The litotetical aspect of removing the tears is seen in that it is equated with “everlasting joy” (Is 61:7). This blessing
is for those who will clothe themselves in practical righteousness like a bride (Is 61:10). Those who are righteous in this
passage are “those who are living by God’s standards.”20 Their righteousness will be seen by others (Is 62:1-2). Thus,
imparted practical righteousness rather than imputed legal righteousness is intended. They will have a “new name”
(Is 62:2; cf. 62:10). They will be God’s crown and royal diadem (Is 62:3); that is, they will be His rulers; they will rule
His kingdom; they will be an expression of His rulership. They will be God’s Bride (Is 62:5). All these items are rewards
and recompenses, not gifts (Is 62:11). These benefits are for those who dwell in the city, that is, in Zion (Is 62:12).
Accordingly, Is 65:19 does not rule out weeping in the new heavens and New Earth of Is 65:17. Rather, it rules out
weeping in the New Jerusalem of Is 65:19.
        Unfaithful believers will live in the new heavens and New Earth, but they will not dwell in Mount Zion during the
millennium or eternity. They will be excluded from the Heavenly Jerusalem (Mount Zion during the millennium) and
from the New Jerusalem (Mount Zion during eternity). They will be in the kingdom but outside the city. To summarize
from the background of the book of Isaiah, the banquet and wiping away of tears for all people in Mount Zion (in Is 25:6-
8) is for all people potentially, but it will not be for all people experientially. The wicked will be excluded, whether they
are lost or saved. In fact, the writer of Hebrews uses the background associated with Is 26:11 to warn believers that they
are in danger of God’s fiery wrath (Heb 10:27). In that day, this mountain city (Is 25:6,10; 26:1-5) will open its gates
only to the righteous, that is, to the “one who remains faithful” (Is 26:2). Believers who were not faithful will be outside
of the mountain city of Mount Zion, excluded by its gates. To say that those who are faithful will be rewarded with having
no tears is a litotes for having eternal joy, inheriting (i.e., possessing/owning) the land, possessing the mountain city,
wearing crowns, being praised, being called priests, having a double portion, having a new name, being rulers, and being
God’s Bride.

                                                        Rev 21:6
       Govett stumbles badly and unnecessarily when he reaches Rev 21:6 and limits the drinking the water of life without
cost to those believers who morally overcome: believers “who are victorious in their resistance against” the flesh.21
Without cost does not really mean without cost in his convoluted comments.

                                                            Rev 21:7
        Earlier in his comments on the first part of this passage, Govett had said, “The city is but one: it is the dwelling
place of all the risen from the dead” (p. 333). But when he reaches Rev 21:7, he says, “The former verses stated the
unconditional blessing enjoyed by the dwellers on the new earth. Now we have the condition of the citizens’ entrance
into the new city of God” (p. 358; emphasize his). Whoa, timeout! First, he has missed the import of the OT background.
According to the OT, these earlier verses in Revelation are just as eternally conditional as this verse, both dealing with
what is necessary to inherit, and thus inhabit, the city in eternity. Second, if entrance into the city is conditional, as Govett
correctly surmises here, then it is not the dwelling place of all the risen. The OT does not equate inheriting/inhabiting
the city with dwelling on the new earth. Govett correctly perceives this misthological inhabit-inherit relationship as it
pertains to the millennial kingdom, but he fumbles when he obliterates this relationship as it pertains to the eternal state,
postulating that the overcoming in v. 7 is used in two different aspects: “Probably there may be two aspects of
overcoming: [1] overcoming so as to be rewarded, then partaking in the millennial glory; and [2] overcoming as
compared with the total unbelief of the worldly...and the final entry into eternal life in the city of the risen” (p. 359).
He correctly understands that Rev 21:7 is dealing with the reward of partaking, but he wrongly assumes that overcoming
is also dealing with the gift of entry. He mixes gift with reward. Without any justification whatsoever he jumps out of
Revelation and cites 1Jn 5:4 in posing the second aspect for overcoming. But John clearly has defined overcomer
exclusively in terms of rewards in Revelation (2:7,11,17,26; 3:5,12,21). Govett knows this. He has abused the context of
Revelation by posing a non-reward aspect to what it means to be an overcomer in 21:7. What he should have concluded
is that millennial and eternal entry and partaking in the city, along with the other rewards promised in the context, are
limited to experiential overcomers. Verse seven should not be split between dealing with both gifts and rewards. It is
dealing exclusively with rewards.


20
     Martin, Isaiah, BKC, 1117.
21
     Govett, Revelation, vol. 2, 353.
Page 16                                                                                               Rewards are Eternal

Chitwood
       Chitwood correctly understands that the column on the left in Illustration 6. Rev 7:19-17 & Rev 21:2-8 is dealing
with rewards but then follows Govett’s lead in performing a split in the column on the right between millennial and
eternal applicability. In several ways, Chitwood is an improvement over Govett. Compared to Govett, Chitwood’s split
makes more sense. Chitwood takes the first part of the right-hand column as dealing with rewards (Rev 21:1-6a) but the
second half as reverting to the millennial kingdom (Rev 21:6b-8). He takes both Rev 21:7 and Rev 21:8 as misthological
(with v. 8 being understood as metaphorical).22
                                         Illustration 7. Rev 21:7-8 in Two Colors

            Rev 21:7                                            Rev 21:8

       For matters at hand, it does not matter how misthologists interpret Rev 21:8: Some take it as blue (e.g., Dillow,
Govett, Lang), some yellow (e.g., Chitwood), others, such as myself, see both colors overlapping to yield green. In other
words, I believe the verse is primarily soteriological (blue), but secondarily misthological (yellow). The misthological
association is correlative, so green does not confuse gift with reward. Blue and yellow are two separate strata.23
                                        Illustration 8. Rev 21:7-8 in Three Colors




        In any event, the important verse is Rev 21:7. Chitwood, Dillow, and I agree that v. 7 is misthological. Dillow and
I believe that it affirms rewards in the eternal state; Chitwood makes the reference to these things (tauta) in v. 7 jump
over the immediate context in vv. 1-6 to refer back to the millennial state. But this is not how John uses tauta, and it does
not fit the context because the promises in 21:1-4 concerning the city, bride, and no tears are reward concepts.
Nevertheless, Chitwood does have an interesting argument, more refined than Govett’s, in explaining why he, like Govett,
tries to make v. 7 jump back to the millennial kingdom. The backbone of Chitwood’s argument is that because Rev 21:4
rules out all forms of death within the eternal state, it must rule out eternal rewards as well.24 I will summarize his
argument syllogistically.
                       Illustration 9. A Syllogistic Summation of Chitwood’s Primary Argument
1. Major Premise: Rewards necessarily entail misthological death (Rom 8:13).
2. Minor Premise: All forms of death are removed for everyone in the kingdom during the eternal state (Rev 21:4).
3. Therefore, rewards must cease at the beginning of the eternal state.
Lang
        The major premise of Chitwood’s argument is sound. He is absolutely correct to affirm that the doctrine of rewards
necessarily entails misthological death for those who fail to obtain misthological life. My response will build upon the
brilliance of that observation. Our point of contention is that he limits misthological death to the millennial kingdom;
whereas, I insist that misthological death must be eternal.
