Chapter 2. No Death in the Eternal State                                                                             Page 17
        My approach was pioneered by Lang. He recognizes that Rev 21:1-8 is dealing with the eternal state and that the
rewards therein are eternal and limited to the faithful. He correctly acknowledges that while these rewards have their
commencement in the millennial kingdom, they continue as rewards limited to the faithful in eternal state. Moreover, he
brilliantly perceives that the Heavenly City is spread out as a bridal canopy as a dwelling for the bridal saints, thus only
for overcomers. He rightly understands Rev 21:1-8 as drawing conclusions regarding three groups of people:
       1. The lost are the ones cast literally into the Lake of Fire (Rev 21:8).
       2. The immature children are the saved who simply enter the kingdom by virtue of the new birth (Jn 3:3-5),
          having drunk the water freely (Rev 21:6) so that God will dwell among them in the sense of dwelling in the
          kingdom with them (Rev 21:3). God will dwell in the Heavenly city above the Earth, while they dwell on the
          Earth below, which, for them, will be misthological death in the outer darkness. Yes, Lang actually indicates
          (in harmony with my model) that these immature believers will experience misthological death on Earth in
          the outer darkness in the eternal state while soteriologically experiencing eternal life by virtue of being in the
          kingdom. They will have misthological death on Earth while having eternal life in the kingdom.
       3. The mature sons are the saved who inherit these things (Rev 21:7) so that as a result they shed no tears and
          feel no pain (Rev 21:4), dwell in the Heavenly City (Rev 21:2), compose the Bride (Rev 21:2), and rule
          throughout eternity (Rev 22:5). Yes, Lang actually indicates (in harmony with my model) that the wiping
          away of tears is one of the things inherited by the faithful.25

                                            Understanding Misthological Death
        Some of Lang’s conclusions he has made explicit, some implicit. My writings take his conclusions and work out
the logical implications of his arguments, making explicit what he has made implicit. Misthological death during the
eternal state is one of his implicit deductions. To understand misthological death, we need to understand it in the context
of soteriological and misthological life. The Gospel of John (GJ) has the most to say about all three, so we will just take
a brief trek through GJ pertaining to that issue.
                               Illustration 10. Misthological Death in the Context of Life




       Singularity of Life
        John begins by pointing out that eternal life is a free gift received simply by faith in Christ (Jn 3:16; 5:24; 6:47),
pictured by the blue level at the bottom in the charts. All the positive benefits bestowed freely upon believers in the
Scripture would be included in this basic level: eternal life as free gift, never perishing like those who do not have this
gift, a relationship with God as your Father, forgiveness of all sins (past, present, and future) in regard to salvation from
soteriological damnation, entrance into the kingdom, living in the kingdom, being a child and heir of God, receiving
glorified bodies, imputational righteousness, etc.



25
  G. H. Lang, Firstborn Sons, Their Rights & Risks: An Inquiry as to the Privileges and Perils of the Members of the
Church of God (London: Samuel Roberts Publishers, 1936; reprint, Miami Springs: Schoettle Publishing Co., 1984),
107-116, 122. Lang, Revelation, 363-381. (1) For the wiping away of tears in Rev 21:4 to be one of these things inherited
by the faithful, see Revelation, 379-380. (2) For the faithful dwelling in the Heavenly City as opposed to the unfaithful
dwelling on Earth, see Revelation, 364-365, 381. (3) For the unfaithful experiencing misthological death in the outer
darkness during the eternal state, see Revelation, 366. These latter two propositions deductively lead to an inescapable
conclusion. Lang believes that the Earth is the location of the outer darkness, which serves as a sphere of misthological
death for the unfaithful during the eternal state, despite their having eternal life in the kingdom. Since he believes that
the unfaithful are to live on Earth and that they are to live in a state of death during that time, because they administered
their talents poorly (and thus incur the outer darkness), Earth must be the location of the outer darkness and misthological
death in Lang’s assessment. Their soteriological life in the kingdom will be lived in a state of misthological death.
Page 18                                                                                                  Rewards are Eternal

       Duality of Life
        Along the way there are hints in GJ that eternal life may be experienced at two fundamental levels (e.g., Jn 8:31-
32). The differentiation between these levels is even explicitly named in Jn 10:10. Not only can we have eternal life, but
we also may have this eternal life abundantly over and above the basic free level. This additional level, termed abundant
life in GJ, is pictured in yellow. This costly yet rewarding aspect of eternal life encompasses all the positive benefits
bestowed as rewards upon believers in the Scripture: eternal life as a reward, fellowship with your Father, forgiveness of
all sins in regards to rewards, rulership in the kingdom, living in the Heavenly City, being a co-heir with Christ, receiving
co-gloried bodies with Christ, misthological righteousness, etc. The decisive element that separates conditional securitists
from unconditional securitists is the threshold that separates the gift aspect from the reward aspect of eternal life. On such
elementary charts this threshold may be referred to as a soteriological threshold since it separates soteriological life from
misthological life.

       Misthological Gap
       Yet because this additional misthological level is not had by all believers, a misthological gap must exist between
simply having eternal life as a gift and experiencing its fullest range as a reward. Those believers falling into this gap
would still have the blue soteriological aspect of eternal life, of course, which cannot be forfeited by the sheer nature of
the case. On the other hand, their misthological experience of eternal life would be dimensioned greatly and perhaps even
be negative in regard to the yellow dimension. Consequently, because this gap is colored by an overlap of blue and yellow
concerns, it is green on the charts. Note that a second threshold is now added to separate those experiencing this gap in
abundant life from those believers experiencing the fullness of abundant life. Because this threshold separates two
misthological possibilities from one another, it is a misthological threshold, which explains the color and second
threshold. Still, what should we call such a gap, which lies exclusively in this reward dimension of eternal life?

       Misthological Death
         A gap in life is best termed death. Those believers failing to experience life in this range cannot lack life at the
foundational level. Thus they do not lack soteriological life. Their death is exclusively in the misthological range, the
reward dimension. Hence this form of death is called appropriately misthological death. GJ interacts with this possibility
(Jn 12:25; 15:6). To use Jn 15:6 as an example, speaking of believers as branches, Jesus says, “If anyone does not abide
in Me, he is thrown away as a branch and dries up; and they gather them, and cast them into the fire and they are burned.”
Believers who fail to abide in Christ dry up, that is, they die in the present in terms of spiritual vitality. Paul describes the
same possibility: “For the mind set on the flesh is death, but the mind set on the Spirit is life and peace” (Rom 8:6).
Believers can either have an experience of spiritual death or spiritual life right now. Speaking of a believer, he says, “But
she who gives herself to wanton pleasure is dead even while she lives” (1Tim 5:6). The prodigal son was dead while he
was still alive (Lk 15:24). John talks about believers abiding experientially in death (1Jn 3:14). Regarding the entire
church of believers at Sardis, Jesus says, “You are dead” (Rev 3:1). This temporal experience of spiritual death is not the
focus of the present study, however. Thus it is not included on the charts. We are concerned about rewards in the hereafter,
and thus the experience of postmortem death in the misthological realm by believers. GJ describes this misthological
aspect also.
         In this very analogy, John proceeds to record Jesus as teaching that the end result of temporary spiritual death is
misthological death, pictured metaphorically in Jn 15:6 as being cast into the fire and burned. Not only is this death the
final outcome, it is the eternal outcome. Correlatively, just as unbelievers are cast into the literal fires of eternal
damnation, so unfaithful believers are pictured as being burned by judgment. The believers are pictured metaphorically
as branches, and the fire is metaphorical as well. However, the metaphorical fire experienced by them corresponds to the
literal fire experienced by unbelievers. The fire for unfaithful believers is not merely metaphorical; it is correlative.
Chitwood insists that any applicability of the second death to the unfaithful believer must be merely metaphorical, not
literal. Basically, I agree with his analysis, though I would express the relationship as being correlative. The same imagery
which is used literally of unbelievers is used metaphorically of unfaithful believers. Thus the shared imagery is not only
used metaphorically but correlatively. The same imagery is used dualistically to apply to both the unregenerate and
regenerate.

       Duality of Death and Life
      Just as death has a soteric-mistholic duality pertaining to unbelievers versus believers, so too life has a soteric-
mistholic duality regarding the unfaithful versus faithful. The applicable Principles of Stratification (POS) are germane.
Chapter 2. No Death in the Eternal State                                                                        Page 19
                       Illustration 11. The Principle of Stratification as Applied to Eternal Life
      1.   POS observes four primary strata in regard to eternal life.
      2.   POS provides logical sequence for the strata.
      3.   POS postulates that passing from a lower stratum into a higher stratum is normally irrevocable.26
      4.   POS proves that each stratum is eternal in its postmortem results.
      5.   POS implements the Principle of Duality (POD) to explain how some passages can appear in more than one
           stratum.

26
   The Principle of Stratification (POS) must be allowed some flexibility regarding irreversibility depending on the data
to which it is being applied. In the case of eternal life, believers can move temporally above and below the mistholic
threshold presently. Both presently and eternally, however, moving back across the soteric threshold is impossible. The
misthological threshold does not become impassible until its postmortem finalization in regard to eternal life. For
example, a believer experiencing eternal life abundantly today might not necessarily experience it abundantly tomorrow.
A believer’s experience of eternal life may fluxgate dynamically and dramatically before physical death, ranging from
death to life back to death again at the experiential level. Of course, increasing the misthological range to include the
temporal present is necessary to make this concession. Misthology proper is normally confined to the postmortem range,
though, and therefore impassible in regards to its proper thresholds.
27
   For treatment of most of these passages, see my other writings, primarily Darkness and Misthological Models.
Page 20                                                                                               Rewards are Eternal

     Those misthologists who think that believers can literally suffer in Gehenna or the Lake of Fire transgress the
Principles of Stratification and run roughshod over the soteriological threshold that, like an impassable chasm, separates
the unregenerate from the regenerate. The soteriological threshold on this more advanced chart is the major threshold,
located in the center of the chart. Each of the three thresholds in the chart separates life from death but the soteriological
threshold in the center threshold separates soteriological death from soteriological life. In a simple bi-threshold chart, the
soteric-mistholic threshold may be referred to as the soteric threshold in contradistinction to the mistholic threshold. In a
tri-threshold chart, however, the soteriological threshold is the one used exclusively in a soteric capacity, separating the
gift of eternal life from the absence of that gift. As unconditional securitists we must be absolutely certain that we do not
transgress that soteriological threshold. Nevertheless, some misthologists erroneously think that believers literally can
suffer the second death in Gehenna or the Lake of Fire. They make a major error. Jesus has given us eternal life with the
result that we will never perish literally in those flames, not even during the millennium (Jn 10:28-29). Plus our being
present with the Lord via the rapture is not conditioned on our moral performance (1Thess 5:9-10).28 Therefore, I am in
strong agreement with Chitwood that believers do not literally suffer in Gehenna or the Lake of Fire during the
millennium.
     Chitwood and I also agree that misthological life necessarily entails misthological death. Physical death is the loss
of physical life. Misthological death is the loss of misthological life. If misthological death is done away with, so is
misthological life; only soteriological life would remain in that case. For humanity, life necessitates death. Life does not
exist without its opposite—death.
     “But death is done away with!” will be the immediate rejoinder. My surrejoinder would be to point out that
soteriological death for the unregenerate portion of humanity is not obliterated. Death continues eternally. (Rev 21:4 is
followed by Rev 21:8.) The soteriological life of the believer will forever exist in juxtaposition to the soteriological death
of the unbeliever. On this matter, Chitwood and I would agree. Also, we agree that misthological life for faithful believers
necessitates a misthological juxtaposition with misthological death for unfaithful believers.
     Since the soteriological juxtaposition is eternal, one would presume that the misthological juxtaposition is eternal as
well. Yet Chitwood believes that the misthological death is only millennial, obliterated at the end of the millennial
kingdom by Rev 21:4, because all forms of death supposedly are ended at that point. I object, however, because the
evidence within Revelation indicates that Rev 21:4 is a litotetic promise limited to faithful believers, in harmony with
the OT background, concerning both the tears and the city. The holy city, New Jerusalem in Rev 21:2 is a Reward City.

       Shepherd
       The imagery of the faithful being led to the springs by their Shepherd in Rev 7:16-17 finds its background in
Is 49:9-10 where Israel, as God’s servant-sheep, contemplates her reward (Is 49:4) and is told that kings, princes,
princesses will bow to her and serve her (Is 49:7,23) and that she will wear those who oppressed her like jewels on a
bride (Is 49:18). Having the Lord shepherd us in this manner, with one’s pasture in the high places, is a reward. Thus
faithful believers will not need fear the valley of misthological death.29
       If one is paying attention to the misthological terrain of Psalms, then by the time one reaches Ps 23 (which comes
just before the misthological peak in Ps 24) one should conclude that David’s affirmation that he will dwell in the house
of the Lord forever is dealing with something far different than simply going to heaven when he dies. Heavenly Mount
Zion, God’s mountain home, is the lofty goal to which David aspires. Being shepherded on in the high places is the
reward to which he aspires. Dwelling in God’s house forever in Ps 23:6 is not a picture of unconditional security.
Obviously, as adjudged by the name of my ministry, Unconditional Security Ministries, and from my books defending
unconditional security, I am firm in my affirmation of unconditional security. Nevertheless, I refuse to rip Ps 23:6 out of
its context so as to make it pretext for my soteriological perspective. Every indication is that this text is misthological,
not soteriological. Who reaches this mountain home? Those believers who walk the paths of righteousness (Ps 23:3).30
This mountain path starts off in the valley, though. Through this valley one must walk if one is to reach this heavenly
mountain home. Walking through the temporal valley of the shadow of death foreshadows the misthological valley of
the shadow of death (Ps 23:4). If Psalms 1 is Davidic in its perspective, and it certainly is, standing as it does at the head
of the Davidic section of Psalms, then it is difficult to believe that David could not see the misthological dimension of
the shadow of death laying between him and that mountain home.

28
   See Destined for Salvation.
29
   Some of this material regarding David is extracted from my forthcoming book, Like a Tree Planted by the Water. See

   Progressive sanctification is described as the path, road, or way of righteousness in a variety of texts (Ps 23:3;

Chapter 2. No Death in the Eternal State                                                                          Page 21


                                         Illustration 13. The Shadow of Death




        David asks this question: “O Lord, who may abide in Thy tent? Who may dwell on Thy holy hill?” (Ps 15:1) The
Hebrew word for hill here is har. The same Hebrew word is used for mountain in Ps 2:6. Some translations render it as
Zion My holy hill, some as Zion My holy mountain. Regardless of the preference, the reference is the same. David is
comparing dwelling on Mount Zion to living in God’s house. But who gets to live there? David answers that question
immediately by providing a list of qualifications. The first verse of that list suffices for our purposes: “He who walks
with integrity, and works righteousness, and speaks truth in his heart” (Ps 15:2). Let’s not water this down by trying to
make it merely descriptive rather than prescriptive. Living in God’s house on Mount Zion is a reward conditioned on
your performing righteous works.
        The same theme is repeated in Psalms 24: “Who may ascend into the hill [mountain, har] of the Lord? And who
may stand in His holy place?” (Ps 24:3) Given the focus on abiding and dwelling on this holy mountain in Ps 15:1, Barnes
comments, regarding ascending and standing to it in Ps 24:3, that the questions are asking: “Who shall ascend there with
a view of abiding there? Who is worthy to dwell there?” In response to David’s question as to who can stand on this holy
mountain, Barnes refers back to the fact that the wicked cannot stand in the judgment in Ps 1:5. The thought is “Who is
worthy to stand before God? Who has the qualifications requisite to constitute the evidence of his friendship?” This
evidence is requisite and prescriptive, not merely descriptive. So what qualifications must you possess in order to dwell
there? David again provides a similar list of necessary quality traits: “He who has clean hands and a pure heart, who has
not lifted up his soul to falsehood, and has not sworn deceitfully. He shall receive a blessing from the Lord and
righteousness from the God of his salvation” (Ps 24:4-5). If you live righteously, then you will be rewarded with this
righteousness as your blessing. The NT imagery of being rewarded with a crown of righteousness would be fitting
(2Tim 4:8). After all, living on this mountain and co-ruling with Christ is reserved for royalty.
        Quite frankly, the whole notion that everyone in the kingdom will be able to live in this Heavenly City in eternity
is silly. The city is large, to be sure, but it is not that large! The human population will continue to expand infinitely.
There is absolutely no way that a population that expands throughout this universe and throughout other universes is
going to be able to dwell in this one city. Even common sense tells us that inhabiting the kingdom cannot be equated with
inhabiting the city. So how are we to understand David when he says that he will obtain this reward of dwelling in this
house forever in Ps 23:6? Can we not credit him with enough common sense to know that the reward will indeed be
limited to the faithful forever? David believes in eternal rewards. For that matter, Ezekiel confirms that David will be
king forever:

       And My servant David will be king over them, and they will all have one shepherd; and they will walk in
       My ordinances, and keep My statutes, and observe them. 25 And they shall live on the land that I gave to
Page 22                                                                                               Rewards are Eternal

       Jacob My servant, in which your fathers lived; and they will live on it, they, and their sons, and their sons'
       sons, forever; and David My servant shall be their prince forever [ sg. olam; sg. aion]. (Eze 37:24-25)
                                               Illustration 14. Kingdom Age




       Trying to reduce a singular olam or aion to the millennial age in such a case is ridiculous. The proposition that
David ceases to be king over them at the conclusion of the millennial age because supposedly in the eternal age they are
elevated to the same status that he has is preposterous. The regal distinction between him as their shepherd and them as
his sheep continues eternally. David is their king forever. This olam/aion will be the age in which David exercises his
eternal kingdom. This misthological kingdom-age is eternal. Likewise, the elevated status of Israel over the other nations
is maintained: “My dwelling place also will be with them; and I will be their God, and they will be My people. And the
nations will know that I am the Lord who sanctifies Israel, when My sanctuary is in their midst forever.” (Eze 37:27-28).
This is one of the OT passages that serves as a backdrop for Rev 21:3. In the eternal state, misthological sanctification
continues. Some people will be set apart for higher status and be shepherded in higher planes than others. The kingdom
age is not limited to the millennial age according to David and Ezekiel. The kingdom age is eternal. This misthological
kingdom-age commences in the millennial kingdom and continues throughout eternity.

       Reward City
        The New Jerusalem is a city of rest that unfaithful believers are prohibited from even entering at all (Num 14:23;
Ps 95:11; 132:13-14; Heb 3:11; 4:3,5). Since the city is a reward (Heb 11:14,16; 13:14), those who lose their right to this
reward completely lose their right to this city. Just as we would not picture a subcomer being allowed to borrow an
overcomer’s crown, we should not picture subcomers as being able to take subways into the city. When Jesus threatens
to take away the unfaithful believer’s portion in this city, we are left to conclude that this means that their participation
in this city will be revoked completely since they will not have access to the fruit and springs of this city (Rev 7:17; 19:7-
8). This bridal city is the reward for bridal saints (Rev 21:2,9-10; 22:2,12, 14,19), not a gift for all believers. It is the
festive city (Is 25:6-9) from which unfaithful believers will be expelled (Mt 8:11-12). Their expulsion is the parabolic
and telolic end of the misthological story. Likewise in Revelation, being with God in His tabernacle and temple is
associated with sharing His presence around His throne (Rev 7:15). Subcomers are not included in this specific aspect of
God’s dwelling among people (Rev 3:12). Unfaithful believers will dwell with God in His kingdom, not in the tabernacle
of His throne room.

       Bridal City
       The Bride (associated with the Reward City in Rev 21:2,9) was identified just a little earlier in Revelation as those
believers who morally prepare themselves for the wedding: “Let us rejoice and be glad and give the glory to Him, for the
marriage of the Lamb has come and His bride has prepared herself. Indeed, it was granted her to clothe herself in fine
Chapter 2. No Death in the Eternal State                                                                            Page 23
                                                                                                      31
linen, bright and clean, for the fine linen is the righteous works of the saints” (Rev 19:7-8; TM). Being under God’s
tabernacle, like a bridal canopy,32 and being a part of His bridal people is a reward limited to faithful believers.

         No Tears
        The initial promise of wiping away the tears in Rev 7:17 is promised only to faithful believers inheriting the
kingdom. To be clothed with white robes, one must actively do the washing for oneself. Just as the Bride must clothe
herself with white garments (Rev 19:8), so overcomers must wash their own clothing. The Lamb will not do the washing
for them. In fact, the only other time this phrase blood of the Lamb occurs, it is described as one of the components
necessary to be an overcomer (Rev 12:11). Being clothed in white garments is seen repeatedly as something one achieves
as a result of overcoming (Rev 3:4-5,18) and is associated with rulership (Rev 4:4). Consequently, the reason those in
7:13 are clothed in white robes is because they themselves have made their own garments white through (dia) the blood
of the Lamb in conjunction with (kai dia) their own sacrifice. The blood of the Lamb and the blood of their own sacrificed
lives are the dual instrumental means by which they overcome (Rev 12:11). They have done their own washing in 7:14,
in fulfilling the requirements necessary to be regarded an overcomer. And 12:11 specifies that this entails cleansing their
garments by the blood of the Lamb and by the blood of their own sacrifice.

         No Death
        Misthological Death during the Eternal State
        Absolute banishment of death and pain cannot be entailed for all humanity in Rev 21:4 because the wicked are
cast into the Lake of Fire just four verses later in Rev 21:8. This promise in 21:4 is composed of numerous litotes: no
tears, no death, no mourning, no crying, and no pain. No tears means supreme joy. No death means a superlative
experience of life, which is used repeatedly as a reward concept in Revelation. Like the preceding verse, verse 4 also
brings back the imagery of ch. 7, which made implicit promises to overcomers who wash their robes in practical
sanctification (Rev 7:14). In addition to the tabernacle experience around God’s throne mentioned above, overcomers are
also promised that their tears will be wiped away as a reward for washing their robes. Further, in this initial context, the
wiping away of tears is also understood as a litotes from a context of litotes (no hunger, no thirst, no hot sun—7:16).
Since the wiping away of tears was limited to overcomers previously in ch. 7, since it is associated with apparent litotes,
since it may be understood misthologically in the present context of ch. 21, since it is for the bridal inhabitants of the
city, and since it refers to more than just the removal of sorrow, the wiping away of tears and the removal of death should
also be taken as a litotes in 21:4, promising supreme misthological joy to its recipients.
        The absolute banishment of any type of death is limited to overcomers who inherit the promises just made in this
context (Rev 21:7). Because the banishment of misthological death is limited to overcomers, we must conclude that
misthological death will continue for subcomers throughout eternity. Chitwood made the exact opposite conclusion
demanded by the text. He was correct to insist that the promise of not being hurt by the second death made to faithful
believers during the millennial state in Rev 2:11 necessarily implies that subcomers will be hurt metaphorically by the
second death during that time. Following the same rationale, he should have concluded that, since overcomers are
promised that they will not be hurt by any form of death in the eternal state in Rev 21:4,7, subcomers will be hurt by the
second death during the eternal state. By misconstruing the misthological nature of the promise in Rev 21:4, he turned
the passage on its head.
        The promise of no more death is a litotetic promise of no misthological death (Rev 21:4). This promise is only
applicable to those who overcome (Rev 21:7). This means that those who do not overcome will experience misthological
death, specifically in the eternal state no less! Litotetically, Rev 21:4 is promising that no death (of any form) will exist
for the overcomer during the eternal state, while Rev 21:8 misthologically is warning that misthological death will
continue to exist correlatively for the subcomer. The no-death-of-any-form promise in Rev 21:4 is eternal in scope and
limited to those who overcome. Misthological death for subcomers continues throughout the eternal state because,
otherwise, the promise that overcomers would not be hurt by misthological death during the eternal state is meaningless.
        Not having this superlative misthological experience of life would be a form of misthological death for believers
who do not overcome. This deductive conclusion is absolutely certain. Logically, therefore, it must be deduced that
subcomers will be hurt by mistholic death. One does not have to reach this conclusion by throwing the litotes in reverse,
only by noting that the positive result (misthological life) is limited exclusively to overcomers. The very nature of this
particular litotes demands that the limiting of such life to overcomers implies misthological death for nonovercomers.

     See further discussion in “The Bride,” ch. 25 in Darkness; also “Reward city,” 604-606.

     See “The Wedding Canopy of Mount Zion,” Darkness, 931-932.

This deduction proves that this litotes must have inverse misthological applicability, within the misthological spectrum,
to those believers who do not overcome. John intends that this soteric-mistholic litotes be understood as having limited-
litotetic-reversal applicability to subcomers. The applicability is limited to the misthological spectrum, of course.
                                    Illustration 15. Limited Litotetic Reversion (LLR)




        By avoiding a full reversion that transgresses from the mistholic realm into the soteric realm, a perversion of the
gospel is avoided, and logical and scientific integrity is maintained. Promising no death to faithful believers because of
their faithfulness necessarily implies that unfaithful believers will experience death within the misthological spectrum.
Rev 21:4 also promises that faithful believers will no longer have any tears during the eternal state. I cannot invert that
promise so as to assume, at least with deductive certainty, that unfaithful believers will have tears in the eternal state.
Rather, the inversion regarding tears is limited to induction and, therefore, to probability. The conclusion regarding
misthological death, though, can be made with deductive certainty because, within the misthological realm, the absence
of life inevitably indicates the presence of death.
        My theory of limited litotetic reversal (LLR) postulates that in a soteric-mistholic-litotetic (S-M-L) promise, the
failure on the part of the believer to obtain the L value means that the believer will be assigned the M value instead.
Jointly, these acronyms combine to yield a LLR-SML (limited-litotetic-reversal—soteric-mistholic-litotetic) model. The
reversal is limited because one cannot perform a mistholic reversal on a soteric benefit. If you are a subcomer, you will
be hurt by the second death in a metaphorical manner that correlates to the manner described by the text.33 In a manner
similar to (but not identical with) the way unbelievers are cast into the Lake of Fire, unfaithful believers will be cast into
the outer darkness.
        First and foremost, John wants overcomers to be assured litotetically, and thus implicitly, that in not experiencing
either tears or death in the eternal state, they will have a superlative experience of joy and life in the eternal state.
Secondly, in limiting this promise to overcomers, John wants subcomers to be warned, with equal implicit clarity, that
they will experience both misthological tears and misthological death in the eternal state.

inverse drops below the misthological threshold that separates rulers from non-rulers, but it does not drop into a neutral
zone that separates the S and L zones. The M zone is decidedly not neural in regards to its misthological implications.
Misthologically speaking, the M zone is definitely negative. Faithful believers get a superlative experience of
misthological life. Unfaithful believers experience a form of misthological death that cannot be equated with
soteriological death.

       Physical Death during the Eternal State
       The conclusion that all forms of death are obliterated only for the overcomers is necessitated not only by the
context of Rev 21:4 and its OT background, but by logic itself. Human procreation will continue throughout eternity.
Therefore, death at the physical level must continue also. Chitwood shares a common misunderstanding: “There can be
no such thing as Christians being hurt of the second death beyond the Millennium, for Rev. 21:4 plainly declares that
there will be no more death during this time.”35 Aside from the fact that the verse is only promising no more death of any
type for overcomers, let us consider the reasonability of some types of physical death occurring during the eternal state.
                                         Illustration 17. What about the Rabbits?




       OEC (Old-Earth Creationists) argue that the food chain existed prior to the fall. For example, Adam’s rulership
over the fish presumably would include his ability to eat them, leading to the affirmation of a food chain. Fishing still
will take place during the millennial kingdom and/or eternal state (Eze 47:9-10), the same time period in which the wolf
lives peaceable with the lamb and lion eats straw (Is 11:6-8; Is 65:25). Even though the whole Earth “will be full of the
knowledge of God,” this no-hurt-state-of-affairs is explicitly said to be in God’s “holy mountain.” Might death be allowed

34
   In reality, the lower threshold is a soteric-mistholic threshold, but for simplicity sake it may be referred to simply as a

   Chitwood, End, 484.
Page 26                                                                                               Rewards are Eternal

offsite, that is, outside the perimeter of that holy mountain as it was outside the Garden of Eden?
        Possibly, one reason God created plants and animals was so that humanity could eat them. Though Gen 9:3 is
post-flood, 1Tim 4:1-5 reaches back to creation with the confirmation that eating meat is good. When my brother and I
were young, we used to squirrel hunt in the woods around our house, but we had a rule: Do not kill the squirrels right
around our house. We liked having those cute squirrels running safely around our yard. Perhaps the holy mountain will
have similar restrictions, just as the Garden of Eden presumably did before the fall. Such a simple proposal would explain
how the rabbits, as well as other animals, would not overrun the Earth.
        We know that death entered the world through sin (Rom 5:12). As a YEC (Young-Earth Creationist), I understand
this to mean that there was no death, in this biblical sense, before Adam and Eve sinned. But does this mean that there
was no death in any sense before Adam and Eve sinned. Adam and Eve certainly could suffer physical wounds involving
the deterioration and death of flesh or bone cells which must then be replaced. Animals could experience scratches or
scrapes too. Dill defends RET (Ruined-Earth Theory, aka the Gap Theory) against YEC (Young-Earth Creationism) by
explaining that death must have been in place before the fall:

       Over the years, I have seen Young-Earth creationists repeatedly try to destroy the Gap Theory by using a
       particular “biblical bombshell” that “proves” they are right. Let me defuse this “bomb.” Young-Earth
       creationists insist that the Restoration Theory is wrong because it means death reigned before Adam. Gap
       Theory creationists look at the geological strata and say that all those fossils (dead things) were formed
       before Adam was created. Young-Earth creationists become indignant when they hear this. They believe
       there was no death before Adam sinned. “By one man sin entered into the world, and death by sin,” they’ll
       quote. According to them, Romans 5:12 and 1 Corinthians 15:21 prove there was no death before Adam
       sinned. If you question them, however, they usually admit that some kind of death must have existed before
       Adam's fall. Plants died when eaten. Cows didn’t carry microscopes to pick off the microscopic mites living
       on the blades of grass before they ate. These mites would have been killed by the cow’s digestion system.
       Insects, nematodes, and other soil-living creatures got squished as great herds of buffalo crossed the plains.
       Plankton-feeding whales would have digested microscopic animals along with microscopic plants. Once
       something is digested, it’s pretty good and dead. The outer-most layer of epithelial tissue (skin) consists of
       cells that are dead. If those skin cells were alive, we would be in excruciating pain every time something
       touched us, and worse than that, we would dehydrate within hours. The layer of dead epithelial cells acts
       as a fluid barrier to prevent desiccation. If those cells weren’t dead, we soon would be! Furthermore, those
       dead cells are constantly being rubbed off and replaced by the growing cells beneath them. Epithelial cells
       aren’t the only cells continually dying in order for animals to live. Red blood cells have a constant turnover
       rate. White blood cells do the same. Osteoclasts are cells that continually kill bone cells so that new bone
       cells can be made by osteoblasts. If this didn’t happen, bones couldn’t grow. Baby animals couldn’t become
       adult animals if living cells didn’t die. If you study embryological development, you’ll discover that certain
       embryonic cells have to die in order for further development to take place. Without death, animals couldn’t
       have multiplied according to God’s command. There had to be death of some kind, even in animals.36

       Regardless of one’s preference for OEC or YEC, Dill’s defense of the geological Gap Theory provides a feasible
defense for my misthological Gap Theory. Forms of death must have been occurring in the Garden of Eden even before
the fall. Informed creationists of both camps concur on this matter. This deduction by creationists causes no problem
with the biblical affirmation that death did not occur until after the fall because the Bible intends for us to interpret this
to mean that human death (i.e., the death of human beings) did not occur until after the fall. It is not denying the death of
human skin and bone cells. Likewise, the proper deduction by misthologists, that death of various forms will continue in
the eternal state, causes no problem for the biblical affirmation that death does not continue in the eternal state because
the Bible intents for us to interpret this to mean that physical human death (i.e., the death of human beings) will not occur
during the eternal state. The death of human skin and bone cells in the eternal state is not denied; rather, it is presumed
for those in physical bodies, needing the leaves for physical healing.




36
   Steven E. Dill, In The Beginnings: The Story of the Original Earth, its Destruction, and its Reconstruction: A Defense

                                       Illustration 18. Eternal ⊂ Millennial-Eternal




        As for Chitwood’s allegation that the promise of no death in Rev 21:4 proves that rewards will not exist in the
eternal state, his argument actually backfires. In his opinion, no misthological death continues at that point, so rewards
cease. Counter wise, as demonstrated herein, the context of Rev 21:4 limits this no-death promise to overcomers.
Therefore, in accord with Chitwood’s own rationale, misthological death, and hence rewards, must extend into the eternal
state. Consequently, since the proper interpretation of Rev 21:4 in conjunction with Rev 21:7 necessarily concludes that
rewards are eternal, we must deduce, in contradistinction to Chitwood, that when aionios is used in relation to rewards,
it must mean eternal, not merely age lasting. Overcomers, and only overcomers, will rule with Christ eternally. The
removal of tears by the joys of rulership will be limited to overcomers.
                                                       Conclusion
       Perhaps some misthologists who insist that rewards are only age lasting will pose that those who teach that rewards
are eternal are adding to the word and thus are susceptible to the warning in Rev 22:18-19:
         18
           I testify to everyone who hears the words of the prophecy of this book: if anyone adds to them, then God
         will add to him the plagues which are written in this book; 19 and if anyone takes away from the words of
         the prophecy of this book, then God will take away his portion from the tree of life and from the holy city,
         which are written in this book. (TM)
                                              Illustration 19. Pascal’s Wager

                 Age-Long Loss                                                   Eternal Loss


       I would counter any such wager with Pascal’s Wager. Let us suppose that those adopting the age-lasting limitation
are right and I am wrong and that this warning in Revelation is applicable to be mistaken in this area. Then I will lose out
on rewards for a thousand years, which in terms of eternity is nothing more than a day (2Pet 3:8). My age-long loss for
adding an eternal dimension to the text is negligible in that case. Realistically, their threat would amount to nothing in
terms of eternity because they have made rewards count for nothing in terms of eternity. On the other hand, if I am right
and they are wrong, then their loss for subtracting the eternal dimension from the text is infinite in terms of eternity. They
could suffer misthological death for eternity. The stakes are high; my gamble, if it be called that, is not, for I am playing
with a stacked deck. I have the preceding context, text, proceeding context, OT background, and logic in my favor. The
only face card they might hope to have in their hand is the assumption that no forms of death exist in the eternal state, an
assumption that is demonstrably false. In terms of the evidence and outcome, then, I am taking no gamble. Even the face
card they hope to use is actually in my hand, not theirs. Some form of death must continue in the eternal state, if for no
other reason than human procreation continues. As proven in Darkness, the Bible promises eternal generations. Those
who live to the end of the millennial kingdom must be able to enter the eternal kingdom in flesh and blood bodies so that
the divine intent and promises of humans inhabiting the galaxies will be realized.37

                                               Illustration 20. Judo Flip




       In Judo, the opponent’s own momentum is used against him. Likewise, the force of Chitwood’s argument
regarding Rev 21:4 should be turned against him contextually so as to conclude that misthological death is eternal. He
correctly argues that misthological life necessitates misthological death. One cannot have eternal life without affirming
the reality of misthological death in the eternal state. However, since I have proven that rewards are eternal, even using
his own arguments to do so, it must be concluded that misthological death is eternal. Contextually, the absence of
misthological death is part of the reward limited to overcomers in the eternal state. Consequently, the presence of
misthological death is inferred in the eternal state on the part of subcomers.
                                             Illustration 21. These Things
                          Chapter 3. Practical Ramifications
                                                        Introduction
        The very text used to disprove the eternality of rewards, namely Rev 21:4, has been shown to prove that positive
rewards actually are eternal for the overcomer and consequently that negative rewards, including misthological death,
are eternal for the subcomer. The verse is a litotetic promise limited to overcomers. It presupposes that physical human
death will not exist during the eternal state and uses that presupposition as a launching pad to promise that no form of
death will exist for the overcomer in the eternal state. Not only will misthological death be ruled out for the overcomer,
but since they have glorified bodies animated by spirit, they will not even experience the death of bone or skin tissue.
Human beings who survive the tribulation and enter the eternal kingdom in physical bodies, in counter distinction, will
still experience the death of human bone and skin tissue. The Bible does not intend to be understood as ruling out this
type of physical death for humanity at large, either before the fall or after the effects of the fall are removed. Consequently,
since the Bible clearly affirms rewards in the eternal state, those texts which are understood most naturally as affirming
eternal rewards should be allowed to retain their normative meaning. A few examples will be mentioned.
                                                       Finest Hour
        A misthologist whom I hold in very high regard frequently exhorts believers not to miss out on millennial rulership
because this would be their “finest hour.” Respectively, I disagree. When we have been in heaven for a billion years, we
will not be making reminiscing remarks about the millennial kingdom as the good old days. I cringe at the thought of
picturing an overcomer remarking with a nostalgic sigh, “I remember in the good old days when being the Bride of Christ
really meant something special, but nowadays everybody is treated alike.” To the contrary, the best will always be yet to
come if you are an overcomer. The millennial kingdom might be likened to a honeymoon, but eternal matrimony cannot
be likened to monotony. If you are an overcomer, your finest hour always lies in the future. John’s stress on the thousand
years in Rev 20 is not his way of saying that the honeymoon is over when he reaches the eternal state in Rev 21-22.
Rather, the eternal state is the misthological finale.
        As Baughman recognizes, “The general teaching of Scripture is that the kingdom is eternal, that it lasts forever. It
is not until chapter 20 of Revelation that the thousand-year interval is marked off. Important events happen at that time,
but they are not the end of the kingdom.” “Revelation 20:6 does not mean that tribulation saints will only reign a thousand
years, but that they will reign with Him during this first thousand years” (emphasis his). Baughman adopts the KJV
translation of zao as lived in this verse and notes that the same tense is used of reigned so that they lived and reigned
would be understood as being simultaneous. They no more cease reigning at the end of the thousand years than they
cease living. An ingressive aorist would make sense, they began living and reigning. The notion of either action ceasing
is foreign to the thought of the verse. Baughman notes that premillennialists usually “overemphasize the millennium”
and supplies corrective charts of kingdom emphasis.38 Some kingdom teachers are certainly guilty of this overemphasis.
                                            Illustration 22. Kingdom Emphasis




38
     Ray E. Baughman, The Kingdom of God Visualized (Birmingham, AL: Shepherd Press, 1972), 120, 127, 186-187, 195.
Page 30                                                                                                Rewards are Eternal


       Following Baughman’s example, I would note that John’s purpose is not to impose a thousand year limitation on
the OT anticipation of an eternal kingdom. Although a transition occurs, a termination does not. The kingdom continues
to get bigger and better for the overcomer. As time progresses the quality of life that overcomers experience increases.
Sadly, many kingdom teachers have diminished the importance of not missing out on kingdom rulership by mistakenly
limiting this rulership to a thousand years—a mere drop in the eschatological bucket. Christ has some very negative
things to say about Christians who lose their first love for Him. Surely, if we keep our first love for Him, we can be
certain that He will not lose His first love for us! He will not love His Wife less than His Bride. The expressions of His
love for her will continue to expand as His kingdom continues to increase.
                                                         Aionios
       The Lord’s “dominion is an everlasting [aionios] dominion which will not pass away; and His kingdom is one
which will not be destroyed” (Dan 7:14). This kingdom, which is to be given to His saints, is eternal: “Then the
sovereignty, the dominion, and the greatness of all the kingdoms under the whole heaven will be given to the people of
the saints of the Highest One; His kingdom will be an everlasting [aionios] kingdom, and all the dominions will serve
and obey Him” (Dan 7:27). The Danielic fifth and final kingdom is that which is introduced by the millennium and
which continues throughout eternity. There is not a separate sixth kingdom for the eternal age. The millennial
kingdom is a transitional inauguration to the eternal regality of both the Lord and His faithful saints. This
reward—this rulership—is eternal.
                                                     Aion and Aionios
        The nominal and adjectival forms of this Greek, aion and aionios respectively, can be used interchangeably to
refer to eternity. For example, in Jn 4:14 both forms of the word are used to say that those who have eternal life will
never thirst. Never means forever, for eternity. Peter likewise says that Jesus’ kingdom is eternal and that our entrance
into this eternal kingdom can be misthologically abundant (2Pet 1:11). Those misthologists who insist that abundant
misthological entrance is limited to the millennial kingdom because rewards are only millennial are working from a false
premise. What is at stake is abundant entrance, or lack thereof, into the eternal kingdom. Compare His eternal dominion
in 1Pet 5:11 (aion) with our reward of eternal glory in 1Pet 5:10 (aionios).
        Whipple correctly acknowledges that aionios is used to talk about our abundant misthological entrance into the
eternal kingdom. Splendid! Yet he limits the fires of Gehenna to 1,000 years by translating aionios as age lasting in
Mt 18:8-90. But if rewards are eternal, as Whipple and I agree, then it makes no sense for him to try to limit negative
rewards to 1,000 years. Thus his literal misthological interpretation of Gehenna may be dismissed on the grounds of his
inconsistency.39 Accordingly, the fires of Gehenna in Mt 18:8-9 are best interpreted as being eternal:
       8
         And if your hand or your foot causes you to stumble, cut it off and throw it from you; it is better for you
       to enter life crippled or lame, than having two hands or two feet, to be cast into the eternal [aionios] fire.
       9
         And if your eye causes you to stumble, pluck it out, and throw it from you. It is better for you to enter life
       with one eye, than having two eyes, to be cast into the fiery hell [Gehenna]. (Mt 18:8-9)

       Some misthologists argue that aionios means “age-lasting” rather than “everlasting” in this passage. Admittedly,
an OT Greek lexicon, L&S, provides definitions of aionios that are inclusive of this meaning. The Greek Dictionary for
the NAS also defines it as meaning agelong or eternal. But the NAS never translates it as agelong in the NT, only as
eternal, eternity, and forever. As L&S indicates concerning aionios and as NIDNTT explicitly states concerning aion:
“In Plato the term [aion] is developed so as to represent a timeless, immeasurable and transcendent super-time…and
from it the Mysteries of Aion, the god of eternity could be celebrated in Alexandria.” As clearly seen in the definitions
provided by NIDNTT, aion is defined in terms of eternity or age-long, but aionios is limited to eternity. So on the basis
of these lexicons, one is still left to conclude that the NT meaning for aionios is eternal. Also, let it be noted that an
alternative OT Greek lexicon, LEH, defines it exclusively in terms of eternal rather than agelong.
       Whipple maintains his position that aionios in Mt 18:8-9 refers to the millennial age in his follow-up book on
Matthew.40 But what is bewildering about Whipple’s continued insistence on limiting aionios to the 1,000 years of the

39
   A correlative misthological understanding of Gehenna is not denied herein. Rather, the soteriological application to
unbelievers involves literal flames. the misthological application to believers does not. Dual applicability is allowed, but
both dimensions are eternal.
40
   Gary T. Whipple, The Matthew Mysteries: A Revelation of the Higher Wisdom Concerning the Church, Israel and the
Gentiles in Prophecy (Hayesville: Schoettle Publishing Co., 1995), 247.
Chapter 3. Practical Ramifications                                                                                     Page 31
millennial reign is the fact that in this second book he states: “The rewards that Peter wrote about for the church are not
those of the millennial kingdom, but the ‘everlasting [aionios] kingdom’ (2 Pet. 1:11).”41 Yet the same form of aionios
is used by Matthew and Peter in both passages. The fire is just as eternal as the everlasting kingdom. Whipple short-
circuits his own argument, apparently without realizing it. He repeatedly states that rewards are eternal but fails to
demonstrate why a believer’s punishment in Gehenna is temporary in light of the eternal nature of the believer’s
misthological punishment.42 He is correct that the misthological reward of the kingdom is eternal and consequently
incorrect that the punishment of the fire is not eternal.
         Although the noun aion is used to refer to a segment of time (i.e., age) or to eternity, its adjectival form aionios is
not used in this capacity in the NT. The adjective falls short of having the same breath of meaning possessed by the noun;
the adjective simply means eternal. Believers are not temporarily punished in Gehenna since punishment in Gehenna is
not temporary. The use of aionios in this Matthean passage would lead the reader of Matthew’s gospel to believe that
this punishment is eternal. Naturally, to be punished in Gehenna would indicate eternal punishment. Since its flames are
eternal, its punishment is most naturally taken as eternal.
         These soteriological flames are equivalent to the soteriological darkness of Jude 1:13, which describes the
apostates of that passage as, “Wild waves of the sea, foaming out their own shame; wandering stars, for whom the
blackness of darkness hath been reserved forever [aion].” Aion is typically translated as forever, for eternity, or for
everlasting in this passage. However, let us entertain Whipple’s argument that aion means millennial age in Jude 1:13
concerning genuine believers who have committed apostasy “for whom the black darkness has been reserved for the
millennial age” (TM).43 After all, aion is used in the singular in this verse, and if it means age, it is referring to one age,
not ages.
         Yet the very same form of the word aion is found in Jn 10:28: “And I give eternal [aionios] life to them, and they
shall never [aion] perish; and no one shall snatch them out of My hand.” Thus, using Whipple’s definition, this verse
could be retranslated as saying, “And I give millennial [aionios] life to them, and they will not perish in the millennial
age [singular]; and no one shall snatch them out of My hand” (TM). Even by Whipple’s definition, Jn 10:28 is saying
that no believer will ever perish in the millennial age. The verse is talking about all believers, since in that context the
picture of Jesus’ sheep as those who follow Him is simply, at least at the most fundamental level, a picture of those who
trust Him for eternal life.44 Those who believe His message and thus appropriate His free gift of eternal life are His sheep.
It is a parabolic picture of the didactical material presented earlier in Jn 5:24.45
         The promise that they will not perish is not limited to the millennium and is not limited to faithful believers. This
same construction is used in the promises He made to all believers in Jn 6:51,58. As a result of eating (i.e., appropriating),
they will live forever. Jesus is not merely promising millennial life or millennial salvation.46
         Apparently, Faust has overlooked a significant text and misconstrued his timeframes as a result. In Jn 11:26, Jesus
assures Martha that those believers who live until He returns will never die (cp. Jn 8:51-52). Physical death will not
intervene. This is a significant exception. Those carnal believers who are alive at the rapture will be caught up to be with
the Lord forever and are assured that they will never die. It would be impossible for them to be killed at the Bema.
         All believers already have eternal life as an absolute quantitative possession. On the basis of Christ’s promise, it
must be concluded that the possession of this life will prevent those believers who are alive when Christ returns from
ever experiencing physical death. The ultraistic premise held by some misthologists, that believers are raised to be killed,
must be rejected. Gehenna is not a literal millennial possibility for believers.
                                                    Sheep and Goats
        Some misthologists think that they have a way around the inconsistency that trips up Whipple. Instead of
considering some rewards eternal and some rewards as millennial, they simply conclude that no rewards are eternal.
Chitwood adopts this approach and thus denies that 2Pet 1:11 is talking about the eternal kingdom. Instead, he takes it
as talking about the age-lasting kingdom. In short, an abundant misthological entrance into the millennial kingdom is at
stake according to Chitwood, but entrance into the eternal kingdom is a level playing field. By limiting all rewards to the

Both the gift and reward strata of eternal life are eternal. So in both cases, aionios should be translated as eternal.

Page 32                                                                                                Rewards are Eternal

millennial kingdom, he has a hermeneutical sword by which to rightly divide, in his opinion, soteriological texts from
misthological texts. Thus, according to Chitwood, if a passage is talking about receiving aionios life as a free gift by
faith, then it must be referring to eternal life. If the passage is talking about winning, earning, or hoping for aionios life,
then it must be referring to millennial life.
        So, in Chitwoodian misthology, if a passage is talking about rewards and uses aionios, then aionios must be
translated as age lasting rather than as eternal in that particular instance because rewards are not eternal. Context is king,
and it must determine if aionios means eternal or age lasting in any given instance. Secondly, the only way the Greek
NT can express the concept of eternal apart from contextual considerations is with the plural from of aion (age). Aion is
the nominal form of aionios. Thus, unless the Greek writer uses the plural noun form for ages, one must consult the
context to know if the concept conveyed is millennial or eternal. And because Chitwood has used this argument to
conclude beforehand that rewards are not eternal, he is being consistent, within his hermeneutical approach, to conclude
that whenever singular aion or adjectival aionios is used in a misthological capacity, millennial rather than eternal
considerations must be in view.47 Granted, one might initially think that in this respect Chitwood has achieved a higher
degree of consistency with this approach than has Whipple. Unfortunately, this methodology has lead Chitwood into
colossal errors. His insisting that the goats cast into the aionios fire are regenerate (Mt 25:41) is only one such example.
His basic approach can be summarized syllogistically.
                                            Illustration 23. Mistaken Syllogism
     1. Eternal life is a gift
     2. A gift cannot be a reward.
     3. Therefore, eternal life cannot be a reward.
        Both his major and minor premises are correct. The problem is that his conclusion does not necessarily follow.
Scripture poses a riddle for us. And Chitwood’s answer to this riddle is highly unsatisfactory. Sometimes aionios is used
to refer to life as a gift; sometimes aionios is used to refer to life as a reward; however, a gift is never a reward. Chitwood
is correct in such affirmations. So how can aionios refer to life as both a gift and a reward? Chitwood’s solution is to
pose that aionios refers to eternal life when talking about a gift but age-lasting life when talking about a reward.
According to this solution, during the millennial kingdom, overcomers will have both age-lasting and eternal life.
Subcomers will only have eternal life during the millennial kingdom. The reward aspect of life is only age-lasting for the
overcomer; it is not eternal. Hence, he concludes that rewards are not eternal. However, we can use the same translation
for aionios—eternal—but in two different senses. My counter proposal is that the word eternal is used properly in relation
to both the gift aspect and reward aspects of eternal life. One need not use a different translation to make the separation
from the gift and reward dimensions. The major premise for the above syllogism would have to be: Eternal life is only a
gift. That presupposition is false, however; thus Chitwood’s argument fails. For eternal life is used in dual capacities.
                                                        Tree of Life
       Another ploy that Chitwood uses in a vain attempt to refute the eternal nature of rewards is to appeal to the tree of
life and add to the text by asserting that everyone will eat from the tree:

47
  For example, see Chitwood’s extremely repetitive claims regarding aion and aionios in The Most High Ruleth, revised

35-36, 59, 100; Prophecy on Mount Olivet, revised edition (2011), 176, 241, 384-386 435-440; Salvation by Grace
through Faith, revised edition (2012), 35; Salvation of the Soul, revised edition (2011), 83-84; Signs in John’s Gospel,

(2011), 77-79, 164-165; The Time of the End (2011), 320-321.
     According to Chitwood, because the gift of eternal life is a present possession, the gift aspect of that life cannot be a
future inheritance. The hope of millennial life rather than eternal life is in view in Tit 1:2 and 3:7. Paul is trying to win
the prize of millennial life in 1Tim 6:2. And passages such as Mk 10:17,30; Rom 16:25; Gal 6:8; and Tit 1:2; 3:7 are
examples where aionios cannot possibly mean eternal. I disagree with all these claims, of course. For Rom 16:25 see
Darkness; for Tit 1:2 and 3:7 see Destined. For the other texts, although discussed elsewhere in my writings, the present
discussion should suffice. Note, I concur with Chitwood, nonetheless, that the blessed hope (Tit 2:13) is the misthological
hope of aionios life. See Destined. However, I believe that this misthological life is eternal. As to the possibility of a
soteriological inheritance, see Gal 3:18 and Eph 1:11. The gift aspect of our present possession of eternal life guarantees
us a home in heaven and soteriological entrance into the millennial and eternal kingdom. An affirmation of the
soteriological dimension of our inheritance of eternal life must be retained. The full realization of our present possession
of the gift of eternal life awaits a future dimension.
Chapter 3. Practical Ramifications                                                                                       Page 33

            Man was created in the beginning to rule and to reign. And though only a part of saved mankind will
     have been brought back into a position to occupy the throne at the beginning of the Millennium (with the tree of
     life made available to them at this time), at the end of the Millennium the whole of saved mankind will be
     brought back into this [ruling and reigning] position (with the tree of life made available to them at this
     time).48 (Italics his.)
Supposedly, after 1000 years, per Chitwood’s claim as stated here, it will no longer matter if you lived for the Lord during
your life on Earth because all of saved humanity is going to rule with the Lord and have access to the tree of life during
the eternal state. Thus, in such Chitwoodian misthology, after unfaithful believers have been in the outer darkness for
1000 years, they will co-rule with Christ for the remainder of eternity. Rewards are only temporary, lasting only for the
fleeting millennium. Thereafter, unfaithful and faithful believers share and share alike in a communistic heaven.
Chitwood’s justification for this assessment is based on his rendering of Rev 22:2:

               This whole overall thought concerning the tree of life in Scripture would have to hold true, for that
       seen relative to this tree in Revelation chapter two is drawn from that which was first seen relative to this
       tree in Genesis chapters two and three. The fruit of the tree of life was in the past (seen in the Book of
       Genesis) and will be in the future (seen in the Book of Revelation) a provision for the rulers in the kingdom.
       This is an evident fact which must be recognized.
               And, in that coming day following the Millennium, the tree of life will be for “the healing of the
       nations” (Rev. 22:2). The Greek word translated “healing” is therapeia, from which the English word
       “therapy” is derived. This is a medical term which has to do with restorative healing. In that day, God will
       restore all of saved mankind to the original place which man occupied at the time of his creation. And,
       consequently, the whole of saved mankind, with regality in view, will have access to the tree of life.49 (Italics
       his.)

        Chitwood correctly perceives that the fruit of the tree of life was originally only given regally to kingdom rulers
in both Genesis and Revelation. The fruit of the tree of life is a reward; therefore, it is not available freely or universally
to all believers. Splendid! However, he then erroneously takes away a key detail from the biblical text in his ongoing
discussion in order to derive his errant opinion that the fruit of the tree of life is subsequently made available freely to
all believers within the eternal state. On the one hand, the Bible says that the leaves of the tree will be for the healing of
the nations. Chitwood, contrariwise, says that the tree will be for the healing of the nations. He has distorted the biblical
message by leaving out a crucial detail. By leaving out the leaves in Rev 22:2, he has made the unwarranted extrapolation
that the fruit of the tree must no longer be a reward limited to the faithful, as it was in Rev 2:7, since the tree is now
allegedly given to nations that did not earn a right to the tree in Rev 22:2. Never mind that the tree of life is still a
reward a few verses later in Rev 22:14,19! Chitwood has abused both the text and the context.50 He assumes, without
any justification, that being given the leaves of the tree entails the right to eat the fruit of the tree. But the leaves are not
the fruit; moreover, the leaves could be harvested and exported to the nations without giving the nations direct access to
the tree of life. With such unwarranted jumps in logic, he then proceeds to presume that all believers will eat the fruit and
thus rule the Earth.
        In addition to these types of errors, Chitwood fails to notice that the leaves die after they have been gathered from
the tree to be applied to the nations. This type of death still occurs in the eternal state. The leaves of the tree of life do not
have eternal life in that they do not live forever. The tree of life does not die, but its leaves do. For that matter, most plants
probably will still be subject to death, even in the eternal state. Certainly the plants we eat will be subject to death. This
observation, by the way, destroys Chitwood’s other argument against eternal rewards, which is based on his faulty
premise that no death of any type will exist in the eternal state, which leads him to make the defective inference that
misthological death cannot exist during the millennial state, which in turn leads to his fallacious conclusion that rewards

cannot continue in the eternal state. Terribly erroneous conclusions can be reached when one takes away from the biblical

       Although Dillow acknowledges that rewards are eternal, he too, like Chitwood, makes some errant assumptions
regarding the nature of death in the eternal state and thus the role of the leaves:

       There is no room here for two kinds of bodies in the eternal state, enhanced mortal bodies which do not
       need healing but can have broken bones and the glorified resurrection bodies mentioned elsewhere. These
       bodies are “imperishable.” They are like Jesus’ body in His resurrection (Philippians 3:21), physical but
       glorified and indestructible. There can be no physical wounds because this would involve deterioration and
       death of flesh or bone cells which must then be replaced. Death has been forever removed at the beginning
       of the eternal state [Rev 21:4].51

       With Dill, I agree with Dillow that the death of skin and bone cells are legitimate forms of death. However, since
these forms of death must have been present in the Garden of Eden before the fall, there is no reason to think that they
will not exist in the eternal state after the effects of the fall have been removed. Dillow wrongly thinks that the leaves of
the tree do not provide healing during the eternal state, while Chitwood rightly acknowledges that the eternal state is in
preview. Inversely, Chitwood incorrectly fails to perceive that some form of death necessarily is inferred by being
hurt physically in the eternal state, while Dillow astutely deduces that some form of death is involved inescapably in
being hurt physically. Their offsetting arguments readily lead to the proper conclusion: Human procreation, healing, and
rewards will continue during the eternal state.52
                                                        Stephanos
        In 1Cor 9:24-25, Paul says that the crown we earn is a stephanos, not a diadem. Our victor’s crown is earned, not
freely bestowed as a birthright. In numerous places in his writings, Chitwood correctly emphasizes the earned nature of
this stephanos.53 And he has an interesting discussion regarding the differences between the two types of crowns wherein
he asserts:

              In this respect, overcoming Christians have been promised a stephanos (victor’s crown), never a
       diadema (monarch’s crown); but the promised stephanos will become a diadema at the time overcoming
       Christians assume positions on the throne with Christ. There can be no such thing as either Christ or His
       co-heirs wearing a stephanos in that day. They can only wear the type crown referred to by the word
       diadema. (Emphasis his.)54

       His theory is interesting. Let us suppose that Chitwood is correct in this respect. Paul states that the overcomer’s
stephanos is imperishable (aphthartos). To argue that this earned victor’s crown (i.e., stephanos) ceases to be an earned
victor’s crown at the end of the millennial age, so as to pose that it is freely given then to all believers, is to disbelieve
the biblical text. This stephanos, this earned victor’s crown, must be won; it is not given as a gift. To replace the Pauline
stephanos (that is contextually limited to overcomers) with a supposed diadem (that is allegedly given to subcomers and
overcomers alike in the eternal state) is to do away with the imperishable stephanos. In doing so, Chitwood makes the
imperishable perishable, which is impossible. A believer’s crown and its attendant rulership must be earned; it cannot be
given freely, and it cannot be taken away in eternity. It is imperishable! If the affirmation of imperishability does not
apply to the form of the crown, then certainly it must at least apply to the qualification for the crown. Overcomers are
winners and their rulership is won; hence stephanos is used to describe it. Stephanos is used of misthological regal
authority. It is a reward-crown to be won, not a gift-crown given simply by virtue of royal birth.55

51
   Dillow, Destiny, 119-120, n. 398.
52
   This part of my argument for the eternality of rewards is derived from the hypothesis that being hurt physically during
the eternal state necessitates a form of death. Part of my defense for the eternality of rewards in this present section pits
Dillow against Chitwood. An even more straightforward argument for the eternality of rewards is derivable from the
thesis that subcomers are hurt correlatively (and thus misthologically) by the second death during the eternal state. That
argument principally pits Chitwood against himself. In other words, Rev 21:4 is not ruling out all forms of death for
everyone, only for overcomers. Misthological death is necessarily implied for subcomers.
