        Moreover, in this very quote Chitwood has acknowledged that his proposed transition from stephanos to diadema
occurs at the beginning of the millennial kingdom! Even the diadem is limited to overcomers! Subcomers do not get a
diadem. If the imperishable stephanos is to become a diadem at the beginning of the millennial kingdom, then the diadem
becomes an emblem of earned reward at that time. Both the imperishable and misthological nature of the stephanos must
be transferred to the diadem. The diadem is an imperishable, hence eternal, reward limited to overcomers. So how can
Chitwood deduce that the Scripture is silent regarding eternal rewards, or claim that rewards cease, or arrogate that
subcomers rule?
        One can be open to Chitwood’s theory that the stephanos becomes a diadema without committing wholesale
treason to the reason the crowns were given in the first place. The crowns are awarded as rewards, not given as gifts.
Chitwood would have done well if he had said of the overcomers: “They, and they alone, can only wear the type crown
referred to by the word diadema.” But as it is, although he does not state it in his oft repeat discussion regarding stephanos,
he makes it implicitly clear, in his equally oft stated argument against eternal rewards and in his redundant discussion of
aionios, that the misthological nature of the stephanos must cease, in his opinion, at the end of the millennial kingdom
because rewards are not eternal. But this claim is tantamount to saying that the imperishable crown is perishable. Winners
lose their winner’s distinction at the end of the millennial kingdom if losers become winners. If everyone is equally
special, then no one is distinctively special. The diadem becomes a mockery of the winners’ faithfulness in Chitwoodian
misthology.
        No wonder another kingdom teacher, who like Chitwood emphasizes the millennial nature of rewards, keeps
looking back at the millennial kingdom as being “our finest hour.” For in the mind of those buying into the age-lasting
limitation to the misthological nature of the rulership, the consequence of their outlook, in terms of investing our current
sacrifice for Christ with any lasting significance, is that eternity in Heaven will be a disappointment when compared to
the golden age of the millennial kingdom on Earth. The eternal rulership may be greater in scope in their minds, expanding
throughout the universe in the eternal state, but it will be lesser in significance, being expanded to those who were
disqualified to receive it previously. In such misthology, having lived for Christ will become absolutely meaningless in
eternity as rulership is given indiscriminately to those who were disqualified to exercise it millennially.
        Because this age-lasting limitation of the misthological nature of the kingdom message is so disheartening to many
of the faithful, taking time to refute the arguments of those who deny that rewards are eternal has been necessary. The
assault on the eternality of rewards by the age-lasting intimidation is nothing less than an attack against the gospel of the
kingdom itself. Sometimes one must defend the gospel of the kingdom from its friends when its friends become its fiends.
Yet now, having shown multiple errors in their misthological limitations, we may proceed unhindered with standard
arguments for eternal rewards.
                                                  Ever, Never, and Forever
       The fact that both the positive and negative results of judgment are eternal is demonstrated in a variety of ways.
In contrast to the destruction that unfaithful believers will receive in Gal 6:8, faithful believers reap eternal life, not mere
millennial life. Isaiah says they will inherit the land forever (Is 60:21). The duration of the inheritance should be
contrasted with that of the non-inheritance. Heb 3:11 and Ps 95:11 assert that unfaithful believers will never enter My
Rest. Never means forever? BDAG (ei, 4) likewise calls this grammatical construction a “marker of strong or solemn
assertion,” in this case, a negation that should be translated strongly as “they shall certainly not enter.” This is an emphatic
negation in the Greek text. The unfaithful elect will never ever enter the rest. Not only are the inheritance and rest eternal,
the disinheritance and unrest are also eternal. Both the positive and negative aspects of the judgment are eternal.56 Those
who are cast into the outer darkness are there eternally, at least in terms of the misthological consequences being
represented by exclusion from the misthological inheritance and rest.
                                        Reception of the Unshakable Kingdom
        Those who died in the wilderness (and who consequently never entered the land) never received the inheritance,
just as Esau never received it. The writer of Hebrews mentions that Esau did receive a limited blessing, “By faith Isaac


aspect of their inheritance is affirmed by Peter to be a reward that will not fade away (amarantos; 1Pet 1:4). To argue
that the misthological nature of this inheritance fades away at the end of the millennial kingdom is to run counter to the
text.
56

limited to the last prophet day, the 1K. However, the stress in Hebrews is that some believers will “never” enter this
rest/rulership. To pose that all believers enter this rest/rulership after the 7th day is contrary to the contextual emphasis.
Additionally, God rested on the 7th day according to Hebrews. Does this mean He rested only 1000 years? If so, His rest
would be over. Yet Hebrews emphasizes that this rest is still present. So obviously God’s rest on the 7th day is not limited
to 1000 years. Context is key, and it does not limit the rest to 1000 years.

blessed Jacob and Esau, even regarding things to come” (Heb 11:20). But the blessing Esau received there does not
nullify the fact that the rejection he experienced in Heb 12:16-17 was eternal.
         15
           See to it that no one comes short of the grace of God; that no root of bitterness springing up causes trouble,

       Further, the writer of Hebrews goes on to say in Heb 12:25-29 that the kingdom we will receive (i.e., rule) will be
unshakable and remain. BDAG (1.a) cites Lk 19:12,15 as examples where receiving a kingdom (basileia) is equivalent
to receiving a kingship. Receiving a kingdom can certainly mean more than entering a kingdom; it can be used to describe
receiving the right to exercise rulership over that kingdom. Although this expression of receiving a kingdom appears
capable of being used soteriologically to denote mere entrance into the kingdom (which appears to be the case in
Mk 10:15; Lk 18:17), it can also be used misthologically to denote rulership of the kingdom received.
                                                               Rest
        Passages such as Num 14:21-23; Dt 3:18-20; 12:8-10; and Josh 1:13 demonstrate that the rest is a place,
specifically the land of Canaan. The capital city, Zion, is especially the resting place (Ps 132:13-14). Further, although
the rest is a place (the land and city), it is also an experience of rulership over the kingdom. Some misthologists are
inconsistent in claiming that all the elect will enter into the place of rest, but only the faithful elect will enter into the
experience of rest. It would be much more consistent to affirm that the unfaithful elect will never enter this rest at all—
either spatially or experientially. After all, the rest is both a place and an experience. Therefore, it should be concluded
that unfaithful believers will never enter into the place or experience of rest (rulership). Subcomers will never enter the
land, the city, or rule in the kingdom. The outer darkness appears to be a picture of this doctrinal fact. The outer darkness
is both a place and an experience. As the rest is both spatial and experiential, so is the lack of rest—the outer darkness.
The outer darkness is best defined as the spatial experience of being within the kingdom but never ever being
allowed to enter the capital city, nor the land of Canaan, and never ever being allowed to exercise rulership over
any part of the kingdom during the millennial or eternal epochs.
                                       Aphthartos, Amarantos, and Amarantinos
       As already noted, the eternity of our recompense is confirmed in Paul’s statement that our wreath would be
imperishable (aphthartos; 1Cor 9:25). Peter likewise said that our inheritance would be imperishable (aphthartos) and
not fade away (amarantos; 1Pet 1:4) and that our crown would not fade away (amarantinos; 1Pet 5:4). Our crown and
inheritance are just as imperishable (aphthartos) as our new birth and the word of God that produces that birth (1Pet 1:23).
Our rewards are imperishable and unfading. If the distinctions between the faithful and unfaithful are obliterated at the
end of the 1000 years, then the rewards cease to be rewards at that time, which cannot be the case. Whether Jesus is our
Savior will have everlasting consequences: heaven versus hell. Whether Jesus is our Lord will also have everlasting
consequences: rest versus unrest.
                                           Eternal Rewards in the New Jerusalem
Tree and City Exclusion
        The tree of life and the city of Rev 22:14,19 are implicitly denoted as rewards in 22:12. This misthological
description of the tree and city follows the discussion of the tree in the New Jerusalem in Rev 21:2 and 22:2.57 “And I
saw the holy city, new Jerusalem, coming down out of heaven from God, made ready as a bride adorned for her husband”
(Rev 21:2). What is at stake in Rev 22:14,19 is not merely exclusion from the tree and the Heavenly Jerusalem during
the millennium. What is at stake are rewards concerning the Heavenly Jerusalem after it has come down out of heaven.
Thus, rewards are at risk regarding New Jerusalem in the eternal state. Indeed, Lang anticipated this very conclusion. In
regard to the question, “Is the vision of the Holy City in chs. 21 and 22 ‘millennial, eternal, or both?’” he responds,
“Certainly it is eternal....But is also millennial, because the marriage of the Lamb takes place just before He comes forth
to set up His kingdom on earth” (Rev 19:6-8). I would prefer to say that it refers to inaugurated eternity, but that minor
point aside, I definitely agree with Lang’s statement that this reward list is limited, both during the millennium and
throughout eternity, “for that limited portion of the saved known to Scripture as ‘the church of the firstborn ones who are
enrolled in heaven’…there attaining thereto is the reward that grace will give for the sufferings which grace enabled them
to bear unto the end.” Moreover, I would agree with his calling this reward “the heavenly portion.” The “eternal kingdom”

57
     Rev 22:1-2 is a recapitulation of the New Jerusalem described in Rev 21:1-2,10-26.
Chapter 3. Practical Ramifications                                                                                    Page 37
will be “RICHLY supplied” to those who overcome (emphasis his). The saved not comprising this rewarded company
“will live on earth, not in the regions above, in heaven” (i.e., the Heavenly City)—as he maintains—both during the
millennium and throughout eternity.58 For the most part, in my writings I simply am tracing out the logical implications
of Lang’s statements with increased clarity and consistency.
       For example, Wilkin is certainly correct to conclude that Rev 22:14-17 is dealing with the New Jerusalem and the
eternal state.59 The misthological threat in Rev 22:19 is also dealing with the tree and New Jerusalem in the eternal
kingdom. Lang correctly perceives that they lose their portion in the city and tree both millennially and eternally.60
Millennial exclusion becomes eternal exclusion from the tree and the New Jerusalem! Nevertheless, Wilkin stops short
of making this connection, but it is the logical extension of his argument. He is inconsistent in concluding that all believers
will enter the city. Whipple tentatively takes a similar position: “Finally, all the saved, i.e., all those whose names are
written in the Lamb’s Book of Life (Rev. 21:27), will have the right to enter the city, though they probably will not be
permitted to live there.”61 Correspondingly, at most the subcomers will enter the city through some other means than the
gates. They certainly will not inherit or inhabit the city.
       This approach is attractive but not entirely persuasive. The presence of one’s name in the Book of Life guarantees
that one will not be thrown into hell (Rev 20:15) and that one will live somewhere in the kingdom of heaven. According
to Rev 21:27, only those who have their names written in the Book of Life will enter the Heavenly City on Earth: “And
nothing unclean and no one who practices abomination and lying, shall ever come into it, but only [ei me] those whose
names are written in the Lamb’s book of life.” Even so, this does not prove that all those who have their name written in
the Book of Life will enter the city. In this verse, the direct contrast may be simply between the lost and saved, not
between subcomers and overcomers. The lost will never enter the city; only the saved will enter it.
       Theoretically speaking at least, if people born without a sin nature in eternity future do not receive the gift of
eternal life and therefore do not have their names in the Book of Life, they nevertheless will be inhabitants of the eternal
kingdom and be allowed to visit inside the capital city—New Jerusalem. They would be exceptions to the ei me clause.
The emphatic negation (ou me) in Rev 21:27 would not apply to them since they will be sinless, in contrast to the lost
who retain their sin nature throughout eternity and who are designated as liars in the eternal state.62 Alternatively, people
born without a sin nature in eternity future may have eternal life and have their names in the Book of Life. In that case,
their being allowed to enter does not prove that subcomers will be allowed to enter.
Special Entrance
       That overcomers will have a special entrance by the gates into the city in Rev 22:14 does not indicate that
subcomers will have a common entrance. The New Earth will be populated by overcomers, subcomers, and general
inhabitants of the New Earth. The New Earth is not ethereal. It is not a Non-Earth. These general inhabitants will
procreate, and they and their descendants will have the general entrance into the New Jerusalem. Subcomers will have
no entrance. In contrast to the general population who inhabit the New Earth, overcomers will have an entrance that
denotes their honor as those who inherit the New Earth (cf. Mt 5:3). To summarize in simplified terms, Rev 22:14
describes overcomers as inside the New Jerusalem; Rev 22:15 describes noncomers as outside the eternal kingdom;
and Rev 22:19 describes subcomers as outside the New Jerusalem—and such will be the eternal state of affairs. A
correlation may be seen to exist between the noncomers and subcomers being outside.

   Lang, Revelation, 380-381. Unfortunately, Lang shies away from adopting a literal view of the Heavenly City (p. 372);
nevertheless, he beautifully describes it as a wedding canopy above the earthly Jerusalem (pp. 364-365). He would
correctly consider this Heavenly City to be a regal reward for the triumphant Bride (p. 379).

                                             Illustration 24. Outside the City




       The outer darkness is best conceived of as a metaphorical description of the eternal unrest and punishment that
subcomers experience by being excluded from (1) the land of Canaan—the Heavenly Jerusalem—during the millennium,
(2) the New Jerusalem for eternity, and (3) the rule of the millennial and eternal kingdom. The form and location of
the outer darkness may change, but just as Jerusalem will be eternal in one form or location or another, so the
exclusion from the New Jerusalem will be eternal also. The black darkness of the outer darkness is eternal. It is
tempting to agree with Chitwood and Whipple that Jude 1:13 describes the outer darkness.63 In that case, I would argue
that these regenerate apostates are “wandering stars for whom the black darkness has been reserved forever” and thus
would claim direct support for my conclusion that the outer darkness is eternal. But the context suggests a correlation
instead and thus indirectly, rather than directly, supports my thesis that the outer darkness is eternal.
                                                        Casting Crowns
        So some think: “What difference does it make if we get a get a crown since we are just going to throw it away
anyway?” This perception that rewards do not really matter throws the analogy of faith into reverse and attempts to deny
the clear teaching of Scripture on the basis of an ambiguous verse in Rev 4:10. In response to such an absurd denial of
the doctrine of rewards, one should step back and ask, “Do we truly ever lose anything that we give to Jesus? Is not the
consistent teaching of Scripture that anything we give to Jesus we will save and receive back a hundred fold?” Second,
does it really make sense for Jesus to exhort us to keep our crowns in Rev 3:11 only to tell us in the next chapter that we
will not keep them? Might there not be a more probable interpretation?
        Moreover, the identification of the twenty-four elders is debatable; even much less certain is the significance of
their action. Assuming that they do represent rewarded believers, as seems probable, then various interpretations of their
action are compatible with the eternal nature of our reward. Their casting their crowns could be a picture of the Spirit-
filled life and the Spirit’s enablement. What we accomplish in this life and the rewards we correspondingly obtain are
attributed to His enablement and pictured as such by our casting our crowns before Jesus’ feet. What is pictured is our
acknowledgement that we have earned our eternal rewards by His enablement. Such a position is not incompatible with
the eternal nature of our rewards. Still, a better interpretation is suggested by historical precedence and Rev 11:16. Lang’s
historical illustration is outstanding:

         In A.D. 63 Tiridates, king of Armenia...agreed to renounce his crown to the [Roman] emperor Nero, on the
         understanding that he should receive it back as the emperor’s feudatory….Having thus divested himself of
         regal power and rank, it remained for Tiridates to undertake a journey to Rome, in order to receive from
         the hand of the emperor the crown of Armenia, together with all the rights and securities the Roman people
         and Senate could confer.64


63
     Chitwood, Judgment (1986), 173; Whipple, Shock, 158-160.
64
     Lang, Revelation, p 113.
Chapter 3. Practical Ramifications                                                                                      Page 39
                                          Illustration 25. The Boomerang Crown




        Tiridates renounced his crown in order to receive it back again as the crown of Armenia. In other words, he gave
up his own crown in order to receive it back from the hand of the emperor. The action in Rev 4:10 could be a picture of
this type of event or of the similar spiritual truth that the person who saves his life will lose it, and he who losses his life
for Jesus’ sake will save it (Mk 8:35). If you do not give it to Jesus, you will find it taken from you (Mt 25:28). If you
give it to Jesus, you will find it given back to you (Mk 10:29-30).
                                                     Retaining Thrones
        In any case, Rev 11:16 says that the elders still have their thrones even after casting their crowns before the
emperor’s thrown in Rev 4:10. The twenty-four elders constantly leave their seats on their respective thrones to fall in
worship before the emperor’s throne (Rev 4:4,10; 5:8; 11:16; 19:4). They obviously return to their own thrones after each
occasion, or they would not be able to fall down before Him on the next occasion. Since falling down before Him and
worshiping Him are not onetime events, it may be reasonably inferred that the same is true of casting their crowns before
Him. In any event, they do not lose their thrones nor their ruling power as indicated by their continuing to have thrones.
They do not cast their thrones at His feet! They still have those thrones in ch. 11, after casting their crowns in ch. 4.
Submitting themselves, their thrones, and their crowns to Him in worship does not result in the loss of themselves, their
thrones, or their crowns.
        They probably cast their crowns before His feet on special occasions, which of course means they do not lose their
crowns. As a matter of fact, the Greek text suggests this very possibility. They cast their crowns when (4:9) the living
creatures give glory. According to BDAG, the word translated as when (hotan) could just as well be translated as
whenever, as often as, every time that to signify repeated action. In fact this lexicon says that it is used to indicate repeated
action “in many instances.” The NAS certainly renders hotan as whenever in multiple instances: Mt 6:16; 10:23; Mk 3:11;
11:19,25; 14:7; Jn 7:27; 8:44; 16:21; 2Cor 10:6. BDAG (1.b.a) considers Rev 4:9 itself to be one of these instances.
Several translations likewise render 4:9 as whenever (NKJ; NAB; NLT) or every time (NJB). In conjunction with the
latter translation and in accordance with the BDAG rendition, it is best to take it as denoting repeated action:
       9
        And every time the living creatures give glory and honor and thanks to Him who sits on the throne, to
       Him who lives forever and ever, 10the twenty-four elders will fall down before Him who sits on the throne,
       and will worship Him who lives forever and ever, and will cast their crowns before the throne. (Rev 4:9-
       10; TM)

       A repeated action is my preferred understanding of the text. On the other hand, my position is not dependent upon
the action being repeated. The submission and recognition that vassal kings (or servant kings) pay to a suzerain (or great
king) in this manner need not be done repeatedly. A similar comparison can be made concerning Christ. His onetime
deliverance of the kingdom up to the Father in 1Cor 15:24-25 does not demonstrate that He will cease to rule or lose the
kingdom! When Christ hands the kingdom over to the Father, this does not mean that Christ will cease to rule. It means
that the nature of that rule changes to a co-rulership with His Father (Rev 22:1). Casting our crowns before Him may
likewise denote the co-rulership we will share with Him (Rev 3:21). Other passages of Scripture show that in giving up
the kingdom, the Lord does not lose it. The Lord did not cease to be Lord! For example, Rev 22:3 shows that the Lamb
Page 40                                                                                             Rewards are Eternal

will still be on the throne in the eternal state. The delivering over of His rule to the Father does not mean that His rule
will cease but that it will change its character.65 Correspondingly, the casting of our crowns before the throne does not
mean that we lose the crowns. We no more lose our crowns or rulership than Jesus loses His throne or His Lordship!
Handing over does not mean giving up. It means to turn over to Him in order to share with Him. Jesus rules forever
(Rev 5:13), and so do we (Dan 7:18; 12:3). Otherwise, He would cease to be King of kings and Lord of lords (Rev 17:14;
19:16). For Him to remain Sovereign King over vassal kings, the vassal kings must continue to function as kings. The
casting of the elders’ crowns before Jesus in Rev 4:10 cannot denote abandoning ruling servitude to Jesus since they sing
about experiencing a future rule over the earth in Rev 5:10. Rulership and rewards are eternal, not merely millennial.
Overcomers rule forever (Rev 22:5; cp. 2Chron 13:5).
                                                         Conclusion
        According to some misthologists, the distinction between a reward and gift evaporates after 1,000 years. This
study has affirmed the eternal nature of rewards by looking at the primary texts used for and against this position. The
everlasting misthological exclusion pictured by the outer darkness is also confirmed. Such believers will never ever enter
God’s rest. Typology concerning Esau from the book of Hebrews further substantiates this misthological understanding
of the significance of rest in the epistle as well so that both Esau and the rest are understood to portray the permanence
of this exclusion and thus substantiating the definition of the outer darkness made herein. The misthological reality
introduced by the millennium will be carried over into and projected infinitely throughout eternity future. Since the
positive rewards are imperishable and unfading, the parallel impoverishment resulting from not having these rewards
must last just as long. Unfaithful believers will not be rewarded with these possessions at the end of the millennium, and
faithful believers will not be stripped of their rewards at that time. Consequently, the distinction within heaven between
the have’s and the have-not’s must remain throughout the never-ending ages. The city and certain privileges pertaining
to it are limited to overcomers, not only in the millennial age, but also in the eternal state. Those believers who are
stripped of their talents and thrown into the outer darkness will have an impoverished experience of life throughout
eternity. This improvised life appropriately may be termed misthological death.
        In emphatic conclusion, the outer darkness may be defined as the parabolic representation of the didactical
reality that unfaithful believers will undergo in the permanent punitory (rather than preparatory or purgatorial)
spatial experience of being in the millennial and eternal kingdom but never ever being allowed to enter the capital
city, or the land of Canaan throughout eternity future, with the result that those submitted to this banishment will
never ever be allowed to exercise rulership (or participate in any other misthological functions, roles, or privileges
pertaining thereto) over any part, sphere, or aspect of the millennial or eternal kingdom during the millennial age
or for any part of eternity future, forever.




65
     Walvoord, Revelation, BKC, 987.
                           Excursus on Judas being Unclean
        Misthologists generally recognize that Judas was unregenerate. However, Chitwood and those misthologists
following his lead take exception to that rule. The passage in which Jesus washed the disciples’ feet is a key passage in
that regard. The words for bathe (louo) and wash (nipto), as well as their corresponding levels of forgiveness, are critical
in that passage. Since Chitwood believes that Judas was a believer and that all believers will rule in eternity, his position
leads to the conclusion that Judas will be a ruler in the eternal state. Therefore, addressing this issue is appropriate in the
present study. For purposes of the present discussion, the temporal plane of forgiveness is excluded from consideration
as a distinct plane, and a lower level is added below soteriological forgiveness so that consideration of the unforgiven
state of unbelievers might be included.
                                 Illustration 26. Forgiven or Unforgiven at Four Levels




       6
         And so He came to Simon Peter. He said to Him, “Lord, do You wash [nipto] my feet?”
       7
        Jesus answered and said to him, “What I do you do not realize now, but you shall understand
       hereafter.” 8 Peter said to Him, “Never shall You wash [nipto] my feet!” Jesus answered him,
       “If I do not wash [nipto] you, you have no part [meros] with Me.” 9 Simon Peter said to Him,
       “Lord, not my feet only, but also my hands and my head.” 10 Jesus said to him, “He who has
       bathed [louo] needs only to wash [nipto] his feet, but is completely clean [holos katharos];
       and you [plural] are clean [katharos], but not all of you.” 11 For He knew the one who was
       betraying Him; for this reason He said, “Not all of you are clean [katharos]. 12 And so when
       He had washed their feet, and taken His garments, and reclined at the table again, He said to
       them, “Do you know what I have done to you?” (Jn 13:6-12)
Page 42                                                                                               Rewards are Eternal

                                                     Completely Clean
        Chitwood acknowledges that the bath (louo) represents the complete washing that happens to all believers at the
point of salvation, pertaining to their being in Christ and that wash (nipto) is the partial washing thereafter. According to
Chitwood, Judas was unclean in the latter sense (i.e., the green sense) rather than the former sense (i.e., the blue sense)
since Jesus washed Judas’ feet.66 However, Chitwood leaves the question unanswered as to how it would have been better
for Judas not to have been born (Mt 26:24; Mk 14:21) if he is going to rule forever! Also, Jesus previously had indicated
that Judas did not believe from the beginning (Jn 6:64), another matter Chitwood does not address. Moreover, contrary
to Chitwood, Jesus indicates that what Judas lacked was the fundamental level of being clean (katharos) and
consequently the advanced level of being completely clean [holos katharos].
        The word order of the text makes it clear as to what level of cleanliness is entailed. Additionally, the sequence of
events in the context confirms what level Judas was missing. When Jesus said, “You [plural] are clean [katharos],” He
was not talking about the green level but the blue level of cleanliness associated with the bath (louo). Jesus had not yet
washed all of their feet. Thus He cannot be understood contextually as saying that they are all completely clean, and thus
talking at the green level, when He says, “You are clean, but not all of you.”
         John provides a parenthetical explanation in v. 11 to clarify why all of them are not clean. John does not say,
“For He had not yet finished washing all their feet.” Rather, he clarifies, “For He knew the one who was betraying Him.”
Foot washing would avail nothing for the one who had not had the bath of regeneration (Tit 3:5). Jesus demonstrates that
He is talking at the fundamental level since in v. 10 Jesus does not say, “You are completely clean [holos katharos]; but
not all of you.” Instead, He talks at the more rudimentary level: “You are clean [katharos], but not all of you.” The word
completely is completely missing in the part of v. 10 that is being affirmed as true of the entire group. What is being
denied is something more basic, the basic level of being clean.
                     Illustration 27. The Duality of Unclean and Clean on a Tri-Threshold Chart




       Judas was not completely clean because he was not fundamentally clean. He had not received the bath of
regeneration. Jesus is pointing out that the typological significance of His washing their feet would not apply to Judas.
Judas was neither soteriologically clean nor familially completely clean. He was unclean at the green level because he
was unclean at the black level. You cannot skip a first base. The foot washing will do no good (relative to partnership in
Christ’s kingship) to those who have not had the bath. In order to have a part in reclining regally with Christ at table in

66
  Arlen L. Chitwood Ruth, revised edition (Norman, OK: The Lamp Broadcast, Inc., 2012), 92-93. Arlen L. Chitwood,
From Egypt to Canaan, revised edition (Norman, OK: The Lamp Broadcast, Inc., 2011), 114.
Excursus on Judas being Unclean                                                                                      Page 43
His kingdom, one must have had the bath followed by the washing. A bath is not enough, as Peter learned. A washing is
not enough, as Judas learned. Jesus is demonstrating the Principle of Stratification. Sequence is important. You cannot
skip steps. To reach the yellow level, you first have to get out of the black level. Judas never made this step. He was
soteriologically unclean. Hence, it would have been better for him if he never had been born because he never believed
and will burn eternally as a result (Jn 6:64). The foot washing is in vain for those who have not had the bath. Soteriological
death is Judas’ eternal result. It would have been better for Judas if he had not been born, and thus never had been born
(Mt 26:24; Mk 14:21). Not equals never in such a case.
       Similarly, Peter had commenced his objection to the foot washing by saying, “Never shall You wash my feet!" To
which Jesus responded, “If I do not wash you, you have no part with Me” (Jn 13:8). Jesus’ not (me) and no (ou) echoes
Peter’s never (ou me) in that Jesus’ double negative (me ou) mirrors Peter’s double negative (ou me). Jesus should not be
misconstrued by age-lasting limitations as saying, “If I do not wash, then you eventually will have a part [meros] with
Me.” To the contrary, those believers who refuse to submit to the washing never will have a partnership with Christ in
His kingdom. Loss of rewards is eternal.
                                                             Typology
       Chitwood would insist, nonetheless, that since Jesus washed Judas’ feet, Judas must have been a believer. My
counter is that Jesus denies that the typological significance of this event applies personally to Judas—who is not clean,
much less completely clean, although he had undergone the symbolic cleansing. My position is in harmony with how
typology works. The typological significance of an event does not necessarily apply to the individual who undergoes that
event. For example, as a result of striking the rock twice, Moses was prevented from entering the Promised Land and
may be taken as a typological representation of a believer who fails to inherit the kingdom misthologically. Nevertheless,
as an individual, Moses did qualify for the misthological inheritance, appearing in white on the Mount of Transfiguration
(in the Promised Land), but he was not allowed to enter the Promise Land during his lifetime due to the typological
significance of the event. Likewise, the application of the blood to the houses of all the Israelites does not mean that all
the Israelites necessarily were justified soteriologically. Also, although all Jewish males were circumcised, this does not
mean that all Jewish males were justified soteriologically.
       Typology permits flexibility. The claim that those who were saved by looking at the serpent could not
typologically represent those who needed soteriological justification/cleansing because they already had experienced this
redemption typologically in the application of the blood in Egypt seems to fail on two accounts: (1) in supposing that the
same group of people cannot be used to illustrate the same truth in a variety of ways and (2) by looking only to the past
and not to the future. If looking at the serpent represents misthological salvation, then what does inheriting the land
represent? Is it not clear that inheriting the land refers to misthological salvation? Those who were saved from the serpents
died in the wilderness and failed to obtain misthological salvation. Thus, from this perspective it would seem best to take
the serpent incident soteriologically since all they had to do was look and live. More was required to inherit the land.
Those who originally obtained the salvation from looking at the serpent failed to inherit the land. What is guaranteed in
Jn 3:16-19 is not misthological salvation. For more on the duality of this text in Num 21:4-9, demonstrating that it is used
in a dual capacity, see my 4D Forgiveness (4DF). That book uses various strata/plane illustrations to make the same point
as being made herein. The strata are alternative means of picturing the thresholds.
                                                  Judas did not Believe
       John not only indicates that Judas was soteriologically unclean in ch. 13, he had earlier indicated that the reason
was because Judas was an unbeliever (Jn 6:64-71), who in that context had failed to appropriate the bread of life. John
already had noted that Jesus was the sacrificial lamb who was to take away the sin of the world (Jn 1:29). He also noted
that the benefits of this death did not apply to all the Jews, since those who did not believe would die in their sins
(Jn 8:21,24). The Pharisees and Sadducees, as a general rule, were unregenerate disbelievers as well, despite the fact that
they offered the sacrificial lamb. Confirmation for this deduction comes in variety of forms, not the least of which is that
Jesus says that they were unbelievers! Repeatedly, Jesus explicitly says, “You do not believe” (Jn 3:12; 5:38,45-47; 6:64;
8:45; 10:26,38). In like fashion, Judas was a Jewish unbeliever who died in his sins; he was soteriologically unclean.
Judas was not a justified Jew who will suffer loss of rewards for a thousand years and then have his tears wiped away
and spend eternity ruling in heavenly bliss.
                                                     Correlativity
       Nonetheless, Chitwood is on to something substantial when he finds it significant that Judas was allowed to attend
the Supper and have his feet washed before being forced to leave during the Supper. Correlatively, although Judas was
an unbeliever, he is used as a typological illustration of what will happen to unfaithful believers at the Lord’s
Supper. They will be cast into the darkness outside the feast. John does not let it escape our attention that “it was night”
when Jesus forced Judas to leave the Supper (Jn 13:30), picturing the outer darkness (Mt 22:11-14). The outer darkness
is exclusion from the Lord’s Supper, the messianic feast. Judas was no more a believer at the Supper than Jesus was a
Page 44                                                                                             Rewards are Eternal

serpent on a pole at the cross. Notwithstanding the fact that Judas was an unbeliever, he could serve as a typological tool
to illustrate what would happen to believers who betray the Lord and the betrothal contract. The Lord’s Supper represents
the betrothal when the terms of the contract were worked out. See 4D Forgiveness. Those believers who abuse this Supper
are subject to temporal and misthological death. They will be condemned, correlatively, along with the world (1Cor
11:27-32). For discussion of this text as it relates to the Principle of Correlativity (POC), see Darkness. Being conceived
rather than born would have been better for them than to become believers who betray the Lord (cp. Mt 26:24).
                      Excursus on Dualisms of Eternal Life
                                                  Three Views of Aionios
     As part of my defense of the eternal nature of rewards, I argue in Darkness that aionios always refers to eternity
within the NT. Those rejecting the eternal nature of rewards will argue, contrastively, that when aionios refers to rewards,
it is limited to an age. Dillow takes a mediating position in that he does not believe that aionios necessarily refers to
eternity; nevertheless, like myself, he believes that rewards are eternal. Thus three basic views are available for aionios
within the kingdom camp.
                              Illustration 28. Three Options Regarding Aionios in the NT
               Chitwood                                    Dillow                                   Cauley
 Not always eternal                        Not always eternal                       Always Eternal
 Not eternal in reference to rewards       Eternal in reference to rewards          Eternal in reference to rewards

      In limiting the maggots and fire of Mk 9:48 to the millennial age in Destiny, Dillow initially seems to be limiting the
reference to Gehenna in Mk 9:47, with which the maggots and fire are linked, to the millennial age, thereby denying the
eternality of Gehenna in this passage (pp. 876-877). His argument is not entirely consistent, however, at least in the
manner in which he originally states it here, in that he rightly acknowledges that passages which warn believers of
possibly suffering a Gehenna fate (e.g., Mt 5:22,29; 18:8-9; Lk 12:4) would describe eternal results: “When Jesus applies
the warnings regarding gehenna to those who are justified, he speaks of a postmortem judgment with eternal
consequences which befalls faithless, but eternally saved, believers (i.e., the Judgment Seat of Christ)” (italics his,
p. 878, cp. 870-871). Precisely. Obviously, this Markan passage is parallel to the Matthean passages (p. 880). Therefore,
on the one hand, Dillow seems to deny that Gehenna is referencing eternity, while yet on the other hand he affirms that
it is referring eternity, in the same passage no less. The inconsistency is more apparent than real, nonetheless, in that he
appropriately concludes: “The contexts of these warnings require us to believe that Jesus invests this [temporal] maggot
infested burning refuse heap with eschatological significance. It refers to an ‘eternal’ fire which involves forfeiture
of honor and greatness in the future kingdom of heaven” (pp. 880-881). I concur. Yet I would allow a dualistic
soteriological applicability to unbelievers.
                                                   Duality of Gehenna
                                  Illustration 29. Temporal-Eternal-Eternal Gehenna




     As explained in Darkness, in regard to the above illustration, I find a dualistic soteric-mistholic understanding of
Gehenna to be plausible. Indeed, the temporal event in A.D. 70 telescopically may foresee both eternal possibilities. In
that discussion, I anticipated a temporal-eternal-eternal perspective, as seen in the illustration. The temporal event in
A.D. 70 could anticipate the soteriological and misthological counterparts. Accommodating Dillow’s current temporal-
millennial-eternal view of Gehenna is easy.
Page 46                                                                                               Rewards are Eternal

                                 Illustration 30. Temporal-Millennial-Eternal Gehenna




     Dillow argues that the background for Gehenna in Is 66:24 is exclusively millennial in scope, and he seeks to rule
out any reference to eternity in Is 66:22 (pp. 875-876). Nonetheless, he acknowledges that Jesus gives Gehenna an eternal
misthological application (p. 878). I find it more reasonable to conclude, therefore, that Isaiah telescopically includes the
eternal application as well. Apparently, Dillow forgot that he had conceded this very point: “Isaiah telescoped them [the
millennial reign and eternal state] together (cf. Isaiah 65:17-25)” (p. 114, n. 380)! The more consistent conclusion, then,
is that the Gehenna dualism in Isaiah is millennial-eternal. Jesus expands it to the temporal event, rising on the immediate
horizon in A.D. 70. In other words, Isaiah’s millennial-eternal perspective of Gehenna is expanded to a temporal-
millennial-eternal perspective by Jesus.
     Regardless as to the precise nature of the expansion, what is important to note for the present study, which is a
defense of the eternal nature of rewards, is that even though Dillow resists simply taking aionios as referring to eternity
in the Gehenna verses, he nevertheless admits that Gehenna has eternal results in these NT texts as they pertain to
misthology. In acknowledging the eternal misthological scope of Gehenna, he is being consistent with his argument that
rewards are eternal.
     The backbone of his argument in this section, for maintaining that rewards are eternal, is based on the typical appeal
to Mt 25:41,46, in which he makes the standard assumption that the eternal soteriological fate of the unregenerate is
contrasted with the eternal misthological reward of the regenerate. Chitwood, however, has made the counterargument
that rewards are not eternal, supposedly proven by Rev 21:4, so that Mt 25:41,46 is not making a soteriological-
misthological contrast. Chitwood short-circuits Dillow’s whole argument. Dillow’s entire argument concerning Gehenna
having eternal misthological implications fails if Chitwood’s interpretation of Rev 21:4 stands, and Dillow is in no
position to refute Chitwood in this regard since Dillow shares Chitwood’s mistaken impression that Rev 21:4 rules out
all forms of death in the eternal state. The present study, however, has demonstrated that both Chitwood and Dillow err
in thinking that Rev 21:4 rules out all forms of death in the eternal state. Ironically, in doing so, the present study has
validated Dillow’s argument that Gehenna has eternal misthological implications.
     Even though Dillow and I agree that Gehenna has eternal misthological applicability to believers, so that his
arguments against aionios meaning eternal in regard to such texts should be qualified to mean that aionios is inclusively
rather than exclusively referencing eternity in these NT Gehenna passages, the previous chart is still correct, for other
reasons, in listing Dillow as not holding an exclusively eternal understanding of aionios in the NT. He holds that aionios
has an exclusively temporal meaning in Rom 16:25-26: “According to the logic of those who require that aionios always
means ‘endless,’ this passage teaches that ages that are ‘past’ are, in fact, ‘endless.’ That is an absurdity! The NASB
correctly renders it ‘long ages past’ not ‘eternal ages past’” (p. 877, n. 2915). Really? An absurdity? Not hardly!
                                         Illustration 31. Three forms of Eternity
Excursus on Dualisms of Eternal Life                                                                                      Page 47

     As explained in Darkness, in connection with this chart regarding the three forms of eternity, this passage in Romans
may be understood simply as referring to eternity past (an expanse of time extending eternally backwards) rather than to
a finite period of time in eternity past. Suffice it to say that Dillow and I disagree regarding aionios in this particular
passage, which is rather trite to the present discussion, since we are agreed that as applied to rewards, aionios refers to
eternity.
                                                      Plural Aion
     Rather than unnecessarily embroil myself in discussions regarding aionios herein, I have used a premise conceded
by Chitwood: Plural aion necessarily refers to that which is eternal. Craig completely misstates the case when he
erroneously states: “Aion always means a limited period of time, an age, with clearly cut and definitely located termini.”67
Nonsense. The plural usages of aion in the OT and in Rev 22:5 prove that Craig grossly has overstated his case.
Consequently, using Chitwood’s more accurate premise, I am able to prove, independently of aionios, that rewards are
eternal. Having proven the eternal nature of rewards through the plural usages of aion, I was then justified to apply the
implications of that axiom to aionios. Since rewards are demonstrably eternal, aionios should not be limited to the
millennial age when referring to rewards. Aionios should be understood as encompassing the eternal state in misthological
contexts. The misthological implications of aionios necessarily involve the whole kingdom age and cannot be limited
merely to the millennial aspect of that kingdom age.
                                             Multiple Dualisms of Eternal Life
Simple Dualisms
         Whereas Craig entitled his book, The Dualism of Eternal Life, a more appropriate title would be The Dualisms of
Eternal Life. At least five such dualisms are potentially possible in any given context. For example, in the account of the
rich young ruler, kingdom teachers debate among themselves as to the aspect(s) of eternal life being considered. If the
ruler is an unbeliever misconstruing nature of eternal life, so as to think that the free gift of eternal life is earned, then the
text may be understood properly as showing the impossibility of earning this free gift. A gift-reward dualism would be
in play in that case. Alternatively, if the ruler is a believer legitimately seeking to inherit the kingdom misthologically,
rather than merely enter the kingdom under the guise of inheriting it, then a misthological qualitative-quantitative dualism
would be entailed, as he would be seeking a higher qualitative experience of eternal life than that which he already had
received freely as a gift.68 Some kingdom teachers see the former dualism; some advocate the latter dualism. I allow both
dualisms in that the text seems to have a purposeful ambiguity, suggesting a dual applicability of both perspectives, a
feature not uncommon in the biblical data.
                                   Illustration 32. Five Simple Dualisms of Eternal Life
                 Gift/Reward      Quality/Quantity       Life/Death       Temporal/Eschatological         Millennial/Eternal
     Lk 18:18      If soteric       If mistholic         If mistholic               ?                            NA

        The double entre surrounding the young ruler’s inquiry, as to what he must do to inherit eternal life (Lk 18:18), is
particularly suggestive in the Lukan account, in that earlier a lawyer tested Jesus in asking Him the same question
(Lk 10:25). In that context, Jesus denounces the Pharisaical attempt to justify themselves (Lk 10:29), just as He does in
the latter context (Lk 18:14). Limiting the inquiry and concern as to what is necessary to inherit the kingdom to believers
who are seeking misthological entrance is highly problematic given such apparent soteric concerns within these
overlapping layer-ruler texts. A more plausible solution thus is to pose that Luke expects the discussion to be seen at two
levels: soteric and mistholic.
        Moreover, if the misthological motif is included, which I suspect is what Luke intends, then it would be appropriate
to note that the failure to obtain life at that higher level is a form of death—misthological death. Eternal life, at the
misthological level, includes the possibility of misthological death. A misthological life-death dualism would be
reasonable if the ruler is a believer who walks away from the genuine possibility of earning misthological life. Therefore,
taking the passage as both soteric and mistholic in its scope allows us to apply three out of five dualisms regarding eternal
life to this particular text.
        To be sure, some kingdom teachers will suggest that the young ruler was a believer seeking an abundant kingdom
experience in the present. If so, a temporal-eschatological dualism between eternal life as a present possession versus

present experience might be involved. Regardless as to whether this particular text poses this particular dualism, other

of eternal life on the chart can be dualistically dual: soteric-mistholic or mistholic-mistholic.
        The fifth dualistic category on the chart is mistakenly taken by those posing an age-long limitation on the duration
of rewards to indicate that the reward of eternal life is millennial while the gift of eternal life is eternal. They impose a
false soteric-mistholic, millennial-eternal dualism. This is not to say that a millennial-eternal dualism does not exist in
any context. Far from it! The resurrection to eternal life in Dan 12:3 is certainly millennial but encompasses the eternal
rulership denoted in Rev 22:5. Mistholically, the dualism in this duo is mistholic-mistholic. The misthological experience
of eternal life bestowed during the millennial age extends and expands into the eternal state for those faithful believers
who have been so rewarded. Nevertheless, on the dualism chart I have marked this final column as NA as it applies to
Lk 18:18. The young ruler was not looking for a temporary millennial life at the misthological level, contrary to age-long
advocates, nor was he supposing a dualism between the millennial and eternal phases of eternal life. This latter dualistic
differentiation, which is a more sophisticated discernment, awaits John’s Revelation. Isaiah, for example, is telescopic
and sees the misthological mountain peaks of both the millennial and eternal periods, but this implicit dualism is not
explicitly distinguished until Revelation.
Compound Dualisms
       For the following analysis, the five simple dualisms of eternal life from Illustration 32 are plotted on the quality-
quantity graph of eternal life, which is inclusive of misthological death, with a break down between the three areas of
time: temporal (T), millennial (M), and eternal (E). For sake of completeness, soteriological death is also included at the
bottom of the chart in Row 4, though the range for eternal life would actually not include that dimension. The dotted
lines breaking the graph up into quadrants are in red.69

                        Illustration 34. Quadrants for Eternal Life as Applied to an Overcomer




       Overcomers will achieve E1, as shown by the conclusion of the purple arrow on the chart as applied to them. They

experiences in the experiential quadrants of the temporal arena, namely T1 and T2. Indeed, they probably will struggle
with misthological death a few times along the way and may even pass back into that temporal T2 quadrant a few times
during their earthly sojourn and thus, surprisingly, pass back over the misthological threshold in a reverse direction in
the T column. Generally, though, it is impossible to cross over either a horizontal or vertical threshold in a reverse
direction.70 The T1-T2 threshold is the singular exception to this rule.
       The three horizontal thresholds are broken up into nine sections by the two vertical thresholds. The T1-T2 threshold
is volatile. The purple line is expressed in waves going above and below this one threshold. How well believers do in
regard to that threshold determines whether they will experience misthological life or misthological death in both the
millennial and eternal ages. Confusing the quadrants throws many biblical interpreters into a quandary, as proven by how
many interpret the following passage.

professing believer was never saved in the first place: He never crossed the soteriological threshold; he never received

        Kingdom teachers know better. Believers can pass back and forth from death into life temporally at the experiential

in the world in how one views the text. One must be able to identify the proper strata. Placing the proper strata in the
proper time frame allows us to make an accurate identification of the quadrants involved. Everyone has a hermeneutical
