        An overcomer can struggle with loving the brethren. Some brethren are not easy to love, or sometimes the cost
involved in loving the brethren is more than what we are willing to pay. Regardless, loving the brethren is not free, as
John immediately goes on to illustrate: “We know love by this, that He laid down His life for us; and we ought to lay
down our lives for the brethren. But whoever has the world’s goods, and beholds his brother in need and closes his heart
against him, how does the love of God abide in him?” (1Jn 3:16-17) In this context laying down your life and giving up
your goods may be required to love the brethren biblically. Obviously, the scope of eternal life under consideration is not
free and is not conditioned on simply believing in Jesus for eternal life. Misidentifying this quadrant of eternal life as T3
is inexcusable. John is not taking about a form of eternal life that is freely given to those who believe but a costly degree
of eternal life earned by those believers who merit it. This T1 dimension of eternal life is very rewarding, setting both
the level and trajectory of one’s reward in the millennial and eternal quadrants. Naturally, the purple line for the
overcomer can never go below the R1-R2 threshold in the M and E columns.
                          Illustration 35. Quadrants for Eternal Life as Applied to a Subcomer




       Overcomers might occasionally rise above the experiential threshold and love the brethren, but most of their lives
are spent below the threshold. No matter how dismal their performance, however, their purple line can never go below
the R2-R3 threshold in any column. They can never experience soteriological death or literally be cast into the Lake of
Fire. You cannot cross the horizontal thresholds after death “inasmuch as it is appointed for men to die once and after
this comes judgment” (Heb 9:27). There is one death and one judgment. Therefore, the results are permanent.
       What about the vertical thresholds? Might one cross over them? I do not believe so, certainly not so as to change
the results of the judgment. However, one might argue that crossing over the timelines might be possible as long as this
time travel does not violate this misthological law or other such laws. What might such time travel look like? We will
examine that possibility in the next excursus.
                                    Excursus on Time Travel
       Time travel is popular in science fiction, and Stearman has posed that the Bible demonstrates that time travel
actually occurs and that time change is possible as a result. In Darkness, I support the proposition that experiential time
travel is possible but reject the theory that spatial time travel is possible. In other words, as it pertains to Stearman’s
proposal, I believe the prophets were able to experience the past or future, but in contradistinction to Stearman, I do not
believe that they traveled to the past or future as places in time as such. Therefore, they would not have been able to
interact with the past or future, which would involve their introducing changes in the past or future. Thus, as this pertains
to the present discussion, I reject the notion that one can travel either backward or forward so as to change events which
take place in the past or future time.
                                                     Simple Timelines
       Normally, this type of discussion focuses on problems related to traveling to the past.71 Stearman seeks to minimize
problems with time travel by suggesting that the biblical examples of time travel of the prophets is always to the future.
Even if this premise were granted, though, I am still skeptical that changing the future time line as to what actually occurs
is possible. But let us begin with the classical problem.
                                             Illustration 36. Simple Timeline




        Normally we think of time as simply moving forward, as illustrated by the timeline. I posed a classic question to
a friend of mine who had read Stearman’s Time Travelers and who, like Stearman, believes that time travel is possible:
“What would happen if a person traveled into the past and killed his grandfather before his grandfather had a change to
sire his father?” He responded that the person who killed his grandfather would never have existed in the case. Simple to
say, difficult to believe! How can a person who never existed kill anyone? The law of non-contradiction nullifies such a
proposal.
                                 Illustration 37. Time Travel into Past on One Timeline




       For sake of illustration, suppose that Curley is the son of Sam and grandson of Hitler. Curley invents a time
machine and goes back in time and kills his grandfather, Hitler, before Hitler becomes the father of Sam. Curley would
never have been born, at least not as the grandson of Hitler and son of Sam in that timeline.72 Curley jumps over Sam
and goes back into the past and kills Hitler. Initially, Curley existed along timeline #1 section B (TL1B). However, after
killing Hitler Curley no longer exists along the same section of time. Curley both exists and does not exist in the same
manner of speaking at the same period of time. Curley is both existent and non-existent in the same way at the same time.
The law of non-contradiction has been violated. Traveling into the past along a single, mutable time is thus not possible.
Changing the past causes impossible contradictions. 73

71
   For the basic approach being taken herein for traveling to the past, see Phil for Humanity, Time Travel Theories.
Available at http://www.philforhumanity.com/Time_Travel_Theories.html. Accessed 12/4/2014.
72
   If Curley were to exist in this timeline after killing his grandfather so as to prevent Curley being born as Curley-
grandson-of-Hitler, it would have to be as another moral entity who holds the same ontological identity even though such
supposedly incidentals details, as being the grandson of Hitler and son of Sam, would have been changed. Even if one
were willing to allow such ontological-incidental distinctions, one still violates the law of non-contradiction and creates
a time paradox unless one poses a new, derivative timeline. Rather than pursue this line of discussion with my friend, I
merely remarked that I would object to his proposal that a non-existent person could kill and not be held accountable for
his actions in a postmortem judgment.
73
   As already mentioned previously, for theological and philosophical reasons, I personally do not believe that one can
Page 52                                                                                                Rewards are Eternal

                                 Illustration 38. Time Travel into Past on One Timeline




       Supposedly, changing the future is not so objectionable philosophically. But is changing the future really any
easier? In this case, suppose that Curley is the grandfather of Sam II, who is the father of Hitler II. Curley travels into the
future to kill Sam II before he becomes the father of Hitler II. So Hitler II never exists, after Curley kills Sam II. The
problem is that, for those who hold the spatial model of time, Hitler II actually exists in the future before Curley kills
Sam II.
       For example, Stearman seems to pose that Moses and Elijah travel in time to appear on the Mount of
Transfiguration and interact with Jesus. But if that is the case, they had to travel to that place in time. Stearman steers
clear of dealing with the technical problems this would pose, however. Nonetheless, that place in time actually had to
exist before they could travel to it in such a model. They could not travel to a non-existent place. Thus spatial time travel

cross over the vertical thresholds in the quadrants for eternal life in a reverse direction. In other words, I do not believe
that spatial time travel is possible, especially along mutable timelines. Admittedly some teachers, such as Stearman,
believe that the biblical and scientific data is sufficient to conclude that time travel is possible. The only way I see to
allow this theoretical possibility is to harmonize it with my Principle of Quadrafication (to coin a term) as being
developed herein. The changes made by the time travel would have to be such so that they could not conflict with the
Principle of Quadrafication for all moral entities involved. Such moral agents would come to the same moral end in any
possible world that God may allow to be actualized by time travel. In other words, the results of the time travel could not
be such so that in one scenario you are a believer, and in the modified scenario you are not a believer. Likewise, you
could not die as an overcomer in the original sequence and then be a subcomer in the modified sequence. If you die an
unbeliever in TL1, then you would die an unbeliever in TL2. If you end up as an overcomer in TL1, then you would end
up as an overcomer in TL2. If you are a subcomer in TL1, then you will be in TL2 as well.
       In making such a conjecture, I am thinking in terms of transworld essence and results. Compare Alvin Plantinga,
God, Freedom, and Evil (Grand Rapids: Eerdmans Publishing Company, 1974), 45-53. To modify and expand upon
Plantinga’s example regarding Curley as his example, let Curley be the moral agent whose moral essence is defined by
ME. Further suppose that in any possible world (W) this ontological moral entity (ME) would believe in Christ for eternal
life but fail to overcome. Some of the details pertinent to ME would be “trivially essential” (p. 49), but some of ME’s
traits would be what I would term morally essential to ME’s nature so that such a person would fail to overcome in any
possible world that God could have actualized (pp. 48-49) in TL1 or allow to be actualized by TL2. The details may vary
from one possible world (W) to another, or in the case of time travel from one possible time stream/string (TS) to another,
but ME suffers from transworld moral failure in all such worlds (p. 52). So, even if one could go back in time and change
some of the details of history regarding Curley’s failure, one would not be able to change the course of history regarding
Curley’s outcome. Even if one could divert the path of the time stream, one would not be able to change the final
destination of this stream of time. More likely, though, one could only throw a pebble into the stream and create a minor
ripple in time but not divert its course.
       This proposition assumes, quite speculatively, that one would be able to travel spatially back in time. Stearman
thinks that the biblical data regarding the prophets and apostles supporting time travel is always “forward (never
backward)” (p. 42), which would be more consistent with my position that vertical thresholds are only traversed in the
forward direction. Nevertheless, in posing that the prophets and apostles make a return trip from the future, Stearman
nevertheless opens up the possibility of traveling back in time. After all, he states that they had “access to the entire
timeline” (p. 34). Also, he allows that Satan and his fallen forces can travel short distances into the past as well as the
future (p. 79), which is again highly problematic. Why would they not have access to the entire timeline if merely being
outside our timeline grants access to any point on our time line? Stearman is inconsistent when he says, “Having lost his
position in heaven, he no longer commands a view of the timeline. He cannot access the future” (p. 186). Even though
my modified implementation of Plantinga’s argument might at least make Stearman’s conjecture a little more palatable
theoretically and morally, his model still fails to address numerous problems. If merely being in heaven grants unlimited
access to the future of our timeline, then why do heavenly angels not know with Christ will return (Mk 13:32). Why do
saints in Heaven ask the Lord, “How long?” (Rev 6:10). Gary Stearman, Time Travelers of the Bible: How Hebrew
Prophets Shattered the Barriers of Time-Space (Crane, MO: Defender, 2011).
Excursus on Time Travel                                                                                            Page 53
                                                                                         74
seems to pose as much a problem for traveling into the future as traveling into the past.
       To further complicate the matter, consider the biblical implications of Moses and Elijah traveling forward in time,
which is the model Stearman advocates for the prophets. This would mean that the Bible, as it was originally written in
TL1 before the jump occurred, had no record of Moses and Elijah appearing to Christ on the Mount of Transfiguration.
Granted, this objection is difficult to fathom. Yet, just as that place in time already had to exist before Moses and Elijah
traveled to it, the subsequent timeline already had to exist as well, which would include the writing of the NT. Therefore,
the Bible was first written one way in TL1 and then written another because of the time jump.
       Due to the complexity of the above objection, a less sophisticated remonstration to their traveling forward in time
may be more readily presented. Evidently, given Stearman’s correct perception that the current Moses and Elijah would
have been with Abraham in Paradise at the time that the Moses and Elijah from the past appeared to Jesus on the Mount
of Transfiguration, one is immediately left to ask two common-sense question: “Even if spatial time travel is possible,
why would Moses and Elijah appear with Jesus from the past rather than the present? Why not simply leave Paradise in
a concurrent time frame to appear with Jesus?”
                                                 Concurrent Time Lines
       The proposition of concurrent timelines certainly is supported by the biblical data. While the rich man was
suffering in Hades, across the chasm from Abraham in Paradise, he knew that his brothers were concurrently still alive
on Earth. He wanted someone sent before it was too late, before they died. Past time would have been past time for both
Earth and Paradise. Future time would have been future for both dimensions as well. Likewise, when Satan is cast out of
Heaven to Earth during the tribulation, his expulsion from Heaven will happen at the same time he is cast to Earth. The
events are concurrent even though they had into two different dimensions. Also, the proposition that one day of Earth’s
time equates to a thousand years in the heavenly dimension supports a time dilation model in which the time rate is
different in the different dimensions, not a time travel model in which the time reference is different. In other words, a
present event in one dimension is not past or future relative to the other domain. Instead, an event is synchronously either
past, present, or future in both dimensions.
                                 Illustration 39. Metaphysical and Physical Timelines




      Suppose roughly 2000 years of Earth time passes between the time when the rich man made his request from Hades
and the time when Satan is cast out of Heaven. Furthers, suppose that the passage of time in both metaphysical dimensions
runs at the same rate relative to Earth, so that one Earth day is equal to a thousand years metaphysical time. After all,
Hades was adjacent to Paradise at the time and Abraham and the rich man were able to carry on a conversation across
the chasm which separated the two, so it is reasonable to suppose that time passes at the same rate in both of those
metaphysical dimensions. If this is the case, then one Earth year would be equivalent to 365,000 metaphysical years! A
staggering 730,000,000 years of metaphysical time will have elapsed on metaphysical TL1 during the corresponding
2000 years of Earth time on physical TL2 between these two concurrent events. This model is biblically, philosophically,
and scenically attractive. I am certainly open to time dilation models and time bubbles. As Stearman correctly points out,
though, we do not need this “fixed ratio” to affirm that God experiences time differently than we do (p. 418).
      In near death experiences, many people see their lives flash before their eyes. Stearman offers the interesting

74
 Alternatively, if Spearman is saying that Jesus and the apostles traveled forward in time at the Mount of Transfiguration,
which may be Spearman’s intent (pp. 427-436), then my objection against his position is weakened for that proposition.
Nonetheless, even this limited place-in-time model still seems susceptible to a time paradox problem.
Page 54                                                                                              Rewards are Eternal

proposal that their spirit is experiencing time in another dimension, which is a reasonable proposal. However, such a
hypothesis does not need to resort to time travel in terms of traveling to the past or future. Rather, their spirit may be
experiencing time at a greatly accelerated rate compared to their bodies, a rate that is 365,000 times faster as a matter of
fact, in another dimension. The model I propose, then, is that metaphysical time and physical time run parallel to one
another in the multiverse. Interaction between the two dimensions therefore occurs concurrently.
                                                       Split Timelines
        A word of clarification needs to be added at this point. In the world at large, those who pose that time travel is
possible normally pose that if a change was made in time as a result of that jump back in time, then a new timeline would
be created at that point. Those timelines would be parallel, but jumping between the two timelines would not be possible.
You would not be able to return to your original timeline. For example, if Curley could actually travel back in time and
kill his grandfather Hitler, this action would create a new parallel time line. Curley would have killed Hitler in TL2, not
TL1. Hitler could not be killed in TL1 before he fathered Sam who fathered Curley. Therefore, Hitler’s death at the hand
of Curley had to occur in the new timeline.
                                 Illustration 40. Metaphysical and Physical Timelines




       To restate this matter, Curley does not actually kill Hitler in TL1 because Hitler had to live on in TL1 so that
Curley could be born. Curley kills Hitler in TL2, and then Curley continues on in TL2, as a time traveler who was born
in TL1. At the point of administering this death, Curley created a new TL2 and entered that timeline himself. Curley has
no choice in the matter. He would now experience future history along TL2.
       If Curley were to get back into his time machine and move to the future, it would be a future along TL2. He can
never return to TL1B or TL1C. Even if he were to get back into his time machine and travel into the past, it would be to
TL1A. But when he moves forward and reaches the split in the timelines, he would move along TL2. Curley would never
experience TL1B again and never experience TL1C at all. If Curley were married in TL1B, then his wife would have
seen him get into the time machine at the end of TL1B to disappear into the past, but she would never see him return.
She would live out the rest of her life in TL1 without him.
       As to the new timeline, there is no guarantee that he will even meet her, much less be married to her, in TL2. His
killing Hitler might have altered that timeline so much that even if he were to travel into the future in order to meet the
women who had been his wife in TL1, she would not even know him in TL2, assuming she even exists in this new
timeline. The features of this model are that it avoids time paradoxes and implements quantum physics and M-theory.
Nevertheless, theologically, I find the split-timeline model just as appalling as the single-timeline model for a variety of
reasons.
       To illustrate my theological misgivings about this split-timeline model, suppose Moses and Elijah traveled from
the future of TL1 to visit Jesus on the Mount of Transfiguration. Doing so would create a time paradox as to what is
recorded in the Bible. Undoubtedly, their traveling into the past would cause a split in the timeline. Since they did not
originally appear to Jesus on the Mount of Transfiguration, the Bible in TL1 has no record of any such event. The law of
non-contradiction prevents their visit from occurring in TL1. The transfiguration cannot (a) have occurred and (B) not
have occurred at the same point in time along the same timeline. Thus the transfiguration does not occur in TL1 but does
occur in TL2.
          As a result, the Bible that those who live by in TL1 does not have a transfiguration. The Bible that we live by in
TL2 has the transfiguration. A mutable timeline means a mutable Bible. Furthermore, Moses and Elijah left TL1 when
they traveled into the past for the transfiguration. They cannot return either to TL1B or TL1C. For those living in TL1,
Excursus on Time Travel                                                                                               Page 55
there will be no Moses and Elijah in Heaven in TL1C. Like Curley in the above example, when Moses and Elijah left
TL1 and went back into the past, they split the timeline so that now they can only live in TL2. Of course, everyone else
that they left on TL1 will continue to live on TL1. Moreover, Peter and the rest of the apostles are on both timelines.
They died on both timelines. Jesus also died on both timelines. Yes, Jesus died twice according to this model! Naturally,
I find such a proposal revolting. According to the Bible, Jesus only died once: “Christ, having been raised from the dead,
is never to die again; death no longer is master over Him” (Rom 6:9). I assume that the Bible in both timelines has this
statement. In any case, the Bible we have in our timeline states this, and I am sticking with it. What is more, it also affirms
that we die only once: “it is appointed for men to die once and after this comes judgment” (Heb 9:27). This verse not
only rules out reincarnation (multiple deaths along one timeline), it rules out split time travel (multiple deaths along
multiple timelines). The theory of split time travel along multiple timelines dies at the hands of death.
                                                  Immutable Time Travel
         The above discussion has shown the problem of changing time and therefore is limited to mutable timelines.
Some pose that time travel is possible along an immutable timeline. According to this immutable model, we can travel
backward or forward in time and observe it in a phantom form, but we could not interact with the past or future time and
thereby change it. Think of it as visiting the past in a movie like It’s a Wonderful Life. As you will recall, Clarence
Odbody is the guardian angel who takes George Bailey (played by James Stewart) into the past and allows him to view
the past as a phantom. In this movie, they visit the potential past, what it would look like if George Bailey never had been
born. The people in the past could not see George.75
         I hold this immutable view, except I believe that we will be able to visit the actual past, not merely the potential
past. So we might think of A Christmas Carol by Charles Dickens, in which the Ghost of Christmas Past is an angelic
spirit who shows Scrooge scenes from his actual past. How about the future? The Ghost of Christmas Future shows
Scrooge his potential future, which is fine, but I believe that time travel to the actual future is also possible. The prophets
saw the actual future. In this respect, I agree with Stearman. However, in contradistinction to Stearman, I would insist
that they only visited the future in phantom form. Moses and Elijah did not travel to the future so as to interact with Jesus
on the Mount of Transfiguration and thereby change the future. Time paradoxes or split timelines are impossible;
therefore, timelines are immutable. Time travel along an immutable timeline certainly would be possible, however.

immutable timeline outside that plane would be beyond the consideration of those two dimensional graphs. Illustration

              Excursus for More Detailed Analysis of Craig
                                                         Introduction
        After the Kingdom conference where I presented my case for concluding that rewards are eternal, I was asked to
interact with S.S. Craig’s Dualism of Eternal Life and later with Cindy Zeigler’s The Full Scope of Salvation. Craig’s
book is a scholarly apologetic for limiting rewards to the millennial kingdom. Zeigler writes from this same age-long
limitation, but at the popular level, using Adam’s being cast from the tree of life in Gen 3:22 as her principle text to
illustrate the millennial limitation of the reward. Craig’s older and more thorough treatment has received preliminary
interaction in the preceding excursus. The present excursus will provide a more detailed focus on Craig’s position. The
following excursus will then interact with Zeigler’s presentation at the popular level at which it was written, using a
Christmas tree to illustrate the point, since the Christmas season is upon us as I prepare for a follow-up presentation for
the conference.
        Chapter 2 of Craig’s book bears the same title as his book and thus presents the heart of his argument. I am strongly
in agreement with his foundational premise (pp. 58, 62, 63, 184): Eternal life is presented dualistically in Scripture as
both a gift and a reward. The gift dimension is foundational and separable from the higher reward level. Although all
believers irrevocable have the free gift of eternal life at the lower level, they may fail to win eternal life as a prize at the
higher level. Craig’s foundational perspective is splendid!
        The primary endeavor of Craig’s book, however, is to prove that eternal life at the higher level “is synonymous
with the coming Messianic Kingdom” and therefore limited to 1,000 years so that aionios must be understood as referring
to millennial life rather than eternal life when referring to life at that higher stratum (p. 65). As he notes, “Aionios is
derived from the noun aion” (p. 70). He concludes, rather prematurely, as already mentioned in the previous excursus,
that “aion always means a limited period of time” (p. 71). He then assumes that this justifies his assumption that the
adjective must likewise refer to a limited period of time so that such “lines of evidence demonstrate conclusively that the
Greek aionios cannot be translated by the English eternal” (p. 72, italics his). His claim is as bold as it is premature.
                                                 Eternity for Singularities
       In the very next paragraph, he proceeds to refute himself: “Whenever it [aionios] is found in association with the
names of Deity it makes good sense to render it by ‘eternal,’ but as already noted that is not its proper meaning” (p. 73).
What?!? Even if eternity is composed of plural ages, which indeed may be the case, his admission that rendering aionios
as eternal “makes good sense” makes absolute nonsense of his statement that aionios “cannot be translated” as eternal.
       His error in semantics is followed closely by an error in judgment: “Surely it is manifest that believers cannot be
eternally damned and eternally saved. The two ideas are mutually exclusive” (pp. 73-74). Actually, the two concepts are
not mutually exclusive. Believers can be eternally damned misthologically yet eternally saved soteriologically. This dual
possibility alludes Craig because he believes that misthological damnation involves burning in the literal flames of
“Hadean shame” (p. 82). Yet if misthological shame does not involve burning in literal Hadean flame, then his assumption
of mutual exclusivity between damnation and salvation at the mistholic-soteric level is bedeviled. Evidently, he would
allow that believers can be damned for a 1,000 years while possessing eternal life, suffering age-long destruction during
that time (p. 91). But if damnation/destruction and salvation are not mutually exclusive for that age, why assume that
they are inherently exclusive in the following age, or in the age after that one, or even in the age after that one, etc.?
       As to this transition between the millennial age and its following age, Craig disappointingly comes to conclude:
At the end of that age [1] the judgment will be lifted after that [2] the carnal believer has gotten right with God, and [3] he
will then enter the Kingdom in its really eternal state” (p. 85). The implication of his proposition is that misthological
judgment will be lifted so that rewards will no longer matter. If the results of judgment are lifted in regard to the carnal,
then apparently rewards are also lifted in regards to the faithful. The unfaithful and faithful will be treated as equals.76
The net effect of his thesis is that rewards do not really matter in eternity. My primary reason for writing this book is to
refute this mistaken perspective. My thesis is that rewards really matter, both in the millennial age and the eternal ages.
        Embolden by what he believes to be sound deductions from his research, Craig restates his position more
forcefully: “Whenever we find the adjective aionios associated with nouns other than those which are descriptive of
God, or His attributes, we are to interpret it as confined to the age to come, and as falling entirely within the limits of

76
  Craig is certainly correct that in 1Tim 6:12 Paul is not urging Timothy to contend for the free gift of eternal life which
he already possessed, but Craig is wrong to conclude that “eternal life in this passage cannot refer to the eternal state
beyond the Millennium, for the reason that absolutely all believers are sure of that” (p. 87). Not necessarily. Craig already
has correctly distinguished the free part of eternal life from the costly part. One may simply pose that all believers are
not assured of the costly part if rewards extend into eternity. Basically, Craig has assumed the point he is supposed to be
proving in making this statement.
Page 60                                                                                                  Rewards are Eternal

the Millennial Kingdom” (p. 86). Really? In that case the following translation would be correct: “For God so loved the
world that He gave His only begotten Son, that whoever believes in Him will not perish but have millennial life” (Jn 3:16).
But how does merely having millennial life keep you from perishing eternally? YLT’s attempt to make this an age-during
life is completely unpersuasive. Evidently sensing the nonsensicalness of his position, Craig eventually amends it: “The
words eternal, everlasting and for ever, except they apply to God and the free gift of eternal life, ought to be banished
from our Bibles” (italics his, 133). Contrary to what he had said earlier (p. 86) and will again say later (p. 153), at least
at this particular point in his book he at last concedes that the word eternal is appropriately applied to the gift aspect of
this life! Whereas before and later he limits the notion of eternality to God or his attributes, we now learn, forty-seven
pages later, that his earlier statement, upon which he built much of his case, is not accurate. And he even ignores his own
concession when he gets to his subsequent conclusion as to what eternal life means in Dan 12:2 (p. 153)! Eternality is
not limited to deity! Eternal life is eternal also, at least the free aspect of it, as he himself had conceded. So his objections
to rendering aionios as eternal are not really lexical after all but must be based on contextual and theological factors.
                                                          Plurality
        At the same time, Craig immediately adds, “Let it not be overlooked, however, that so far we have not touched
the discussion of the terms olam and aion in their plural and duplicated forms. These carry us far beyond the limit
of the age to come” (p. 133). Amazing! Craig has not even dealt with the plural forms of the critical lexical terms that
would spill over into the realm of eternity, and yet he expects us to believe that rewards are not eternal without having
first examined this crucial piece of evidence! Would prudence not dictate that the verdict as to whether rewards are eternal
be suspended until one has determined if the plural forms of aion, aionios, olam, or alam are ever used in relation to
rewards!?!77 This is the Achilles’ heel of his entire argument, yet he completely glosses over its import. He acknowledges
that the compound affirmation of the ages of the age(s) refers to forever and ever and even includes Rev 22:5 in his
citations. Yet this verse says that some people will rule forever. Would it not behoove him to at least discuss, in such a
crucial context, whether these eternal rulers are composed of faithful believers or not? He acknowledges that the torment
of the damned expressed by ages of the ages in Rev 14:11 is forever and ever as well. The only thing, therefore, necessary
to derail his entire age-long limitation regarding the nature of rewards is to demonstrate that such phraseology is applied
to rewards. The present study has done just that.
        A few pages later, he correctly deduces that Dan 12:2 is dealing exclusively with believers in a premillennial
judgment (pp. 140-141). Very, very good. However, according to about twenty six translations that I consulted, this
means that the faithful believers would be raised to the reward of eternal/everlasting life while the unfaithful believers
would be resurrected to eternal/everlasting shame. Only the YLT gives a translation that supports Craig’s interpretation
of age-long versus age-long shame. What Craig fails to notice, though, or at least fails to tell his readers, is that in the
very next verse the wise are acknowledged, even by the YLT no less, as shining brightly to the age and for ever. The
eternal nature of the resulting reward is thus confirmed in the very next verse by the addition of ad to olam. This
expression is typically rendered as forever and ever. The eternal nature of the reward is expressly indicated, and Craig
just as clearly has failed to acknowledge it. Therefore, his conclusion is completely erroneous that “the reference in
Dan. 12:2 is confined to the Millennial period” (p. 152). Not true! If he would just read the context, then he would
discover that the millennial results of Dan 12:2 have eternal implications.
        Yet he runs away from both the immediate context of Dan 12 and the broader context of Daniel to jump to other
OT books to show that the word translated shame in Dan 12:2 is always used in a temporal sense elsewhere, so he assumes
that this proves his argument that temporary shame must be intended in Dan 12:2, completely ignoring the immediate
and broader Danielic context. This crucial OT text, which is the first explicit mention of eternal (olam, aionios) life, is
clearly referring to dual positive and negative misthological possibilities in the millennial kingdom, as Craig himself
correctly perceives. His error lies in not giving serious consideration to its context, which confirms that these
misthological results have eternal repercussions.
        He makes a charge that translating these terms as eternal “except when they refer to God and His attributes, and
when the concept of eternity is already in the noun, is nothing short of a monumental disgrace to Christian scholarship.
Of this I am absolutely sure, on historical, philosophical and exegetical grounds,” to which he immediately adds this
challenge: “It would be interesting to find the Christian scholar who has the courage to attempt a concrete denial of this
impeachment” (p. 153). Well, he has found one. The proverbial egg is on his face since he has failed to give the plural
forms the exegetical and contextual analysis they deserve.

77
  Contrary to his statement here, Craig had at least broached the topic of plurality previously: “What the aion was to the
Greeks the olam was to the Hebrews. When longer periods of time are involved the idea is expressed by plural forms,
or by duplication” (p. 103). His argument dies at the hands of plurality, and his failure to deal adequately with the
duplication leads to duplicity.
                  Excursus on the First Misthological Death
                                                       Introduction
       Misthological death started in the Garden of Eden. God had warned Adam that in the day that he ate of the tree of
knowledge he surely would die (Gen 2:17). Adam died that very day. Some have objected that Adam did not immediately
die that very day because the lexical implication of the text includes physical death. The death cannot be a mere spiritual
death. “So Adam was doomed to death,” they would say, “But he did not die suddenly.” Others would allow that Adam
actually died that very day by immediately becoming mortal and beginning a long process of dying as a result of being
separated from the tree of life that very day. Others have added that Adam did die that very day, when that day is viewed
from God’s perspective, wherein one Earth day equals a thousand years in the heavenly dimension. Adam died at the age
of 930, thus within the 1,000 year window of one day (Gen 5:5).
                                                             Zeigler
        Zeigler poses the interesting possibility that Adam died immediately at three levels: spiritually, soulically, and
physically. (1) Adam’s spirit was separated from God immediately, the estrangement being obvious that very day when
Adam hid from God. (2) His soul became corrupted and perverted with the experiential knowledge of good and evil.
(3) His body lost the covering of light. This part of her discussion is commendable, and I would add a technical
clarification. His body became corrupted by a sin nature that now dwelt in his flesh (cp. Rom 7:18). Corruption can be a
form of death (Gal 6:8). So in this sense his soul and body died that very day. Additionally, Adam was created originally
with spiritual life, not eternal life. He lost his spiritual life when his spirit died. Thus the spiritual life which was
transmitted by his spirit to his soul and by his soul on to his body was no longer present. Consequently, his loss of spiritual
life was indeed a loss of life, both for his soul and his body, not only for his spirit.
                                          Illustration 47. Death at Three Levels




       If his spirit is pictured as a battery and his soul as the wire supplying current to his body, which serves as the light
bulb in this analogy, then one could think of the situation as being analogous to an electrical failure: The battery died;
the wire died as a result; and the light bulb died as well. Actually, the battery did not merely die but was damaged in a
short circuit—impossible simply to recharge. The wire was not merely dead but burned. The light bulb did not merely
cease to shine, but the filament within it burned out. Adam would need more than a mere jump start to shine again. Even
a new battery would not be enough for his body to shine. The wiring and bulb had to be repaired.
       He was dead soteriologically at all three levels: spirit, soul, and body. Also, he was dead misthologically at all
three levels. Hence Paul prayed that believers would be saved misthologically at all three levels (1Thess 5:23). The
misthological salvation of one’s soul and body poses no problem, of course. The salvation of one’s soul is easily perceived
to be a misthological issue. Likewise, having a body that shares a higher degree of glory as a co-heir with Christ that is
clothed in white is readily confirmed and need not detain us here. In Darkness, I even argued that the redemption of one’s
body has misthological overtones; therefore, I am appreciative of Zeigler’s approach to this shared perspective on this
matter. Posing the misthological salvation of one’s spirit is more challenging, however. Nevertheless, I did so in
Darkness:

       Paul prays that God may sanctify the believers wholly in order that they may be holistically blameless at
       the Bema. Specifically, he desires that their spirit will be misthologically blameless. To have one’s spirit
       saved from misthological blame would be a misthological salvation of one’s spirit. Conceptually at least,
       one may acknowledge the misthological salvation of one’s spirit. Perhaps one might combine my
       preference for the spirit denoting a soteriological secure ontological core with this misthological view by
Page 62                                                                                                  Rewards are Eternal

         suggesting that Paul desires the misthological salvation of the soteriological core. A believer’s regenerate
         spirit will be soteriologically blameless and ontologically sinless, but the misthological blamelessness of
         the believer’s spirit will be contingent upon the purity of the believer’s walk. Even those believers who do
         not walk in the light will find that they themselves are saved soteriologically (1Cor 3:15) because of their
         regenerate spirit, but they will not find that their regenerate spirit is saved misthologically.

         Simply put, the Principle of Stratification applies conceptually to the salvation of one’s spirit, soul, and body.
Whether Zeigler would allow the possibility of a misthological salvation of one’s spirit is not immediately clear to me
from her discussion. In any event, I enjoyed her discussion of the soteriological nature of the salvation of one’s spirit.
Spiritual birth restores spiritual union with God, is conditioned simply on a singular response of faith, and results in our
being made alive together with Christ (p. 19).78 We are completely agreed at that stratum.
         The only issue I have with Zeigler’s presentation is her repeated limitation on the duration of rewards: “This facet
of salvation has millennial verities, not eternal” (p. 12). Eternal glory in 2Cor 4:16 merely means “age-lasting” glory (p.
13). “The ramifications of this judgment will be realized in and throughout the Coming Age, the 1,000 year reign of
Christ when He comes to rule over the earth with His co-heirs. This salvation has nothing to do with eternity, with the
eternal ages” (emphasis hers, 36). “The future 1,000 year Messianic Kingdom is still in time and space, and still has to
do with this present earth. In no way, shape, or form is it part of eternity” (p. 36). As to living forever in Jn 6:51, she
says, “Again the word forever here is the Greek word aion, in the singular use, which means a long period of time!” (p.
44, n. 10, emphasis hers). Her argument in favor of this position is, “This is not speaking of the free gift of eternal life
(i.e., life in the ongoing ages (plural); this is dealing with eating bread by the sweat of your face, in order to obtain to life
in the Coming Age! This is hard work!” (p. 44).
         I can allow the possibility of misthological life in this particular Johannine context, though if I were to do so I
would do so dualistically, seeing a soteriological foundation upon which misthological implications are built. That point
aside, however, for the present purposes, my initial questions is, “What about the singular form of aion found in passages
such as Jn 4:14 and 10:28?” In the context of the former, the eternal life in question is explicitly referred to as “the gift
of God,” and both passages are affirming that one will never thirst or perish. How is never limited to 1,000 years?
Notwithstanding this apparent weakness in her argument, I will, for the most part, ignore it in the present study, as
pursuing it is unnecessary since her entire argument against the eternality of rewards collapses under the weight of her
own concessions.
         Her conclusion is that if believers are unfaithful, then they will find themselves “in the exact condition for which
God placed the man and woman outside the Garden in Genesis chapter three, shutting off the way to the tree of life, so
that they could not live for a long period of time in that condition!” (p. 49, italics hers) To be sure, I agree that they
would be in the same condition, but I disagree as to the duration. Her conclusion is derived from the heart of her argument
regarding their exclusion from the tree of life:
         22
            Then the Lord God said, “Behold, the man has become like one of Us, knowing good and evil; and now,
         lest he stretch out his hand, and take also from the tree of life, and eat, and live forever [olam; aion]”—
         23
            therefore the Lord God sent him out from the garden of Eden, to cultivate the ground from which he was
         taken. 24 So He drove the man out; and at the east of the garden of Eden He stationed the cherubim, and
         the flaming sword which turned every direction, to guard the way to the tree of life. (Gen 3:22-24)

       The heart of the matter is whether to interpret the Hebrew olam and Greek aion in v. 22, as forever (denoting
eternally) or age-long (denoting millennially)? Zeigler unwittingly weakens her argument when she takes it to mean a
long period of time. Translating it as a long period of time might be possible in other contexts but not in this context for
two reasons. First, Zeigler has acknowledged the exactness of the condition between their temporal displacement and the
millennial displacement. If being cast out of the Garden pictures being shut off from the tree of life millennially, then the
most natural inference is that God is concerned that they not live for an age in that condition, the age being a 1,000 years
in the former since it is a 1,000 years in its typological counterpart. Second, her long-period-of-time rendition does not
make sense. Note the tension that it creates in her explanation:

               As evidenced by God’s Own Words, He did not want them to live “forever” with a redeemed spirit,
         and an unredeemed soul and body….It was not God’s desire for the man and the woman to have physical
         immortality in a partially redeemed state.
               We should note here that the Hebrew word translated “forever” at the end of v. 22 is the transliterated

78
     Cindy Zeigler, The Full Scope of Our Salvation: Understanding WHY we are Being Saved. No publisher, no date.
Excursus on the First Misthological Death                                                                              Page 63
         word “olam”. This word literally means for a long period of time. The Greek counterpart to this Hebrew
         word is “aion” which also literally means a period of time. It is interesting that neither of the two languages
         through which God gave His Word—the Hebrew and Greek—has a word that is comparable to our English
         word eternity. Of course any of the authors of the Bible could express the concept of eternity, but He would
         have to use a series of words, or the plural form of the word in order to get across the concept of eternity.
         But in the passage in Genesis chapter three, the singular form of the word olam is used, literally meaning
         “for a [singular] long period of time”. (Emphasis and insert hers, p. 29.)

        So which is it? “Did God not want them to live forever in this partially redeemed state, or He did not want them to
live a long time in this state?” At first Zeigler says one, then the other. Evidently, she really means the latter. So God
drove them out so that they would not live for a long period of time in this state. If Zeigler is correct, then the obvious
implication is that Adam did not live a long time in that state as a result of being driven out. If that is true, then the
question that immediately comes to mind is, “How long did they live in that state?” The context informs us: “So all the
days that Adam lived were nine hundred and thirty years, and he died” (Gen 5:5). Adam did not live a long time in that
state, only 930 years! Obviously, Adam did live a long period of time in that state, and just as clearly Zeigler’s long-time
interpretation is refuted by the context. Zeigler’s argument cannot even get out of the starting box.
        So that I can engage her argument in some intelligible manner, I will strengthen it. Zeigler is correct to perceive
that some duration of time must be involved. The best durability to pose for her model is that olam means a 1,000 years
in this context. One might even recall that Adam died before the 1,000 years were up. Thus he died that very day, just as
God had warned. Perhaps this is why God did not want Adam to live the full 1,000 years in that state. According to this
explanation, God would have been content to allow Adam to eat of the tree of life in his partially redeemed state except
for the fact that it would conflicted with God’s warning that Adam would die before the 1,000 years expired. The issue
is merely the quantity of life, not quality of life. This exclusive focus on the quantity of life is not convincing, however,
in that Zeigler correctly pointed out the correlation between unfaithful believers and Adam. Being cast away from the
tree of life definitely entails a qualitative dimension.
                                                          Chitwood
       At the other end of the spectrum, Chitwood seeks to do away with any quantitative association with eating of the
tree of life in favor of an exclusively qualitative understanding:

                 Adam had lived in an undying state prior to his sin, as he continued to live in an antithetical dying
         state following his sin; and to say that the tree of life was given to Adam in his unfallen state to provide
         perpetuity of life, preventing death, cannot possibly be correct. Nor could it possibly be correct to say that
         the tree of life would have had anything to do with providing physical life (keeping Adam alive physically)
         following the fall. A tree can produce only “after his kind” (Gen. 1:11, 12). In this respect, fruit from the
         tree of life simply could not have produced one result before man’s fall and another following man’s fall.
                 In Rev. 2:7, partaking of the tree of life has been promised to the overcomers from among those
         already possessing eternal life. Consequently, in this passage, the tree of life can have nothing whatsoever
         to do with perpetuity of life; and it is the same in the Genesis account when man was first brought upon the
         scene to rule and to reign.
                 The tree of life in both Genesis chapters two and three and Revelation chapter two appears in a
         different setting entirely. The tree of life reserved for Christians in Rev. 2:7 is associated peculiarly with a
         provision for those who will rule and reign as co-heirs with Christ; and viewing Adam’s position in the
         Genesis account — created to rule and reign, in possession of an unending life, with the fruit of the tree of
         life at his disposal — the same thought concerning regality in connection with the tree of life would hold
         true. Thus, Adam in a fallen state, no longer in a position to rule, could not be allowed to eat of the tree of
         life, for fruit from this tree would provide “life” in relation to regality. (Emphasis his.)79

       Suppose that Adam had been allowed to stay in the Garden of Eden and eat of the tree of life. Then according to
this perspective, Adam would have died while eating of the tree of life. That proposition, though hypothetical, strikes me
as rather odd. Also, Chitwood evidently fails to perceive how dangerously close to Arminianism his argument sounds.
Arminians do not believe that it is necessary to believe in Jesus for eternal life in order to receive the gift of eternal life.
You can believe in Him for temporary life and get this so-called eternal life. If you stop believing in Jesus, then you lose
this so-called eternal life. You believe in Jesus for probation in order to get salvation. Unfortunately, even some in Free
Grace theology who believe in unconditional security think that Arminians can be granted unconditional security by

79
     Chitwood, Bride, 94-95.
Page 64                                                                                                 Rewards are Eternal

believing in Jesus for conditional security. This is like saying that you can believe in Jesus for a ham sandwich and get
eternal life instead. I will not belabor this point in the present book since I deal with it so thoroughly elsewhere in my
writings.
       More importantly for our present purposes, Chitwood’s claim that living forever (i.e., eternal life) in Gen 3:22
cannot have anything to do with quantity of life because those eating it already have eternal life fails because he fails to
consider the Principle of Stratification as it applies to this part of the discussion. Yes, overcomers soteriologically have
eternal life independently of the tree. However, even for them the tree imparts life at the higher stratum, the misthological
stratum. Continual eating of the tree is necessary, even for those who possess eternal life at the higher stratum, if they are
to continue to enjoy life at the highest level. Eating of the tree perpetuates life at the misthological level. If their right
to eat of the tree of life were taken away, then they would be subject to misthological death. Chitwood does well to
associate the eating of the tree with regality. But his argument against perpetuity is not in the least bit convincing. He has
posed a false dichotomy. The tree of life provides regality perpetually. No dichotomy exists between regality and
perpetuity. If you lose your right to the tree of life, then you lose life at the misthological level, not at the soteriological
level.
                                                          Synthesis
       However, because one cannot have life at the higher level without having life at the lower level, I am inclined to
agree with Dillow regarding the duality: “The original tree of life would have provided immortality on earth in mankind’s
natural bodies had Adam not sinned and been expelled (Genesis 3:22). This future tree of life will provide an enhanced
experience of life in the new heavens and the new earth.”80 Nevertheless, I would combine this position with my response
to Chitwood to create a synthesis. Dillow is correct to perceive a perpetuity in the past and regality in the future. Chitwood
is correct to perceive a regality in both the past and future. Moreover, I would add the best part of Zeigler’s argument,
which in essence is that God did not intend a dichotomy between perpetuity and regality back then and neither will He
do so in the future. They had to die so that the perpetuity imparted by the tree of life would not prevent them from
eventually attaining regality. Zeigler is correct in that perspective, but she misses the fact that the regality cannot be
separated from the perpetuity: not by a day, much less a long period of time. This is why God had to remove them from
the tree of life immediately, as Chitwood correctly perceives. Let us be reminded of these future texts before expressing
a conclusion.
                                            Illustration 48. The Tree is not Free
         To him who overcomes, I will reward [didomi] him to eat of the tree of life, which is in the Paradise of
         God. (Rev 2:7; TM)

         Behold, I am coming quickly, and My reward [misthos] is with Me, to reward [apodidomi] every man
         according to what he has done….Blessed are those who wash their robes that they may have the right to
         the tree of life, and may enter by the gates into the city. (Rev 22:12,14; TM)

         If anyone takes away from the words of the book of this prophecy, God will take away his portion from
         the tree of life and from the holy city. (Rev 22:19; TM; CT; MT)
                                    Illustration 49. Synthesis of Chitwood and Zeigler




80
     Dillow, Destiny, 667.
Excursus on the First Misthological Death                                                                              Page 65

        My synthesis is that the tree provided a perpetual regality in the past and will do so in the future as well. God cast
Adam out of the Garden of Eden not merely because eating the tree of life would have kept him alive in a partially
redeemed state but because he was no longer entitled to its regality. Zeigler was correct to perceive that God’s providing
garments of skin for Adam in Eve in the Garden of Eden demonstrates “their spiritual rebirth” (p. 42; emphasis hers).
But God did not intend for them to eat of the tree in this partially redeemed state—ever, much less for a long period of
time, millennially, or eternally. Contrary to Zeigler, I insist that it was God’s intention that they not be allowed to eat of
this tree for even one Earth day, much less for a long period, in this partially redeemed state. My perception is that God
cast them out of the Garden of Eden that very day, after providing them with the garments. They were born again and
clothed, and then cast out of the Garden of Eden, just like unfaithful believers will be cast out of the Reward City and
away from the tree of life in the future. Misthological redemption is necessary for misthological participation in the tree
and city. Soteriological redemption will not suffice for eating of the tree. Adam and Eve were cast out for the rest of their
lives. The moral to this story is that if we are unfaithful as believers, we will be cast out for the rest of our partially
redeemed lives as well. Soteriological redemption will not suffice. Misthological redemption must be obtained, and it
must be obtained before the Bema because there is no opportunity to achieve it afterwards. Living by the sweat of your
face is a picture of the outer darkness. The expulsion is a picture of the Bema. They were cast for the rest of their lives,
typologically picturing forever. The tree of life was a reward for faithfulness in the beginning. It will be a reward during
the millennium. It will remain a reward during the eternal state because rewards are eternal.
                                        Killing Three Birds with One Stone
       With a concession made by all three writers, I am able to prove, in one fell swoop, that rewards are eternal by
examining the plural and duplicated occasions of the words normally translated eternal and pointing out that they are
used in this same form regarding rewards.

•     Craig: “Plural and duplicated forms…carry us far beyond the limit of the age to come” (p. 133).
•     Zeigler: “A series of words, or the plural form of the word” conveys “the concept of eternity” (p. 29.)
•     Chitwood: “The only way in which the Greek text can express “eternal” apart from textual considerations is
      through a use of aion in the plural.”81

Since I already have proven my point in the body of the present work, I will only supplement that treatment with a brief
survey of alam by noting the fifteen verses in which it occurs in the OT.
                                              Illustration 50. Alam in the OT

       YLT completely misses the contextual setting of alam when it translates Dan 7:27 as, “And the kingdom, and the
dominion, even the greatness of the kingdom under the whole heavens, is given to the people—the saints of the Most High,
His kingdom is a kingdom age-during, and all dominions do serve and obey Him.” Yet even YLT cannot avoid the eternality
of Dan 7:18: “And receive the kingdom do the saints of the Most High, and they strengthen the kingdom unto the age, even
unto the age of the ages.” The singular kingdom age in question is unto the age of the ages. The kingdom age = the age of
the ages. Its singularity is expressed by the plurality. Their misthological regality is a perpetuity. The same conclusion
depicted earlier regarding olam is fully applicable to alam. As applied to the kingdom age, both words are used to confirm
the eternal nature of rewards.

                                                  Where do the Birds Lie?
        The previous risk assessment performed in Illustration 19. Pascal’s Wager pitted the age-long view against the
eternal view. The present appraisal will offer a fine tuning of that previous analogy. In order to have a fuller misthological
spectrum against which to evaluate the risk, I will include those teachers who pose that rewards don’t really matter
(RDRM). At the conclusion of my last appendix in Darkness, I related the following evaluation, which is abbreviated
here. I attended a FG conference in which the teacher asserted that all believers are elected corporately to be a part of the
Bride of Christ. During the Q&A period at the end of the session, I asked if his position insulated believers from the
results of the Bema. He responded that the Bema was merely educational, which he explained to mean that all believers
will have the deficiencies in their understanding corrected at the Bema so that thereafter they can rule with Christ as
members of his Bride. He indicated that he was familiar with bridal misthology but rejected it. A number of those
attending his workshop session clapped at his response. Likewise, many teachers assert that all believers will, for all
practical purposes, be treated equally at the Bema.
Excursus on the First Misthological Death                                                                        Page 67
                                   Illustration 52. No RDRME or RDRMC in TOD




          •    RRM   Rewards Really Matter
          •    RDRM Rewards Don’t Really Matter
          •    RDRME Rewards Don’t Really Matter Eternally
          •    RDRMC Rewards Don’t Really Matter Comparatively
        Accordingly, my theory is that those RDRM teachers who promote misthological licentiousness (i.e., that the way
you live does not really matter in terms of heavenly rewards) will fail to inherit the kingdom. Even if they themselves do
not stumble into licentiousness behavior, they will have placed a stumbling block before others that discourages them
from being faithful. Consequently, such teachers will not qualify for bridal rulership. As a result of disbelieving the
kingdom message, they will disqualify themselves from kingdom rulership. Misthological unbelievers will not be
kingdom rulers. The illustration places RDRM teachers on the scale at the remotest section of TOD (outer darkness) by
placing them to the far left. At the opposite end are teachers who teach that rewards really matter (RRM). These teachers
will shine brightly forever (Dan 12:3). RDRME and RDRMC teachers fall in the middle. Some RDRME teachers are
better than some RDRMC teachers, and some RDRMC teachers are better than some RDRME teachers. It is a matter of
emphasis. Notwithstanding, will their mistaken teaching not place any of them in the TOD as pictured in Illustration 52?
More likely, some of them will be in the TOD. The proposal that none of them will be subject to the outer darkness
despite the degree of error in their misthology is unreasonable.
Page 68                                                                                            Rewards are Eternal

                                  Illustration 53. All RDRME and RDRMC in TOD




       Another possibility is that their wrongly dividing the word on this matter will place all RDRME and RDRMC
teachers in the TOD as pictured in Illustration 53. This position is probably just as extreme in the opposite direction as
the previous proposal, however.
                                 Illustration 54. Some RDRME and RDRMC in TOD




       A more balanced hypothesis is that some RDRME teachers will be in TOD as a result of their devaluing rewards
and some will not be. Likewise, some RDRMC teachers will be in the TOD while others are not. For example, RDRMC
teachers acknowledge that rewards are eternal but assert that the Bema is merely an award’s podium. So although the
Excursus on the First Misthological Death                                                                           Page 69
rewards are eternal, they do not really matter. To the degree a RDRMC teacher’s emphasis approaches a RDRM
perspective, that kingdom teacher places himself or herself in danger of a RDRM result, namely the outer darkness.
       Likewise, just as my anticipation in Darkness is that those teachers teaching an exclusively educational Bema put
themselves in danger of being cast into the outer darkness, I further suspect that those teaching an exclusively millennial
Bema may be in danger of the same outcome. To the degree that a Kingdom teacher’s RDRME emphasis approaches a
RDRM perspective, that Kingdom teacher is in danger of a RDRM result, namely the outer darkness. I will not belabor
the point. I am simply writing as an analyst, and that is my analysis as to the most probable projection. Be warned.

        At the end of the video I posted on YouTube for Truth Topic 10: Misthological Death
(http://youtu.be/XNzrtG3MG8I), I used animation software to help illustrate the possible variations in emphases. Some
RDRME teachers will emphasize millennial rewards but never mention that they believe these rewards are limited to
1,000 years. They maximize the doctrine of rewards as much as possible within their presentations. Though they are
mistaken in thinking that rewards are limited to the millennium, I do not believe that such teachers are in any danger
whatsoever of disqualifying themselves from inheriting the kingdom. On the other hand, some RDRME teachers, holding
this same perspective, will emphasize that rewards are only millennial in scope and thereby minimize rewards in view of
eternity. To the degree that such a teacher minimizes rewards, that teacher risks being cast into the outer darkness. This
minimization of rewards can easily lead to the perception from the pew that rewards do not really matter at all.
Pragmatically, RDRME can approach RDRM and result in TOD.
