only in nature.” He merely has shown that there will be some differences pertaining to some of the rewards, an assessment
with which I would fully agree. As noted in the first edition of RAE, Chitwood apparently has moved beyond his
statement above, which merely says that Scripture is silent about ER, so as to conclude that Scripture actually does deny
ER. For he says in another book:

               Scripture deals with millennial rewards and/or loss, never with eternal rewards and/or loss. This
         should be easy enough for anyone to understand, for if rewards are eternal, so is loss of rewards. And loss
         of rewards involves an association with death (Rom. 8:13), something which Scripture clearly reveals will
         be done away with at the beginning of the eternal ages beyond the Millennium (I Cor. 15:26; Rev. 21:4).94
         (Emphasis his.)

Chitwood’s deductive argument amounts to this:

        1.   “If rewards are eternal, so is loss of rewards.”
        2.   “Loss of rewards involves an association with death.”
        3.   Death “will be done away with at the beginning of the eternal ages.”
        4.   Therefore, rewards are not eternal.

If Chitwood is correct in his first three statements, then the proper conclusion for his deductive argument is that rewards
are not eternal, not that Scripture is silent about whether rewards are eternal. His deductive argument necessitates a denial
of eternal rewards. Chitwood is inconsistent in saying that Scripture does not deal with whether rewards will continue
into the eternal state and then turning around and insisting that Scripture clearly reveals that rewards will be done away
with in the eternal state. Moreover, his statements in both of these books were from that same year—2011.
                                                           No Death
        In RAE, I already have shown the superiority of ER to TR in regard to the no-death promise. In short, the promise
of no form of death of any type in Rev 21:4 is a reward promised and limited to overcomers according to both Rev 21:7
and its broader context and background and logical considerations. Unfaithful believers, that is, subcomers, will be hurt
by the second death eternally in that they will experience misthological death in the form of loss of misthological life
forever. The free gift of eternal life assures all believers of being with Christ in His kingdom and thus being saved from
the Lake of Fire during both the millennial age and the eternal state. The reward aspect of eternal life, however, such as
eating of the tree of life and wearing the crown of life, is limited to faithful believers (i.e., overcomers) during both the
millennial age and eternal state. The promise of not being hurt by the second death in Rev 2:11 is limited to such
overcomers and is best taken as indicating that subcomers (i.e., unfaithful believers) will be hurt by the second death
eternally.
        Chitwood asserts otherwise: “It is the overcomer’s promise to the Church in Smyrna which has to do with the lake of
fire, something which can only be millennial within its scope of fulfillment. That is, the conditions alluded to for the
nonovercomer in this promise will exist for the duration of the Messianic Era, not throughout the eternal ages beyond”
(emphasis his).95 But why is being hurt by the Lake of Fire limited to the millennial age? The lost certainly will be hurt by the
Lake of Fire during both the millennial age and eternal state. If unfaithful believers can be hurt by the second death throughout
the course of the millennial kingdom without endangering the gift-reward differentiation regarding eternal life, then why can
these unfaithful believers not be hurt by the second death in the eternal state as well without doing violence to this distinction?
        Contrary to Chitwood, various theories are possible to explain how Rev 2:11 could have eternal misthological
implications. For one thing, many interpreters would just take the verse as a litotes. If this is correct, then Rev 2:11 is not
saying that unfaithful believers will be hurt by the second death. Personally, though, I am inclined to agree with Chitwood
that unfaithful believers will be hurt by the second death. I discuss these various options in TOD. As discussed therein,
Chitwood prefers to say that unfaithful believers will be hurt metaphorically by the second death; I prefer to say that they
will be hurt correlatively by the second death. Just as unbelievers are hurt soteriologically by the Lake of Fire, so
unfaithful believers could be hurt correlatively by the Lake of Fire—both for eternity. This correlative model is the
misthological model I posed in TOD. Unfaithful believers are never submitted literally to the flames, but a correlation
exists between the soteriological death of unbelievers and the misthological death of unfaithful believers. Alternatively,
and more recently, Hollandsworth has posed a very interesting model in which unfaithful believers are hurt by the literal

94
     Arlen L. Chitwood, Mysteries of the Kingdom, revised (2011), 166.
95
     Chitwood, Mysteries, 166.
Excursus on the Rod of Iron                                                                                            Page 95
flames of the Lake of Fire at the Bema, but these unfaithful believers are not liable to literal flames after the fiery judgment
at the Bema. I would call this proposal by Hollandsworth the quick-dip model. Regardless as to which proposal is adopted,
Hollandsworth and I are agreed as to the eternal nature of rewards and the loss thereof.96
        In TOD, I refuted the long-dip model, which poses that believers will be submitted to literal flames for a thousand
years. Hollandsworth’s much more modest proposal, though, is worthy of consideration and seems to be a viable
alternative to my own correlative model. Indeed, one might allow that his model and mine are both correct in that after
the quick dip believers are no longer subject to literal fire; nevertheless, the misthological results are eternal and
thus correlative. In fact, in my Misthological Models Part 4 (MMP4), I find Hollandsworth’s model very useful in
posing a possible solution as to how to reconcile some near death experiences with Free Grace misthology. In any event,
Chitwood’s appeal to the no-death promise as a supposed proof text in favor of TR actually backfires when Rev 21:4 is
studied in its context. If unfaithful believers can be hurt for a 1000 years by the second death, then there is no reason to
think that they cannot be hurt in the subsequent eternal state by the Lake of Fire. Indeed, my analysis of Rev 21:4 in RAE
gives substantial grounds to conclude that Rev 21:4 actually implies that unfaithful believers will be hurt misthologically
by the second death during the eternal state. The loss of reward that they experience by the painful Bema flames will be
eternal. Subcomers will not burn eternally—neither metaphorically, nor correlatively, nor literally—but they will suffer
the results of having been burned, for all eternity. Strike one for Chitwood’s three-pronged TR claim above regarding the
three allegedly problematic rewards in Rev 2-3. The promise of not being hurt by the second death poses no problem for
ER.
                                                  No Rod of Iron
      Chitwood also overstates the strength of his argument in relation to the rod of iron. Those who hold to the ER
ideology acknowledge that ruling with an iron rod is no longer necessary in the eternal state. However, this
acknowledgement poses no problem for the ER model, as I stated in TOD:

              In conclusion, procreation will continue so that mankind can inhabit and rule the whole creation. No
       rulership can exist without subjects to be ruled. A distinction must be made between the nations and those
       who rule over them in the millennial age (Rev 2:26). This distinction extends into the eternal age. Yet the
       transition from one age to the other will bring a change. Whereas the millennial nations will be
       composed of men and women in sinful fleshly bodies who reproduce offspring with sin natures, the eternal
       nations will be composed of men and women in sinless flesh and blood bodies who likewise reproduce
       sinless offspring. Correspondingly, a change in the nature of the rule will occur that reflects the change in
       the nature of the subjects. Ruling with a rod of iron and forcing submission will no longer be necessary
       (Rev 2:26[-27]). In eternity their subjects will submit joyfully to the serving rulership of their rulers.

       The obvious conclusion from the ER perspective is that rulership will continue to be a reward in the eternal state,
but the nature of that rulership will change in that it no longer will be carried out with a rod of iron because the nature of
the subjects will have changed, not because the rulership will have ceased to be a reward. Chitwood is thinking much too
simplistically when he assumes that the transition necessarily spells an end to the misthological nature of the rulership
just because ruling with a rod of iron is no longer necessarily. Three times in the above quote from TOD, I said that there
would be a change! Both ER and TR acknowledge that a change takes place. By not owning up to this fact, Chitwood
has failed to pose a valid objection to the ER model. Strike two for the Chitwoodian three-pronged TR claim above. The
fact that overcomers will not rule with a rod of iron in the eternal state poses no problem for the ER perspective.
                                             No Separate Throne for Christ
       Chitwood’s appeal to Rev 3:21 as proof that millennial rewards are not eternal is the weakest of his three
arguments. Christ promises: “He who overcomes, I will grant to him to sit down with Me on My throne, as I also overcame
and sat down with My Father on His throne.” Apparently, for some seemingly unexplained reason, Chitwood seems to
think that Christ’s not having a separate throne in the eternal state means that overcomers will not have a misthological
throne in the eternal state. But why? Will Christ no longer deserve to rule in the eternal state by virtue of His having
overcome? Perish the thought! But if Christ still deserves to rule in an expanded capacity in the eternal state by virtue of
having overcome, then why not conclude that overcomers will also? Chitwood correctly states:

             The rule of Christ itself during the eternal ages will no longer be limited to the earth. Rather, it will
       extend beyond the earth (the new earth), out into the universe.

96
   James S. Hollandsworth, The End of the Pilgrimage: Your Judgment Seat Verdict and How it Determines Your Place
in His Kingdom (USA: Holly Publishing, 2015).
Page 96                                                                                               Rewards are Eternal

              Christ will be seated upon a throne from which there will be an administration of power and authority
       throughout the universe — “the throne of God and of the Lamb” (Rev. 22:1, 3); and the Christians’
       continuing rule “with Christ” (Rev. 22:5) would have to be of a like nature, for the power will no longer
       emanate from Christ’s throne, but from the throne of God and of the Lamb. In this respect, the rule by
       Christ and His co-heirs over the earth during the Millennium can only be extended to a rule over worlds
       throughout the universe following the Millennium.97 (Emphasis his.)

      As argued in TOD, I am in complete agreement that our misthological rulership will expand throughout the
universe. So how is this a problem for the ER position? Chitwood continues:

              And the position which man (all of saved mankind) will occupy in this universal rule is unrevealed,
       though, as previously seen, it appears clear that man’s rule in that day will be universal since power will
       emanate from “the throne of God and of the Lamb.” Possibly this rule will have to do with, or include,
       positions under God over provincial rulers (angels) in the multiplied billions of provinces scattered
       throughout the multiplied billions of galaxies comprising the physical universe. But, again, we’re not told.
       This is something yet to be revealed.98 (Emphasis his.)

        Chitwood’s argument, as he has stated it in connection with Christ’s extended rulership, which is affirmed by
Revelation itself, fails to show why this transition in rulership poses a problem for the ER position. Overcomers will
eventually rule the entire universe. This proposition poses no problem for ER.99 If this is what Chitwood means by
universal, then I am in complete agreement. On the other hand, he might mean that it will be universal in the sense that
all men will rule.
        Presumably, the latter is what Chitwood intends, judging from what he says in Mysteries: “Christ and His co-heirs,
beyond the Millennium, will no longer rule over the nations, as this rule is pictured in Rev. 2:26, 27. Rather, the Gentiles
comprising these nations will be brought into positions of rulership themselves with Christ and His co-heirs, as this rule
extends beyond the earth, out into the universe (Rev. 22:2, 5).”100 My response is, “Prove it! Don’t merely assert it!”
        As demonstrated in RAE, Rev 22:2,5 should be taken as affirming misthological rulership in the eternal state,
limited to the faithful. Chitwood provides mere assertion; I provide demonstration. Even apart from my arguments herein,
however, it suffices for present purposes to note that, when we get beyond the millennial kingdom in Rev 20 to the end
of the book in Rev 21-22, the distinction between kings and subjugated nations still persists: “And the nations shall walk
by its light, and the kings of the earth shall bring their glory into it” (Rev 21:24). The kings rule the nations. The nations
are not coequal with the kings. The nations are not the rulers. Chitwood’s errors are manifest. Strike three for his three-
pronged TR claim above. The fact that Christ will experience some transition in the form of His rulership poses no
problem for ER posing that overcomers will experience a similar misthological transition.
        In conclusion, Chitwood’s three alleged proof texts from Rev 2-3, which purportedly prove that rewards are only
millennial in scope when compared to the eternal state, actually prove that rewards are eternal when interpreted properly
within the context of the eternal state. This cross examination of the TR appeal to three of these promises in Rev 2-3, as
supposedly proving that rewards are only millennial, has demonstrated the superficiality of the TR claim.
                                                        Conclusion
        Let us conclude by comparing the rewards mentioned in Rev 2-3 with the eternal state in Rev 21-22 in a survey
of the following list of rewards from those initial chapters:

       1. Eating of the tree of life
       2. Having the crown of life
       3. Not being hurt by the second death
       4. Eating hidden manna
       5. Having a white stone

       6. Having a new name written on the stone

        The claim that a look at this list would indicate that these rewards are not eternal certainly does not follow plainly.
We will not even be informed of a millennial limitation until ch. 20. This twenty-item list comes much earlier, in chs. 2-
3. Even then, when we get beyond the millennial kingdom in ch. 20 to the end of the book in chs. 21-22, we find that the
tree is still a reward (Rev 22:14,19) and that the distinctions between kings and subjected nations persist (Rev 21:24).
Casting crowns before Jesus’ feet in Rev 4:10 does not prove that rewards only last during the tribulation. Thus one
cannot assume that the promised crown in the list is a temporary reward. So, at the very least, one should be cautious
about assuming that any of these rewards actually terminate and only last during the millennium. Rewards are still limited
to overcomers in the eternal state (Rev 21:7), just as they were limited to overcomers throughout the list in Rev 2-3.
Having washed robes, and thus by implication white garments, is still a misthological theme in the eternal state (Rev
22:14). Being a part of Christ’s Bride is still a reward (cp. Rev 3:20; 19:7; 21:2).101
        In summary, only one of these twenty rewards might seem, with nothing more than a superficial look, to suggest
a limited duration perhaps, namely, the iron rule. But that transition is much better explained by posing that the nations
become willing subjects after the 1000 years, not by posing that the nations become rulers after a 1000 years. After all,
as already noted, the distinction between rulers and those ruled will continue in the eternal state (cp. Rev 21:24).
        As to not being hurt by the second death, overcomers are not hurt by the second death during the millennial
kingdom nor during the eternal state. This reward is not limited to a 1000 years. The prospect of the being hurt by the
second death certainly seems to be a real (albeit correlative) possibility in the eternal state (Rev 21:7). There is no
legitimate reason to think that John is using this promise, nor any of these promises for that matter, to limit rewards to
1000 years. Not having one’s name erased from the Book of Life (Rev 3:5) would most naturally seem to be an eternal
reward. At the conclusion of the millennial kingdom and on into the eternal state, this book continues to play a
misthological role—whether correlatively or misthopologically (Rev 20:12,15; 21:27; 22:19?).102 See the next excurses
for further discussion.
        Granted, Dillow and Chitwood have used a statement that comes much later in the book, in Rev 21:4, to argue that
there is no death of any type in the eternal state. Chitwood then concludes that rewards cease in the eternal state. He has
an interesting argument, but it is in no way derived from a simple reading of chs. 2-3. Rather it is a sophisticated argument,
as proven by the fact that Dillow did not pick up on it and even Chitwood himself did not in Time of the End.103
Notwithstanding, it is an interesting argument, and I have refuted their joint no-death-in-the-eternal-state argument in
detail herein.

   1
      Then I saw a new heaven and a new earth; for the first heaven and the first earth had passed away, and there was no

are faithful and true.” 6 Then He said to me, “It is done. I am the Alpha and the Omega, the beginning and the end. I will
give to the one who thirsts from the spring of the water of life without cost. 7 “He who overcomes will inherit these
things, and I will be his God and he will be My son. 8 “But as for the cowardly and unbelieving and abominable
and murderers and fornicators and sorcerers and idolaters and all the liars, their portion will be in the lake that
burns with fire and brimstone, which is the second death.”
         9
           Then one of the seven angels who had the seven bowls full of the seven last plagues came and spoke with me,
saying, “Come here, I will show you the bride, the wife of the Lamb.” 10 And he carried me away in the Spirit to a
great and high mountain, and showed me the holy city, Jerusalem, coming down out of heaven from God, 11 having the
glory of God. Her brilliance was like a very costly stone, as a stone of crystal-clear jasper....
         23 And the city has no need of the sun or the moon to shine upon it, for the glory of God is its light, and the

Lamb is its lamp. 24 And the nations will walk by its light, and the kings of the earth will bring the glory and the
honor of the nations into it, 25 and its gates will never be shut during the day, and there will be no night there, 26 and
they will bring the glory and the honor of the nations into it in order that they may enter it. 27 But nothing unclean
will enter it, nor anyone who commits abomination or falsehood, but only those whose names are written in the
Lamb’s book of life.
    1 Then he showed me a river of the water of life, clear as crystal, coming from the throne of God and of the

Lamb. 2 In the middle of its street, the tree of life ran along on both sides of the river, bearing twelve kinds of fruit,
yielding a different kind each month; and the leaves of the tree were for the healing of the nations. 3 There will
no longer be any curse; and the throne of God and of the Lamb will be in it, and His servants will serve Him; 4 and
they will see His face, and His name will be on their foreheads. 5 And there will no longer be any night ; and they
will have no need of the light of a lamp nor the light of the sun, because the Lord God will illumine them; and they
will rule forever and ever.
         6

His angel to show to His bond-servants the things which must quickly take place. 7 “And behold, I am coming quickly.
Blessed is the one who keeps the words of the prophecy of this book.” 8 I, John, am the one who heard and saw these

fornicators and the murderers and the idolaters are outside, and everyone who loves and practices lying.

       “I, Jesus, have sent My angel to testify these things to you for the churches. I am the root and the descendant of

to everyone who hears the words of the prophecy of this book: if anyone adds to them, then God will add to him
the plagues which are written in this book; 19 and if anyone takes away from the words of the prophecy of this
book, then God will take away his portion from the tree of life and from the holy city, which are written in this
book. 20 He who testifies to these things says, “Yes, I am coming quickly.” Amen. Yes, come Lord Jesus! 21 The grace
of the Lord Jesus Christ be with all the saints. Amen.
                                Excursus on the Book of Life
                                                       Introduction
                                               Single Book Hypothesis (SBH)
        The tenuous nature of the TR position is evident in the strained attempt of some of its advocates in suggesting that
not having one’s name blotted out of the Book of Life (BOL) in Rev 3:5 is a temporary reward. According to some TR
advocates, after the names of unfaithful believers are erased from the Book of Life for over a 1000 years, their names are
placed back into this same book at the beginning of the eternal state! This proposal is made by some TR advocates who
would reject the two-book solution. Govett, for example, rejects the proposition “that there may be two books of life,”
since, according to him, “’the book of life,’ as far as can be seen, is one and the same throughout all the New Testament.”
Thus he states: “I have no better solution to propose, than that there may be a temporary blotting out of the name of the
believer from the book of life, during the period of reward enjoyed by the others; and a restoration of the name, ere the
final award settles the position of each forever” (Rev 20:15).104 Having one’s name in the Book of Life, which formally
was a reward, now becomes a gift of grace. This TR approach by Govett confuses the gift with the reward by turning the
reward into a gift. Just as bad exegetically, it adds to the text by posing that the names are rewritten in the book. The text
makes no such affirmation. Moreover, it suffers from the same problem incurred by all such single-book models; namely,
some texts suggests that the names are entered permanently since they are recorded from the foundation of the world.
        Faust is a TR advocate who entertains both possibilities. On the one hand, he approvingly quotes Govett and adapts
the single book hypothesis (SBH) as a plausible model for TR. After all, a temporary blotting seems intuitive enough for
a temporary reward. According to his TR spin on the SBH, though, after unfaithful believers have been resurrected, they
are killed for the second time physically in the Lake of Fire, then they burn in Gehenna for a 1000 years, after which time
they are supposedly taken out of the literal fires of Gehenna, and their names are rewritten in the Book of Life.105 They
go from the frying pan into the oven, so to speak, from the Lake of Fire into Gehenna, before being taken out of the oven
after cooking for a 1000 years. The texts that Faust cite in defense of this rewrite proposition are a real stretch, however.
For instance, being beaten with many stripes would be better taken as a picture of the Bema than a 1000 years of beatings
(Lk 12:47). Being saved so as through fire would better picture the Bema than being saved a 1000 years later (1Cor 3:15).
Although Israel was temporally broken off, Paul’s point is not that unfaithful believers will be temporally broken off in
Rom 11:22. Paul simply says that unfaithful believers “will be cut off.” While it is contextually true that all Israel will be
grafted in again, this is not a postmortem regrafting. Moreover, it is limited to the temporal remnant. As explained in
TOD, being handed over to the torturers until one pays back what one owes in Mt 18:34 is better explained as a permanent
condition rather than a temporary one, given the enormity of the dept.
        Moreover, even if the SBH is correct, this one-book model works very well for the ER perspective. The SBH is
the one favored by many advocates of ER and is the one I favor in TOD. An ER-SBH (Eternal Rewards—Singular Book
104
   Govett, Revelation, 1:171 (s.v. Rev 3:5). An anonymous writer, whom I will refer to as AW (and presume to be male),
takes this same position: “Implicit in Rev. 20:15 above is that some will be found written in the book of life, and
accordingly, will not be cast into the Lake of Fire, but will receive their glorified bodies and enter into life at
that time” (emphasis his). AW thinks that unfaithful believes will be delivered out of Gehenna at this time. However,
his conclusion does not necessarily follow from his premise. I too believe that there will be those present at this GWT
judgment who have their names in the Book of Life. Those believers who died during the millennial kingdom (and
possibly many of them at the rebellion at its end) will probably be judged at this time and given their eternal rewards—
exclusion from the Reward City for all eternity. What punishment is dispensed upon those believers who died in this
rebellion according to the TR position? None! Thus Rev 20:15 is better interpreted from the ER position than the TR
perspective. Anonymous writer, The Salvation of the Soul and the Things that will be Hereafter, The Word of the
Kingdom of Heaven (3-15-2015), 487. Available at http://www.salvationofthesoul.org/A_-_Salvation_of_the_Soul_3-
15-15.pdf. AW equates the initial recording of one’s name in the LBOL with the BOL and the salvation of the spirit (p.
20), thus advocating the SBH and making the BOL, in regard to the initial recording therein, a soteriological issue.
Nevertheless, the blotting out of the BOL is strictly a misthological issue (p. 60). The rewriting of the name the second
time is a soteriological issue (p. 487). AW is thus inconsistent in treating the singular name in the same book as both a
soteriological and misthological construct. Somehow putting the name in the book is soteriological but taking the same
name out of the same book in the same way is not soteriological. His befuddlement is inherit to the TR-SBH.

    J.D. Faust, The Rod: Will God Spare It? An Exhaustive Study of Temporary Punishment for Unfaithful Christians at the
Judgment Seat and During the Millennial Kingdom (Hayesville: Schoettle Publishing Co., 2002), 211-216. Also see pp. 72,
230, 406.
Page 100                                                                                              Rewards are Eternal

Hypothesis) is entirely reasonable. Contrastively, it is a difficult perspective for TR to maintain the SBH without doing
exegetical gymnastics and making implausible inferences mentioned above. Even worse, the TR-SBH (Temporary
Rewards—Single Book Hypothesis) seems to confuse the gift and reward aspects of eternal life by making the writing of
one’s name in the BOL a matter of soteriological grace but keeping it there a matter of misthological works. Additionally,
TR-SBH has to run through a gauntlet of objections rising from: (1) soteriological texts like Jn 10:28 and 11:26, which
promise that a believer would never die in the sense of suffer the same type of death as unbelievers, (2) eschatological
texts like 1Thess 5:9-10 which promise that all believers will be with the Lord, even if they are morally asleep, and
(3) logistical texts like Heb 9:27, which pose a singular physical death and postmortem judgment.
                                           Multiple Book Hypothesis (MBH)

                                                            Faust
       On the other hand, unlike Govett, Faust is open to the multiple book hypothesis (MBH): “First, there is a book of
physical life in this world (Exodus 32:32, Psalms 69:28); secondly, there is a book of millennial life in regard to the age
to come (Revelation 3:5); and finally, there is a book of absolute, eternal life (Revelation 20:12)” (emphasis his).106 Faust
poses that the BOL is used misthologically in Rev 3:5 and soteriologically in Rev 20:12. He is on the right tract.
       Even though I favored the single book view in TOD, I subsequently have become much more open to the multiple
book proposition.107 The multiple book view is a reasonable deduction even if John is limiting the scope of his discussion
to the singular vantage point. Even if John is limiting his discussion to the singular book vantage point, however, this
might only be the primary vantage point. The multiple book perspective might be a secondary vantage point. In any case,
the multiple book hypothesis is fully compatible with the broader scope of data. Even so, once again, the ER model is
superior to the TR model even if the MBH is preferred. Fuller’s presentation of the MBH is a classic and available
online.108

                                                         Chitwood
       I already have interacted with the arguments of both Faust and Fuller in TOD and thus will not repeat that
discussion here. Instead, I will turn to Chitwood since he, like Faust, holds to the MBH from a TR perspective, thus a
TR-MBH model (Temporary Rewards—Multiple Book Hypothesis). Yet, unlike Faust, Chitwood does not further
complicate this TR-MBH combination by posing that unfaithful believes are killed twice physically or spend a 1000
years literally burning in Gehenna. In other words, if Ockham’s Razor is applied to this discussion, then Chitwood’s TR-
MBH model is more probable than Faust’s version of this model in that particular regard. Chitwood’s model does not
have to suffer the additional burden of having to run through the gauntlet of objections mentioned above. One might turn
to Chitwood, then, in hopes of finding the best defense for the TR-MBH. He explains his model as follows:

                At the future judgment of the unsaved dead in Rev. 20:11-15 a number of books will be opened,
       including “the book of life” (v. 12). God has a library in heaven, and the book of life is only one book within
       this library. A book which seems to be entirely separate and distinct, but often confused with the book of
       life, is the Lamb’s book of life [LBOL] in Rev. 13:8 (cf. Rev. 21:27). This book would appear to be the
       place wherein the names of redeemed individuals have been inscribed rather than the book of life in Rev.
       3:5; 20:12....
                The book of life will be opened at the judgments of both the saved and the unsaved. The entire scene
       in Rev. 3:5 has to do with issues of the judgment seat of Christ, with the book of life being the only book
       from God’s library of books singled out and mentioned by name. The same thing can be found in the
       judgment of the unsaved dead in Rev. 20:11-15. The book of life alone is singled out and mentioned by
       name. The purpose and content of the book of life are clearly revealed in Rev. 20:12: “…the dead were
       judged out of those things which were written in the books, according to their works.” The book of life is
       a book, among other books, containing the deeds/works of individuals, both those of the saved and those
       of the unsaved; and from the emphasis placed upon the book of life in connection with both judgments,
       along with information concerning other books in Scripture, one could conclude that this is probably God’s
       primary record book containing the deeds/ works of every individual....
                There is no such thing in Scripture as a judgment of the saved and a judgment of the unsaved

       occurring together at the same time....
              All future judgments will be based strictly upon the works of those being judged, which renders it
       impossible for issues surrounding eternal verities to ever enter into these judgments....
              The book of life contains records that have been lived, and the blotting of a Christian’s name out of
       this book follows his judgment on the basis of that which has been recorded in the book and involves
       millennial verities alone.109 (Emphasis his.)

       Surprisingly, however, Chitwood’s differentiation between the books of life is more strained than that of Faust.
At least Faust recognized that having or not having one’s name in the BOL in Rev 20:12-15 is a soteriological issue,
which is the most natural reading of the text: “If anyone’s name was not found written in the book of life, he was thrown
into the lake of fire” (Rev 20:15). Chitwood acts as if this BOL text is dealing with regality rather than soteriology so
that he can treat the LBOL texts as his soteriological texts. This convention is a very forced contraption that traps
Chitwood into making a woefully unconvincing proposition. He strains his argument even more when he says,

              Revelation 21:8 cannot be equated with Rev. 20:14, even though both verses refer to “the lake of
       fire” and “the second death.” These two verses are not dealing with the same thing, the same individuals,
       or even the same time period. Revelation 21:8 deals with the judgment of the saved preceding the
       Millennium, with millennial verities in view; and Rev. 20:14 deals with the judgment of the unsaved
       following the Millennium, with eternal verities in view. Nothing is the same in the two passages, save the
       existence of the same lake of fire, with an associated second death.110 (Emphasis his.)

       Having the Lake of Fire and an association with the second death in common are extremely significant connections
and make the desperation of Chitwood’s attempt to disassociate the passages all the more apparent. Moreover, Rev 21:8
comes after the millennial period described in Rev 20. Therefore, Rev 21:8 would be most naturally taken as referring to
the eternal state, as what comes after the millennium not before it! Chitwood poses an artificial disassociation and
sequence.
       His proposition that the BOL is only one of the Books of Works (BOW) seems highly doubtful as well. A
juxtaposition would be better hypothesized. The BOL is another (allos) book. Yet even if allos suggests another book of
the same kind, this might mean simply another book consulted at judgment, not necessarily a book listing works. The
text dos not state that the BOL is another one of these books. One cannot necessarily infer that the BOL is another one
of the BOW.
       Not all of Chitwood’s TR compatriots would agree with him that the BOL and LBOL are separate books. Chitwood
attempts to pose a distinction between the two as his skeleton key to deal with the complexity of the discussion. The
primary problem with this supposed distinction, nonetheless, is that the names written in both the BOL and the LBOL
are written therein from the foundation of the world (Rev 13:8; 17:8). This phraseology strongly suggests that the names
recorded in both books are recorded permanently. Accordingly, posing a distinction between the two books based on the
alleged removability of the names from the BOL seems artificial. Also, both verses contrast those written in the LBOL
and the BOL with those who dwell on the Earth. Those posing a distinction between the LBOL and BOL typically
assume, like Chitwood, that the LBOL is the book of all those redeemed by the blood of the Lamb who was slain from
the foundation of the world (which is an alternate way to translate Rev 13:8). Yet the BOL is most naturally taken to be
a soteriological record as well (Rev 20:12,15). Such considerations make it very difficult to believe that two different
books are being described. Thus, my preference in TOD was to equate the BOL with the LBOL and adopt the SBH.
       Notwithstanding my earlier preference for the SBH in TOD, due to other factors, I will entertain the MBH herein.
Accordingly, a reasonable MBH would be to entertain the possibility that the BOL has one part that is permanent (denoted
by the entry being made from the foundation of the world) and another part that is erasable (due to the implied implication
of Rev 3:5). Chitwood is in error in trying to deny the soteriological nature of the BOL in Rev 20:12,15.
       Not all of Chitwood’s fellow TR theorists would agree with him that the judgment in Rev 20:11-15 cannot deal
with both believers and nonbelievers. Like these dissenters, I would anticipate that the if anyone in Rev 20:15
presupposes that some will be found at this judgment to have their names in the BOL: “And if anyone’s name was not
found written in the book of life, he was thrown into the lake of fire.” The natural presumption is that some will be found
there who have their names written in the BOL. Otherwise, if Chitwood were correct, one would expect the text to read:
“Since no one’s name was found written in the BOL, they were thrown into the Lake of Fire.” Chitwood’s assumption

109
    Chitwood, Judgment, 141-143. Chitwood’s comment that no future judgments can deal with eternal issues is refuted
by the very topic under consideration. The judgment in Rev 20:11-15 determines the eternal fate of the unregenerate.
110
    Ibid., 94.
Page 102                                                                                                 Rewards are Eternal

confuses if anyone with since no one. His attempt to deny that the BOL has a soteriological intent in Rev 20:15 should
therefore be rejected.
        The BOL has inerasable soteriological entries and potentially erasable misthological entries. What about the
LBOL? Would the LBOL be soteriological or misthological or both? In TOD, I advocated a soteriological understanding
for the LBOL. Doing so poses no problem for my misthology. For example, in that case, Rev 21:27 merely would be
affirming that “only those whose names are written in the Lamb’s book of life” will enter New Jerusalem. It does not
assert that all those who have their names recorded soteriologically will be able to enter the city misthologically.
Regardless as to whether I take a SBH or a MBH, my argument is that unfaithful believers will not be able to enter the
misthological city. Soteriological entry in the book does not entitle one to misthological entry into the city.
        On the other hand, if the MBH is correct, then I am free to retain that original assessment or to entertain the
possibility that the LBOL is a misthological record. The names of unfaithful believers would not be included in the LBOL
in that case.111 If Rev 13:8 is translated in such a way as to suggest that the names of those written in that book have been
there permanently since the foundation of the world, a hypothesis with which I am very comfortable, then the LBOL is
the resulting misthological list of misthological overcomers. After all, Jesus told the faithful sheep: “Inherit the kingdom
prepared for you from the foundation of the world” (Mt 25:34). This misthological preparation is from the foundation of
the world. Proposing that the Lord knows who will inherit the kingdom and that He already has prepared places for them
is certainly reasonable. Indeed, Jesus says as much: “To sit on My right and on My left, this is not Mine to give, but it is
for those for whom it has been [not will be] prepared by My Father” (Mt 20:23). The Lord uses the perfect tense, not the
future tense, thereby indicating that in some sense the preparation has been completed for those selected to these positions.
Yet, in some other sense, the preparation is still underway: Jesus said that He would go and prepare a place for us (Jn
14:2-3). A reasonable conjecture is that the preparation has been completed in the sense that their names have been
permanently recorded from the foundation of the world as occupying those misthological positions. If so, the
misthological recording of their names from the foundation of the world is further evidence that rewards are eternal. Thus
the perfect tense and the fact that the misthological preparation was from the foundation of the world jointly seem to
demand that we conclude that rewards are eternal. The only matter really open for speculation is whether the LBOL is a
prewritten misthological record made for the purpose of that preparation. Dogmatism is unnecessary. My thesis does not
require that the LBOL be regarded as a misthological record of those who successfully pass the Bema examination. Even
so, that proposition seems most natural.
        Conceptually, then, I can agree with Chitwood that Revelation has two books of life: one soteriological and the
other misthological. But I disagree with him as to which passages deal with which aspect. Using the BOL versus the
LBOL as his point of demarcation, he would break the passages up in Revelation as: (1) soteriological life (LBOL)—
Rev 13:8; 21:27; and (2) misthological life (BOL)—Rev 3:5; 17:8; 20:12,15; 22:19(?). I have highlighted his references
to Rev 20:12,15 in red because his trying to pass off the references to the BOL in Rev 20:11-15 as not being soteric is
counter intuitive. After all, the passage concludes by saying, “And if anyone’s name was not found written in the book
of life, he was thrown into the lake of fire.” The soteriological significance of this conclusion is self-apparent. Chitwood
nevertheless tries to reject this conclusion with this strained claim:

               Man’s redemption — wrought through Christ’s finished work at Calvary — has its direct connection
       with that revealed in Genesis surrounding the reason for man’s creation, Eve’s subsequent fall because of
       Satan’s deception, and Adam’s resulting subsequent act. “Salvation” in Scripture is connected with regality,
       not as man often presents the matter, with a rescue or deliverance from the lake of fire.
               Though the lake of fire does await individuals rejecting Christ’s finished work at Calvary, viewing
       salvation with respect to a deliverance from the lake of fire is really not the correct Biblical approach. The
       lake of fire was prepared for “the devil and his angels,” not for man (Matt. 25:41). It was prepared for the
       ones originally ruling the earth who rebelled against God’s supreme regal power and authority.
               In this respect, the lake of fire has its connective origin with regality as it pertains to the earth — the
       same as the purpose for man’s salvation. And this connective origin of the lake of fire with regality and the
       earth is why man, rejecting God’s remedy for sin, will end up in this place.
               Fallen man will have rejected that which has to do first and foremost with regality and the earth. He
       will have rejected a salvation which finds its revealed purpose in the reason for man’s creation and
       subsequent fall. And man, rejecting a salvation of this nature, is doing little more than rebelling against

111
   Perhaps the names of those born in the eternal state are also recorded. They would be able to visit the city, although
they would not live in the city. In any case, the book would still be misthological in that the absence of the subcomers’
names would be intended to exclude subcomers misthologically from the city.
Excursus on the Book of Life                                                                                          Page 103
       God’s supreme regal power and authority — the same as Satan and his angels had done, though after a
       different fashion.
              Thus, though the lake of fire was originally prepared for angelic beings who had rebelled against
       God’s supreme regal power and authority, man, also rebelling in a manner which has to do with regality
       and the earth, will be cast therein as well.112 (Italics his.)

       Chitwood’s claim is bazaar. Unbelievably, in this first paragraph he actually states that redemption/salvation is
connected with misthology (i.e., regality), not with soteriology (i.e., deliverance from the Lake of Fire). In the second
paragraph, he continues his claim that salvation should not be viewed soteriologically. In essence, his is making
soteriology the byproduct of failed misthology: The penalty imposed upon man for failing to gain regality is eternal
damnation. His convoluted claim is that the purpose of redemption was to make it possible for man to earn the reward of
regality, not for the purpose of providing man the gift of salvation from eternal damnation. Why can it not be for both?
Chitwood has set up a false dichotomy in posing that redemption cannot be for both purposes: soteriology and misthology.
In denying that redemption has a connection with soteriology, Chitwood has confused the concept of gift with reward,
fusing them together into one. He is refuted by ample passages. Salvation often deals simply with soteriology. Time and
again Scripture affirms a foundational level of salvation that grants unconditional security as a gift apart from one’s
works, and therefore apart from any attempts to merit regality (e.g., Jn 3:17; 10:9; Acts 16:30-31; Eph 2:5,8; Tit 3:5).
The same is true for redemption (e.g., Rom 8:24; Eph 1:7,14; Col 1:14). Both salvation and redemption have fundamental
soteriological dimensions. Chitwood has grossly overstated his case, so much so as to pervert the gospel of grace.
Salvation and redemption are connected with both soteriology and misthology, not solely with misthology. His placement
of Rev 20:12,15 exclusively in the misthological category is a big red flag, as denoted by the red highlight above.
Thankfully, there are better kingdom models for the MBH from which to choose.

                                                           Whipple
        Whipple is a kingdom teacher who also adopts the MBH and poses three books of life in Scripture. He uses the
tripartite breakdown: (1) physical life (body)—Ps 139:16, (2) soteriological life (spirit)—Phil 4:3; Rev 13:8; 17:8; 20:15;
21:27; and (3) misthological life (soul)—Rev 3:5; 22:19 (KJV).113 Like Chitwood, Whipple considers the LBOL
soteriological. Unlike Chitwood, Whipple does not limit the LBOL to the two texts that use that designation. Rather, in
some texts Whipple equates the LBOL with BOL; alternatively, in some texts he does not. Naturally, he places the two
texts suggesting that an unfaithful believer’s name may be erased from the BOL in the misthological category. The other
verses dealing with either the BOL or LBOL are lumped together in the soteriological category. Whipple correctly places
Rev 20:15 in the soteriological category. Like Chitwood, he places Rev 21:27 in the soteriological category, but he gives
it a limited misthological application even in the eternal state: “All the saved, i.e., all whose names are written in the
Lamb’s book of life (Rev. 21:27), will have the right to enter the city, though they will not be permitted to live there.”114
Moreover, Whipple correctly affirms that rewards continue in the eternal state, with unfaithful believers living as paupers
outside the city. Bob Wilkin also entertains the possibility that not all believers will be allowed to live inside the city;
nevertheless, they will be allowed to enter the city.115
        Whipple and Wilkin are light years ahead of Chitwood. Their position is much more soteriologically and
misthologically astute. Likewise, I technically considered Rev 21:27 a soteriological text in TOD but pragmatically gave
it full misthological applicability. Therein I denied that unfaithful believers will even be allowed to enter the city, and I
still hold to that position. Even though, like Whipple and Wilkin, I initially placed this verse in the soteriological category,
I have now come to question that location. In TOD, I had to categorize Rev 21:27 as a soteriological verse with a
misthological import because I held to the SBH. Since I subsequently abandoned the SBH in favor of the MBH (for
reasons explained in WWWS, MMP2 and MMP4), I would now flag down Whipple and Wilkin’s placing Rev 21:27 in
the soteriological category. Therefore, I have used a red font to question Whipple’s placing this text in the soteriological
section. That placement is permissible, but a misthological placement is better.

                                                        Hollandsworth
        Like Whipple, Hollandsworth follows the tripartite breakdown for the books of life also. But Hollandsworth places
the key passage under discussion in a different location than Whipple. Hollandsworth’s breakdown of the passages is as
follows: (1) physical life (body)—Ex 32:32-33; Ps 139:16, (2) soteriological life (spirit)—Lk 10:20; Phil 3:20; 4:3; Rev
13:8; 20:15; and (3) misthological life (soul)—Rev 3:5; 21:27; 22:19 (KJV). As seen, he adds a few more passages than
Whipple and, most interestingly, moves Rev 21:27 to the misthological location.
        Splendid! I had migrated slowly to a similar position regarding Rev 21:27 for reasons explained briefly at a Word
of Truth Bible conference in 2014, which Hollandsworth attended. At the conference, I presented a Power Point
presentation of the Principle of Correlativity as it applies to these key Johannine passages: Rev 21:8,27; 22:15.116 Months
later, as I was preparing the update in this excursus for the second edition of RAE, I inquired as how Hollandsworth came
to this conclusion. He responded that, as a result of attending the conference the previous fall, in which Scott Crawford
and I had defended the thesis that rewards are eternal, he had come to embrace that perspective as well. As a result of
adopting the ER perspective, Hollandsworth had now come to make a misthological connection between Rev 21:27 and
Rev 22:14-15 (and thus misthology in the eternal state); whereas, before, he could only connect Rev 21:27 with Rev 2:7
and 3:5 (and thus with misthology in the millennial kingdom). At the time I made my email inquiry, I completely had
forgotten that I had spent seven minutes at the previous conference explaining how Rev 21:27 could be interpreted
misthologically.117 I had posed a dualistic interpretation, interpreting it primarily as soteriological but applying it
secondarily as misthological, yet stating that John intended for us to see both dimensions. So I interpreted it
soteriologically with secondary misthological applicability via the Principle of Correlativity.
        To capsulate the migration in my thinking, the following endnote from TOD is a summation of my initial position:
              Rev 21:8,27; 22:15 are recapitulations of the judgment of noncomers described in 20:11-15;
       Rev 22:17 is a recapitulation of the comers described in Rev 21:6 (and some comers will be subcomers—
       Rev 22:19), and Rev 22:3-5,14 are recapitulations of the overcomers of Rev 21:7. Thus, I agree with
       Wilkin’s conclusion that Rev 22:15 describes the nonelect outside the eternal kingdom. See Robert N.
       Wilkin, “Who are the Outsiders? Revelation 22:14-17,” GIF (November-December 1993).118 (Bold
       original, red added.)
        Although I held to the ER position in TOD, I basically agreed with Wilkin in equating Rev 21:27 and 22:15 with
Rev 20:11-15 and 21:8 as soteriological texts. Like Wilkin, at that time I reasoned that these latter two texts were certainly
soteriological. And Rev 21:8,27 and 22:15 all simply seemed to be parallel vice lists to 21:8 and 22:15. Granted, Wilkin
overstated his argument for the soteriological position when he surmised: “Rev 21:27 says that only those whose names
are written in the Lamb's Book of Life will enter ‘it.’ The word it there most naturally refers to the kingdom since there
will be no one anywhere on the New Earth whose name is not written in the Lamb’s Book of Life” (emphasis his). Not
so! In that context, it most naturally refers to the city since the city is the topic under discussion in Rev 21. Just read Rev
21 and see it for yourself. Wilkin’s conclusion that everyone on the New Earth will have their name in the LBOL is an
assumption that is only potentially true, if the LBOL is a soteriological record, but even then it would not necessarily be
true, as I pointed out in TOD. But if the LBOL is a misthological record, which is the point under consideration in the
present discussion (and a proposition made possible via the MBH), then this aspect of Wilkin’s argument collapses
entirely.
        As seen in the present study, my subsequent assessment of Rev 21:8 has become much more refined than that
simple soteriological interpretation in that endnote from TOD, in which I originally concurred with Wilkin regarding
basic classification of the texts in question. As stated in the present book, in regard to Illustration 8. Rev 21:7-8 in Three
Colors (p. 16), I subsequently came to conclude that Rev 21:8 is primarily soteriological (blue), but secondarily
misthological (yellow). Conversely, as discussed in MMP2, I would regard Rev 21:27 as primarily misthological
(yellow), but secondarily soteriological (blue). Correspondingly, Rev 22:15 should be understood dualistically as well.119

Therefore, I would now flag down my own simplistic comments in the above endnote from TOD in red. Thus I have used
a red font to question my own earlier placement in TOD of these texts in the soteriological category.
       Thankfully, I did begin to experiment with the Principle of Correlativity in TOD, which helped pave the way for
my being able to come to the dualistic position. Independently of all my complex calculations, however, Hollandsworth
was able to pose a completely satisfying misthological understanding of Rev 21:27. A pastor had questioned him at a
roundtable discussion at an earlier conference in 2014, following Hollandsworth speaking on kingdom themes. The pastor
wanted to know how Hollandsworth explained the “blotting out” of the LBOL. Unfortunately but honestly, at the time,
Hollandsworth had to admit that he did not have the answer, but he stated that he would make it a subject of study. It
plagued him throughout the year, as he meditated on the subject frequently. A year later, when Hollandsworth presented
his book to that same pastor at the annual conference, the pastor was delighted that Hollandsworth had come to these
conclusions, for they seemed to make sense to the pastor who had posed the question. Hollandsworth credits Crawford
and I with having been helpful in his coming to that conclusion.120

                                                          Cauley

       Principle of Correlativity
       By the same token, I need to credit Hollandsworth with helping advance and simplify the discussion.
Hollandsworth has made his own independent contribution to the discussion, not only by being able to express the matter
succinctly, but by cataloging Rev 21:27 exclusively as misthologically. This gives us an excellent competing model to
my soteric-mistholic model that I presented at the conference in the fall of 2014. Biblical interpretation is a science, and
good science appreciates good competing models. They help us explore the possibilities more thoroughly and thereby
gain new insights. In fact, this iron-sharpening-iron interaction lead me to tweak my own position. Whereas at the 2014
conference I was inclined to a soteric-mistholic interpretation of this text, after thinking through Hollandsworth’s
exclusively mistholic proposal, about a year later, I am now more inclined to migrate one step further toward his position
by adopting a mistholic-soteric option as my preference. In other words, the misthological interpretation might be primary
and the soteriological interpretation secondary. After all, I had defined the Principle of Correlative (POC) as flowing in
both directions in TOD:

               Principle of correlativity is a subset of the broader field of correspondence that refers to a specific
       type of intentional correlation in which a broad soteric-mistholic or mistholic-soteric typological relation
       is perceived to exist between two different persons, events, or states. A mistholic-mistholic correlation,
       therefore, such as that which exists between the out-resurrection and the outer darkness or between the
       priests that serve in the outer courts versus their typological counterparts, would be outside the scope of the
       type of correlation being described by the principle of correlativity.

       Five Options
        Accordingly, a full range of options for Rev 21:27 is pictured in Illustration 67. Olam and Alam. For years, I held
the first option, the soteric one. Thus TR advocates should not suppose that a soteriological interpretation necessarily
leads to the conclusion that rewards are temporary. Quite to the contrary, the ER position was maintained by Whipple,
Wilkin, and I, even while holding to a soteric interpretation of the LBOL in Rev 21:27. Option one is quite compatible
with ER. To the complete demise of TR, though, if options two, three, or four are taken, then TR is completely mistaken.
For if either of these latter three possibilities are correct, then Rev 21:27 is affirming that misthology continues in the
eternal state.
                                             Illustration 67. Olam and Alam

               Monolithic                              Dualistic                           Monolithic
                  1                             2                        3                    4
                    Soteric            Soteric-Mistholic        Mistholic-Soteric             Mistholic


characteristically in reference to (1) the premortem experience of the regenerate and (2) the premortem and postmortem
experience of the unregenerate. Alternatively, Rev 21:27 might be articular, depending on which Greek text is consulted
(so CT and HMT as opposed to BYZ). Personally, I prefer the articular rendition, though it makes no difference as to my
overall, dualistic interpretation.

Page 106                                                                                             Rewards are Eternal


        Since I already have provided defenses for both the first and second options elsewhere in my writings, I need only
say a word here in defense of option three. Hollandsworth should be consulted for option four. Incidentally, I bought a
copy of Hollandsworth’s book for my pastor, father, and both of my sons. As an introductory text, it is unsurpassed.
Hollandsworth has the ability to make the complex simple. I seem to have the opposite gift. Our roles complement and
complete each other.
        Even if the LBOL is a book of the redeemed, this does not prove that it is a book of all the redeemed. I disagreed
with Chitwood above when he tried to interpret redemption exclusively in misthological terms. Redemption certainly has
a soteriological foundation. Yet many would fall into opposite error by interpreting redemption exclusively in
soteriological terms and never rise above the foundation. Chitwood’s comments can be read profitably as a
counterbalance. Mediation is the solution. Redemption is dualistic, encompassing both soteric and mistholic themes. The
soteriological level is foundational for the more advanced misthological material. In 1Pet 1:17-19, Peter warns us to live
rightly as Christ’s blood-bought redeemed because we will be judged as such. Paul likewise expects our redemption to
have an impact on our performance in view of the blessed hope of misthological reward (Tit 2:13-14). “You have been
bought with a price [i.e., redeemed]: therefore, glorify God in your body” (1Cor 6:20). Most germane to our present study
is John’s Revelation: “And they sang a new song, saying: ‘You are worthy to take the scroll, and to open its seals; for
You were slain, and have redeemed us to God by Your blood out of every tribe and tongue and people and nation, and
have made us kings and priests to our God; and we shall reign on the earth” (Rev 5:9-10). This beautiful song of the
redeemed is a song the angels cannot sing, for they are not redeemed. Equally apparent, not all the redeemed can sing
this song because all the redeemed will not reign on the Earth. Rulership is limited to the faithful redeemed, that is, to
overcomers.
        If the LBOL in Rev 21:27 is a soteriological record of all the redeemed, then the verse is not saying that all the
redeemed will enter the city, only that only the redeemed will enter the city: “Nothing unclean and no one who practices
abomination and lying, shall ever come into it [the city], but only those whose names are written in the Lamb’s book of
life.” In this case, only ≠ all. This would be the case in option one.
        However, just as readily, and even more naturally in view of the context of ch. 21 and the background from
Revelation in general, only could mean that all those redeemed who are qualified for such entrance (and thus recorded in
the LBOL) will be allowed to enter. In this case, only = all. The LBOL would be an exclusively misthological record in
that case, just as Hollandsworth posed. Indeed, Revelation even opened with this statement: “To Him who loves us, and
released us from our sins by His blood, and He has made us to be kings, priests to His God and Father” (Rev 1:5-6;
TM). The redeemed are to rule, yet not all the redeemed will rule. The LBOL could easily be understood in the broad
context of Revelation and the specific context of ch. 21 to be the record of those among the redeemed who are qualified
to rule. If so, it would picture the misthological exclusion of unfaithful believers from the city. If so, the exclusion of
unbelievers from the city and kingdom would only be a potential, secondary consideration via the vice-list parallelism
with Rev 21:8, which is my correlative impression.
        What about angels? Will they be able to enter the city? Of course. Are their names in the LBOL? Of course not.
Then how can we pose that angels can enter the city if only limits entrance to those whose names are in the LBOL and
their names are not in the book? We can simply pose that angels are exceptions to the rule because, in the context, John
is obviously talking about humanity—only humanity is potentially qualified to rule. The realization of their qualification
is conditioned on their faithfulness. They must believe soteriologically and behave misthologically. This kingdom
rulership is not intended for those, such as angels, for whom the potential to rule is not applicable. John is not intending
to exclude angels from the city but only to exclude those among humanity to whom the vice lists are applicable: unfaithful
believers and unbelievers. If these human beings would but trust and obey, they would qualify to live happily in the city.
        How about those born in the eternal state? Will they be able to enter the city? Of course. Are their names in the
LBOL? Perhaps, perhaps not. A similar argument might be made that they are exceptions to the rule. They might be
exceptions to the rule because, in the context, John is obviously talking about faithful and believing humanity as opposed
to unfaithful or unbelieving humanity in relation to the vice lists and those among the redeemed who have the potential
to become kingdom rulers. The rule is not intended for those for whom the potential to rule is not contingently applicable.
John is not intending to exclude the nations born during the eternal state from visiting the city since vice is impossible
for them.121 The contextual intent is simply to exclude those among humanity for whom the vice lists are applicable, who
would have had the opportunity to rule if they had believed and then overcome. By his reference to the LBOL, John only
intends to exclude unfaithful believers and unbelievers from entrance into the city. The book is not applicable for those
for whom the LBOL does not have the potential to be applicable.

121
   Those born in the eternal state will have physical bodies but no sin nature. They will be incapable of sin, much less
vice.
Excursus on the Book of Life                                                                                            Page 107
        Then again, maybe the names of those born during the eternal state are recorded in the LBOL but listed at a lower,
non-ruling status. Just as they will have access to the leaves of the tree of life, but presumably not to the fruit of the tree
of life, perhaps they are recorded as having a right to enter the city but not the right to enter by the gates into the city
(Rev 22:14). In this case, John only intends to exclude unfaithful believers and unbelievers from entrance into the city,
and thus only intends to exclude their names from entry into the LBOL. The book would only be applicable secondarily
to those born in the eternal state since they only have a lower level of entry both into the city and in the book.
        In any case, misthological citizenship, and corresponding misthological entry or exclusion, is the focus of John’s
concern regarding the book in Rev 21:27. He laid the groundwork for this conclusion in his letter to the church at Sardis
in Rev 3:5. Unwittingly, Keathley gives the historical backdrop that leads to this very possibility:

              There is historical evidence that in the city of Sardis a person’s name was sometimes removed from
       the city register before death if he had been convicted of a crime. When these messages were written,
       Christians were under the constant threat of being branded as social rebels and stripped of their citizenship
       if they refused to recant or denounce their faith in Christ. In other words, they were branded as criminals.
       Thus, as a source of motivation and encouragement the Lord personally reminds the overcomer not only of
       the safety of his heavenly citizenship, but of the special acknowledgment the Lord Himself will give him
       before the Father and before His angels.122

        Given this historical context for the warning in Rev 3:5, in which the BOL was understood as pertaining to the
names of believers who are currently enrolled as citizens of the city, the most natural deduction is that John entertains
the possibility that the names of the believers within the church at Sardis could be erased misthopologically, not
soteriologically, from the BOL. Only the names of those believers who overcome would be left in the book as it pertains
exclusively to citizenship in the Heavenly City.
        Therefore, I am quite open to Hollandsworth’s exclusively misthological interpretation of the LBOL in Rev 21:7.
In fact, as I was composing this chart of four options regarding the range of possible plausible interpretations, a fifth
option occurred to me: to conclude that all four vantage points are valid ways to perceive the data. As it turns out, at the
time I was contemplating this possibility, my wife, Dianne, was diagnosed with stage 3 breast cancer. She found it
overwhelming to consider all the possible factors pertaining to the four primary possible treatments: lumpectomy, partial
mastectomy, full mastectomy, and bilateral mastectomy. All four options were viable ways of dealing with the data, the
data being the fact that she has cancer. In contrast, I did not find it overwhelming at all to contemplate the options. Indeed,
as I told both her and the doctor, mapping out all the possibilities being factored into consideration could be done with a
spreadsheet, just as I am accustomed to doing for interpreting complex passages such as this one. During this same time,
I was computing possible scenarios for the possibility of the rapture occurring in September.123 Each of these scenarios
is feasible given the current state of our knowledge. Only one will prove to be correct eventually, however. On the other
hand, all four ways of interpreting Rev 21:7 might be viable ways of viewing the data, just as all four surgery options are
viable options boiling down to personal preference. The point is not that truth is in the eye of the beholder but that the
biblical text intends for the data to be viewable from multiple vantage points. Each perspective is true in that it
corresponds to a legitimate way to view the data pertaining to the BOL and the LBOL.

       Spreadsheet
       In Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep (WWWS), I warmed up to the multiple book hypothesis. Names could be
erased misthologically from the form of the book in which the salvation of the soul was being determined, which is a
misthological theme. If that is the case, then the names left in the latter book would entail the permanent record after the
Bema. In WWWS and MMP2 and MMP4, I developed the hypothesis that the Bema Book (which I also referred to as
the Book for Life)124 is the record of those names that are permanently recorded as successfully passing the Bema, and

                        Physical         Soteriological             Misthological Life                 Misthological Life
       Name               Life                Life                    (Potentially)                       (Actually)

        This Book of Life Spreadsheet (BOLS) only has the names of believers within it (Rev 20:15). Their names have
been written in it from the foundation of the world (Rev 17:8), which suggests that in some sense their names are
permanently recorded within it. Nevertheless, in another sense their names may be erased (Rev 3:5). Building upon
Hollandsworth’s suggestion, then, this model would propose that the names of everyone who would ever believe in Christ
for eternal life have been recorded permanently in the BOLS from the foundation of the world. That would pose a model
regarding their names in the illustration, but what about check marks used in the illustration to represent logically
contingent subentries of their names? (1) In row one, Jane Smith has not yet been conceived, so she has no check mark
yet, except in the last column because at the Bema she will be found to be an overcomer. Her name as a believer has been
recorded soteriologically from the foundation of the world in the first column, and her check mark as an overcomer has
been recorded misthologically from the foundation of the world in the last column. These two columns are static. The
inner three columns are dynamic in that check marks entered here are only made as they become applicable. The middle
column (soteriological life) is dynamic-static in that once a check mark is entered in this column (at the inception of
regeneration), it cannot be erased. (2) In row two, John Smith has been born but has not yet believed, so at that point in
time he only has physical life. (3) In row three, John Doe has been born and believed. When he believed, he was marked
as having soteriological life and thus the potential to qualify for misthological life. In the context of Rev 3:5, concerning
having not having one’s reward status erased, the natural assumption is that Jesus is encouraging us to make a distinction
between potential and actual rewards when He warns: “Hold fast what you have, in order that no one take your crown”
(Rev 3:11). Living believers have the potential to earn the crown of life. At the point of death, they will no longer have
the potential for that reward. At that point, they either qualify to actualize that potential or they do not. In the chart, John
Doe is currently in the running to qualify for the crown of life and thus misthological life. (4) In row four, Jane Doe has
died and qualified to realize her potential. Her check mark is found to have been written misthologically in the Bema
Book from the foundation of the world. (5) In row five, Paul Smith has died but failed to qualify for the crown of life.
Therefore, his check mark is erased from the potential column. He only has soteriological life.126

the earthly Jerusalem and the Heavenly/New Jerusalem during the millennial kingdom and on into the eternal state).
       The OT presents this background for the victorious Jewish survivors: “And it will come about that he who is left
in Zion and remains in Jerusalem will be called holy—everyone who is recorded for [eis, LXX] life in Jerusalem” (Is
4:3). Eis can be translated appropriately as for (as well as to, into, resulting in, etc.). This Record for Life is a city registry
of those who will have citizenship in Jerusalem. I will call it a Book for Life. Some would call this the Book of Millennial
Life. To be sure, it is a promise of reward for Jewish believers who victoriously survive the tribulation and who therefore
are allowed to dwell in the earthly Jerusalem during the millennial kingdom. Be that as it may, in view of several
considerations, it will be considered the Book for Eternal Life in the present discussion. For one thing, Daniel supplies
the appellation eternal (aionios) to the quality of life that is bestowed upon those Jewish believers who emerge victorious
from the tribulation. So whereas both Daniel and Isaiah say that the result is eis (to, into, for) zoe (life), Daniel extends
the result into eternity. Eis life (Isaiah) = eis eternal life (Daniel). Thus, adding the Danielic appellation of eternal
provides this additional clarification.

      Hollandsworth’s simple three-part breakdown and scriptural delegation cited above is commendable.
Nevertheless, as explained in MMP2, I prefer a much more complex and thus complete approach:

       The BOL is used in four different ways in Scripture. (1) Book of earthly citizenship: it may refer to
       citizenship on earth (secular usage). (2) Book of earthly life: it may refer to life on Earth—(Ex 32:32-33;
       Ps 69:28a; 139:16). (3) Book of misthological life: it may refer to life in the Heavenly Jerusalem—(Ps
       69:28b; Is 4:3-4; cf. Rev 3:5b; 3:12; 21:27; 22:19?); Lk 10:20 (secondarily?); Phil 3:20 (primarily);
       Heb 12:23; Rev 3:5b (primarily), 13:8 (primarily?); 17:8 (secondarily?); 20:12,15 (secondarily?); 21:27
       (primarily).127 (4) Book of soteriological life: it may refer to life in heaven—Dan 12:1-2; Lk 10:20
       (dualistically); Phil 3:20 (dualistically); 4:3 (dualistically?); Rev 3:5b (dualistically); 13:8 (dualistically?);
       17:8 (dualistically?); 20:12-15 (dualistically?); 21:27 (dualistically).128

        For the sake of simplicity, I will not pursue the potential dualism in the present discussion to any depth. For present
purposes, a text generally will be treated as simply falling into one category or the other. Nevertheless, a word must be
said about Phil 3:20 since I flagged it down in red above when Hollandsworth cited it as a soteriological text. Much more
likely, the verse is primarily misthological. If cited simplistically, rather than dualistically, then it should be listed with
texts describing the salvation of the soul rather than the salvation of the spirit.
